Pro Private Markets
Bain Capital Ventures's Ajay Agarwal Discusses Software Industry; Venture capitalists reflects on 2016 and year ahead

229 words
29 December 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

AJAY AGARWAL, managing director, Bain Capital Ventures

What surprised you the most in 2016?

The resiliency of the [software-as-a-service] market. After some of the top names like LinkedIn [a Bain Capital Ventures investment] and Tableau saw their market caps cut in half in February, the best companies all roared back, followed up by some very strong back half of 2016 IPOs such as Twilio.

What do you think will be the biggest challenge your corner of the industry will face in 2017?

Way too many software companies got funded in 2013 to 2015, given easy access to capital and the lower time and cost required to start a company. As a result, every space is crowded with thousands of startups: [human resource] software, sales software, security software. A wave of consolidation needs to occur as the strongest players in each space build out true platforms and suites.

Will business-to-business investments yield solid returns despite the cloud investment frenzy and overheated valuations?

Yes we are still at low penetration percentages for cloud software overall and especially in specific verticals. Big segments of the economy such as logistics, supply chain and transportation have yet to fully leverage the power of cloud and machine learning. We are just getting started!

Pro Private Markets
IPO Outlook Might Not Be All Rainbows and Unicorns; Investors will be watching how things go for Spotify and Dropbox

By Scott Martin and Patience Haggin 
1,509 words
29 December 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Here's the billion-dollar question: Will Wall Street discount Sand Hill's once-hot startups in initial public offerings?

For investors in billion-dollar "unicorn" startups companies facing cash burns and liquidity demands from all directions the question looms as pressure for some to go public runs high.

Spotify AB raised convertible debt in March, agreeing to terms that set the clock ticking on an IPO in return for the $1 billion in financing that enabled the $8.5 billion-valued company to take money without a valuation reset.

"It's not only huge, but it had some terms that they had to go public," said Matt Murphy, a managing director at Menlo Ventures.

About 151 companies are valued at a billion dollars or more, according to The Wall Street Journal's Billion Dollar Startup Club, and many that raised late-stage funding in a frothy investing environment over the past several years now face cash concerns and potential lowered valuations.

Investors will be closely watching Spotify and other highly valued startups to move forward with IPOs for whether their share pricings come in below their last private financing prices. Many face this splash of cold water as a hangover sets in following recent venture investor euphoria.

"There's a disconnect between the private venture capital-type markets and the public markets," said Martin Wellington, a managing partner at law firm Sidley Austin LLP in Palo Alto, Calif.

Dropbox Inc., the 10-year old cloud storage startup, is another company with a heady valuation, raising $500 million in debt financing in 2014. Dropbox was valued at $10 billion in its last funding round, but publicly traded private-equity investor BlackRock Inc. has since written it down 45%.

Amid renewed market skepticism, flat and down private financing rounds are more in vogue these days, reflecting the desire by venture investors to come to grips with valuation compression.

Still, the 2017 IPO market is expected to see offerings from highly valued startups including Snap Inc., Blue Apron Inc. and Bloom Energy Corp. Many more likely have filed confidentially with the Securities and Exchange Commission under a provision of the Jumpstart Our Business Startups Act and remain under the radar.

But the IPO market remains a concern. The 37 U.S. venture-backed IPOs through Dec. 14 put 2016 on pace for the lowest level of public offerings since the end of the financial crisis in 2009, according to data from VentureSource, a unit of Private Equity Analyst publisher Dow Jones & Co.

Many investors remain unworried about how heated valuations play out in public markets, saying that those who invested earlier made money. But the late-stage investors who helped fuel the valuations frenzy for startups in 2014 and 2015 are the ones positioned for less stellar gains.

"If the unicorn valuation is disconnected from what the market valuation will be and it's too high, then somebody is gonna get hurt," said Mr. Wellington.

Mutual funds and other institutions who bought in at rich valuations might see their shares underwater on the public markets, triggering ratchet protections to be made whole if written into the investing terms. Venture investors say the institutional set of investors has become more selective on backing companies recently in the wake of valuation concerns.

That factor already has been borne out on investments in startups. The median premoney valuation for late-stage rounds fell to $148.6 million in the first half of 2016 from $269.3 million in 2015, according to VentureSource. The median premoney valuation for Series D or higher rounds dropped to less than $200 million in the second quarter of 2016 from a peak of about $400 million in the third quarter of 2015, according to law firm Cooley LLP.

Megavaluation startups have fallen out of favor in IPO markets. The U.S. median postmoney valuation of startups at IPO plummeted to $139.6 million in 2016 as of Dec. 14, 69.3% lower than the 10-year high of $455.2 million in 2011, according to VentureSource.

Indeed, venture-capital investors have throttled back on late-stage funding. In the third quarter of last year, the median size of later-stage rounds declined to $8.45 million, the lowest since the third quarter of 2010, according to VentureSource. In the same period in 2015, the median amount invested was pushed up to $18 million in the clamor to give cash to companies with valuations of $1 billion or more.

Crossover investors aren't the only ones chasing unicorns. Venture-capital firm Sequoia Capital, whose megahits include the likes of Airbnb Inc. and Dropbox, holds the title for the largest stable of unicorns, according to data from the Journal's Billion Dollar Startup Club. Sequoia is trailed by the likes of Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers, Accel Partners and Andreessen Horowitz in billion-dollar startup stakes.

To be sure, many investors see the megavaluations of startups such as Uber Technologies Inc., Snap and Airbnb and plenty of others as the next big ticket. Some of the late-stage investors who picked up golden lottery tickets in these companies could expect to see rewards at the end of the rainbow.

The question is whether 2017 will be more receptive for tech IPOs. A handful of IPOs went out in recent months that have done well, offering hopes for the return of the public markets. Shares of Crispr Therapeutics AG, Coupa Software Inc. and Nutanix Inc. have stayed aloft in post-IPO trading, up 39.4%, 46.6% and 79.6%, respectively, as of Dec. 16, according to data from the WSJ Pro Venture-Backed Post-IPO Index.

"Hopefully, your private valuation is not completely out of line with how the public markets might view your company," said Eric Paley, a managing partner at Founder Collective.

Private markets are showing some signs of IPO-related interest in shares, too. On the secondary market for private shares, demand has soared for companies thought to be planning an IPO. "The companies that are being discussed as potential near-term IPO companies are seeing increased interest on our platform," said Greg Brogger, chief executive of private securities broker SharesPost Inc.

But even when unicorns see a strong public pop, gains don't always hold. The 21 U.S. unicorns that went public between 2011 and 2015 saw their shares close an average of 45% above the offer price on the first day of trading. Six months after the IPO, their shares traded only 33% above the offer price on average, according to data provider Dealogic Ltd.

"The question is what happens when a private investor wants to get out," said Kathleen Smith, co-founder of IPO researcher Renaissance Capital.

Venture firms, often mandated to get liquidity soon after a company goes public, hope those gains will hold through the six-month lock-up period after an IPO or at least long enough for them to cash out in an authorized secondary public offering before the lock-up ends. Some recent highly valued companies have seen a big drop when they announce that venture-capital firms plan to sell.

When Twilio Inc. hit the market in May as the first large tech IPO in months, its stock surged in part because it attracted interest from retail investors. In October, the company announced plans to let private investors sell shares in a secondary offering, and its share price fell 12% in one day, then continued to slide. Four weeks later, the stock traded 45% below its price before it announced the secondary offering.

Who can make it to an IPO is the question. IPO veterans expect only those unicorns with the strongest business fundamentals will take chances in the first few months of 2017.

"It's much harder to market a growth-at-any-cost kind of deal [since 2016] than it would have been in 2015 or 2014," said Mike Tuchen, chief executive of Talend Inc., a big-data integration company that went public in July with positive cash flow.

Two unicorns known to be waiting in the wings Snap and Blue Apron are expected to have numbers in line for Wall Street. Snap told investors last year it expected revenue between $250 million and $350 million in 2016. Blue Apron says it ships 8 million meal kits per month. At $10 each, that's nearly $1 billion in annual revenue.

Some unicorns are dropping IPO hints, saying their business is sound. In June, Dropbox Chief Executive Drew Houston announced the company was cash-flow positive. With that news, Dropbox signaled to the markets that its business was working, said Menlo Ventures' Mr. Murphy, who is not an investor in the company.

"There's going to be more pressure for them to go public. The question is can they get comfortable with going out with a down value?" he said.

Pro Private Markets
Roundup of Form D Regulatory Filings--Dec. 27

By Zachary Cole 
409 words
28 December 2016
02:44
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

(The following is a roundup of selected companies that have revealed securities sales in recent Form D regulatory filings. These companies are required to file a Form D notification within 15 days after the first sale of securities in an offering, which could indicate fundraising, acquisitions or other securities activity. The filings don't list the recipients of the securities being offered. However, they do list board members, so in cases where new investors have surfaced, we'll report them below.)

CallMiner Inc., Waltham, Mass., an enterprise speech analytics company that extracts information from call center conversations, has sold $3 million toward a $4.2 million equity round. In July 2015, the company received $12 million in growth funding from NewSpring Growth Capital. Inflexion Partners, Intersouth Partners and Sigma Partners previously invested in CallMiner.

MetaCommunications Inc., Iowa City, Iowa, which makes software for marketing and creative teams, has issued $9.9 million of a $15 million round of equity, debt and rights. In September 2015, the company closed an $8 million growth funding round led by Next Level Ventures.

Minerva Surgical Inc., Redwood City, Calif., a medical-device company focused on women's health care, has sold $16.7 million of a $26.7 million equity round. A January filing showed the company selling $15 million in equity. New Enterprise Associates lists the company in its portfolio.

Neurala Inc., Boston, a developer of software for robots, has sold all but $300,000 of a $14 million equity round. In October 2015, the company secured a $750,000 seed round led by Tim Draper, Draper Associates and Robolution Capital. Orkos Capital and Techstars list Neurala in their portfolios.

Opal Labs Inc., Portland, Ore., creator of collaborative planning software for brand marketing teams, has sold $12 million of a $16 million equity round. In 2014, the company secured an $8 million Series A round led by Madrona Venture Group.

Therabron Therapeutics Inc., Rockville, Md., a developer of treatments for respiratory diseases, has issued $2.3 million of a $6 million offering of debt, rights and securities. Brace Pharma Capital led the company's Series B round last year.

Triblio Inc., Reston, Va., which provides content-marketing software, has sold $2.5 million of a $3 million equity round. In 2014, the company raised a $3.4 million seed round led by Kepha Partners and Longworth Venture Partners.

Pro Private Markets
General Motors to Mentor Companies Through 500 Startups; It is the first corporate partner track for the seed program

141 words
28 December 2016
02:11
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

General Motors Co. is working with 500 Startups on a seed program for young companies working in areas of interest to the auto maker.

The arrangement is the first such corporate partner track for 500 Startups. About five companies will be chosen for the program and will receive mentorship and the possibility of follow-on funding from GM.

The program will operate for 500 Startups' current batch of companies and will continue for the next group in April 2017.

The specialized GM track will be run by Robert Neivert, Lupin Campos, Aaron Blumenthal and several members of GM's Advanced Technology Silicon Valley Office.

Areas of interest for the program include mobility services, machine learning, virtual reality, robotics and additive manufacturing.

More Relief for Consumers --- Penalties for two big European banks carve out a bigger portion for loan modifications

By AnnaMaria Andriotis, Aruna Viswanatha and Gabriel T. Rubin 
617 words
24 December 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
B2
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Billions of dollars of relief may be on the way for borrowers who got mortgages before the housing bust.

Deutsche Bank AG and Credit Suisse Group AG agreed to pay consumers $4.1 billion and about $2.8 billion, respectively, as part of settlements they struck with the federal government this past week over their handling of mortgage-backed securities before the housing meltdown.

The two settlements, totaling $12.5 billion, are the latest penalties in a long-running series between the Justice Department and large banks over their role in deceptively selling billions of dollars of subprime mortgages as safe securities, exacerbating losses for investors nearly a decade ago. The settlements are notable in part because consumer relief accounts for a greater share of the total fines compared with many previous agreements, and consumer advocacy and other nonprofit groups aren't expected to get a piece of the funds.

The relief funds will go toward loan modifications, similar to the framework in the settlement reached between Goldman Sachs and the Justice Department in April, according to people familiar with the negotiations. Loan modifications in that accord included forgiveness of certain unpaid mortgage balances and forbearance to struggling borrowers, including those who owed more on their mortgage than their home is worth.

Payments to nonprofits and community groups -- a staple in many of the U.S. government's early mortgage settlements and a sticking point for many Republicans -- aren't expected to be included in these settlements, according to the people.

Justice Department settlements over toxic mortgage securities with Bank of America Corp. J.P. Morgan Chase & Co. and Citigroup Inc., among others, included provisions that require the banks to turn over unpaid consumer-relief funds to consumer-advocacy nonprofits that focus on affordable housing, legal aid help to low-income borrowers and other groups. That provision was excluded from the Justice Department's settlement announcement with Goldman Sachs.

The exclusions could mark a turning point for how the consumer-relief portion of mortgage settlements are structured.

The settlements could also be a hot spot for the incoming administration. In September, the House voted almost entirely along party lines to restrict payments to third-party groups as part of Justice Department settlements, saying the arrangement subverts Congress's spending power and gives money to left-leaning activist groups with little oversight.

The Obama administration threatened to veto the bill, saying it "seeks to address a problem that does not exist," and the Senate didn't take it up. Republicans plan to move forward with new legislation on the issue early next year, a House Judiciary Committee aide said.

Consumer payouts have been a signature piece of most of the crisis-era mortgage settlements. The terms of these deals and how successful they have been at actually getting money to borrowers, however, have come under fire from some consumer advocates and Republicans.

Billions of dollars have been allocated to help borrowers in Justice Department settlements over toxic mortgage securities. Goldman Sachs agreed to pay out $1.8 billion to consumers earlier this year, Bank of America agreed to $7 billion in 2014 and Citigroup agreed to $2.5 billion the same year. J.P. Morgan agreed to pay out $4 billion in 2013.

"Everybody is focused on the big number but where the money actually lands and how it lands and if it is really hard money or not is something very different," said Basil Petrou, managing partner at Federal Financial Analytics.

European Bank Drama: Justice Settles with Deutsche Bank and Credit Suisse , Sues Barclays ; New Italy Bailouts | Why Icahn Likes the Volcker Rule | SEC  Attendance Fraud' | Ackerman's Take: Trumpian Clash Between Activists, Big Business; The Wall Street Journal's financial regulation newsletter for Friday, December 23, 2016. 

9,654 words
23 December 2016
13:11
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Ackerman's Take: A Trumpian Clash Between Activists and Big Business

Deutsche Bank , Credit Suisse Reach Multibillion Settlements Over Toxic Securities

U.S. Sues Barclays Over Mortgage Securities in Rare Step

Italy Sets Up Fund to Help Troubled Banks

The Volcker Rule and Other Regulations Carl Icahn Likes

A Trumpian Clash Between Activists and Big Business

A common assumption about Donald Trump is that he'll run one of the most pro-business administrations in recent memory. But what if, instead, he hews to more of a pro-investor agenda that clashes with big business?

That is certainly an increasingly likely outcome after Wednesday's announcement that the president-elect has tapped billionaire investor Carl Icahn for an unpaid advisory role on overhauling financial regulations. Mr. Icahn, who has already played a part in selecting Mr. Trump's head of the Environmental Protection Agency , is also vetting candidates to lead the Securities and Exchange Commission , The Wall Street Journal reported.

The appointment suggests the president-elect will select policy makers more in the mold of Mr. Icahn, who has spent the past four decades battling big companies as an activist investor, than those favored by the pro-business wing of the Republican Party. While it's true that Mr. Icahn generally supports rolling back regulations that he believes crimp the U.S. economy, he is also an outspoken advocate of corporate-governance changes loathed by the business community.

Among these changes, the most controversial involves so-called universal ballots, proposed by the SEC in October. These would significantly ease how investors choose between board candidates offered by activist investors and those offered by a company's management. The U.S. Chamber of Commerce says the measure would turn director elections into political-style campaigns and further discourage companies from going public.

Though the measure was widely panned by Republicans and the business lobby, Mr. Icahn applauded the move at the time, releasing a statement saying universal ballots would "eliminate needless voter confusion in contested elections, give shareholders greater freedom of choice, and hopefully end some of the gamesmanship employed by incumbent boards to keep shareholder-nominated directors out of the boardroom."

He also criticized the Chamber, saying he hoped it would fail in its effort to defeat the initiative. In 2011, the business group successfully challenged a related proxy measure in court.

(The dig at the U.S. Chamber wasn't totally out of character from a Trump confidant. Mr. Trump himself derided the Chamber as "totally controlled by the special interest groups" this summer, a day after the federation criticized his trade proposals.)

Mr. Icahn backs other initiatives that would likely meet resistance from the business community, such as steps to reduce corporate stock buybacks. He says such buybacks are driven by short-term thinking that weakens corporate balance sheets. "You could incentivize a company to invest in their own businesses rather than buy back stock," he said Thursday on CNBC. The billionaire also has warned about risky high-yield bond investments, suggesting he may support tougher restrictions on the funds' sales to mom-and-pop retail investors.

If Mr. Icahn's views prevail in shaping the next administration's approach to Wall Street, gear up for a big clash with big business.

Note to Readers: WSJ Pro Financial Regulation won't publish a newsletter on Monday, Dec. 26 (Christmas Day observed). Newsletter publication will resume Tuesday, Dec. 27.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Deutsche Bank , Credit Suisse Reach Multibillion Settlements Over Toxic Securities

By Jenny Strasburg and Max Colchester in London and Devlin Barrett in Washington

The Obama administration scrambled to resolve its remaining crisis-era megabank mortgage cases, striking multibillion-dollar settlements with Deutsche Bank AG and Credit Suisse Group AG over toxic securities while separately filing a lawsuit against Barclays PLC alleging more than $30 billion in fraud-tainted sales.

U.S. authorities settled with Deutsche Bank for $7.2 billion Thursday. Early Friday, Credit Suisse said it had agreed to pay about $5.3 billion to close the matter.

Deutsche Bank and Credit Suisse shares rose 4% and 2%, respectively, on Friday morning. Shares in Barclays dropped less than 1% as investors digested the potential uncertainty of a protracted legal battle with the Justice Department.

The dramatic back-to-back announcements show the urgency among senior Obama appointees in the Justice Department to resolve the outstanding probes of precrisis conduct at major banks before those officials leave office in mid-January. Part of the rush stems from a great deal of uncertainty about how a Trump administration might pursue, settle or drop the remaining probes, according to people familiar with the discussions.

The Deutsche Bank settlement is likely to bring some relief for the German bank's shareholders, who earlier in the year worried about a much bigger penalty. The Justice Department had originally sought $14 billion from Deutsche Bank , The Wall Street Journal reported in September, raising concerns about whether the institution would be able to negotiate that down.

Less than half the settlement requires a cash payment that would have an immediate impact on Deutsche Bank 's bottom line. The settlement was divided into a $3.1 billion penalty and a pledge to pay $4.1 billion over time to a "consumer relief" fund to be distributed by the government.

The terms of that relief including loan modifications to help consumers still must be finalized between the bank and the government.

Deutsche Bank announced the pact Thursday evening in the U.S., saying it had reached an agreement "in principle" with the Justice Department.

A Justice Department spokesman declined to comment. In major corporate settlements, particularly when the deal isn't yet finalized, it isn't uncommon for firms to announce the framework of a deal before the government, often in the hopes of a garnering a positive reaction from shareholders.

Credit Suisse said it would take a $2 billion charge to cover its settlement. The Swiss bank's deal includes a penalty from the Justice Department of $2.48 billion, and consumer-relief payments of about $2.8 billion, to be paid over five years. Again, the deal remains subject to "final documentation."

The settlements follow similar multibillion-dollar agreements reached over the past three years with other big banks, like J.P. Morgan Chase & Co ., Citigroup Inc . and Goldman Sachs Group . Inc.

The decision to sue Barclays is more unusual but not unheard of. The Justice Department filed two lawsuits against Bank of America Corp . related to precrisis sales of mortgage-backed securities in 2012 and 2013. One of those was thrown out earlier this year on appeal. The damages sought against Barclays weren't quantified.

Barclays said in a statement that it would seek the suit's "dismissal at the earliest opportunity," and that it considers the claims "disconnected from the facts." Bank officials declined to elaborate on why the talks broke down, but one person familiar with the matter said that continuing the talks under "a fresh pair of eyes" in a new presidential administration might be more beneficial.

A prolonged legal battle would be handled by Justice Department officials appointed by President-elect Donald Trump . Neither Mr. Trump nor his aides have indicated how they might handle legacy financial-crisis fallout inherited from President Barack Obama. But Mr. Trump in 2013 criticized J.P. Morgan CEO James Dimon that year as "the worst banker in the United States" for reaching a $13 billion settlement in a similar case. "What happened to the days when you actually go to trial?" Mr. Trump said at the time.

The Deutsche Bank settlement and Barclays suit come as the Justice Department still has probes outstanding with two other big European banks  Royal Bank of Scotland Group PLC and UBS Group AG .

The Justice negotiations this year have been a big cloud hanging over an already-fragile European banking industry, weighing on several major lenders' stock prices. Investors have worried about whether those institutions' capital is sufficient to cover any possible U.S. fines.

The Deutsche Bank settlement came together quickly in the latter part of this week following intense negotiations between lawyers representing the bank and the government, a person familiar with the matter said. Going into the week, the two sides still had significant differences to overcome, people close to the discussions said.

Deutsche Bank and the government were steadily closing in on terms, including the government's statement of facts about alleged wrongdoing, each day this week, according to a person close to the talks. The final main issue boiled down to money, the person said.

A Deutsche Bank settlement was appearing more promising earlier Thursday even without the Barclays lawsuit, but the government's move to sue the U.K. bank added incentive to close a deal, the person said.

The German bank said it will take a roughly $1.17 billion pretax charge this quarter as a result of the $3.1 billion penalty. The additional consumer relief has less immediate and less solid consequences, because it is to be paid out over at least five years, the bank said, adding that the settlement isn't expected to have a material impact on its 2016 financial results.

Hours before the Deutsche Bank settlement was announced, the Barclays talks broke down as executives at the U.K.'s second-largest bank made clear they were wary of being pressured to make a big payout.

Barclays Chief Executive Jes Staley had told people in recent months that the bank wouldn't give U.S. authorities a blank check, and wouldn't settle if he felt the price was too high, according to people familiar with the discussions.

Government officials had discussed with Barclays lawyers the possibility of suing, but the bank only learned Thursday that a lawsuit was arriving the same day, according to a person familiar with the matter.

The impasse between the two sides was still big: They remained multiple billions of dollars apart, with Barclays unwilling to pay $5 billion an amount the government had said was in the range of possibilities, according to a person familiar with the matter.

The 198-page lawsuit by the U.S. government says the bank "engaged in a fraudulent scheme to sell tens of billions of dollars of residential mortgage-backed securities, in which it repeatedly deceived investors about the characteristics of the loans backing those trusts."

Barclays sold the securities to a wide range of investors, including financial institutions, Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac , federal home-loan banks, credit unions, pension plans, charitable and religious organizations, and university endowments.

"Many of these investors suffered devastating losses,'' according to the suit, which was filed in a U.S. District Court in New York. The suit singles out two former New York-based Barclays executives, Paul Menefee and John Carroll, who had been hired in 2003 to help run the bank's asset-securitization team, according to an announcement at the time. Both were previously at Morgan Stanley and left Barclays in 2008.

The complaint is filled with expletive-laced conversations involving those men and others familiar with the loans, taken from transcripts of recorded phone calls and emails. In one, the complaint says that Mr. Menefee, who was in charge of due diligence on the subprime deals at issue, allegedly said that one loan pool "scares the sh*t out of me.'' At another point, that same executive said about a Wells Fargo & Co . pool, according to the complaint: "We have to eat their sh*t loans."

Mr. Menefee's lawyer called the complaint "a misguided attempt'' to blame his client and others for losses incurred by sophisticated institutional investors after an industrywide housing market collapse.

Mr. Carroll's lawyer called the decision to sue "extremely disappointing" and said his client "expects to be fully vindicated."

The suit also alleges that companies hired to conduct due-diligence checks on the mortgages warned the bank, and those warnings were essentially ignored. "These vendors described some of these securitized loans as  craptacular,' others as  scariest collateral,' and others as having the  distinct aroma of default,'" the suit says.

The allegations relate to activities and employees at Barclays , before the bank's 2008 buy of the collapsed investment bank Lehman Brothers .

During Barclays 's settlement negotiations, one of the key arguments the bank made to Justice Department lawyers was that Barclays was the most-exposed investor in the securities in question, and had already taken a big write-down during the financial crisis as a result of losses.

Barclays also insisted that losses from the securities were related to broad market events, not its own practices, and argued that its due diligence on the deals was deemed at or above industry standards, including by government-backed entities that approved of Barclays 's methodology at the time.

In recent days, government lawyers were scheduling calls with Barclays lawyers, pushing for progress in the talks, a person familiar with the matter said.

On Thursday, the government gave the bank a brief heads-up before filing the complaint about 3 p.m. ET.

Lawyers for the bank could respond to the lawsuit as early as next month, people close the matter said.

 Aruna Viswanatha and John Letzing contributed to this article.

U.S. Sues Barclays Over Mortgage Securities in Rare Step

By Max Colchester, Devlin Barrett and Jenny Strasburg

The U.S. Justice Department took the unusual step of suing Barclays PLC , alleging the bank fraudulently sold more than $30 billion of mortgage securities that helped fuel the financial crisis, after long-running settlement negotiations broke down.

The complaint filed Thursday against the U.K.'s second-largest bank shows the urgency among senior Obama appointees in the Justice Department to resolve the outstanding probes of precrisis conduct at major banks before those officials leave office in mid-January. Part of the urgency stems from a great deal of uncertainty about how a Trump administration might pursue, settle or drop the remaining probes, according to people familiar with the discussions.

Many of the banks caught up in the multibillion-dollar inquiries have reached settlements over the past three years without going to court. The Justice Department did file two lawsuits against Bank of America Corp . related to precrisis sales of mortgage-backed securities in 2012 and 2013, one of which was thrown out earlier this year on appeal.

The government complaint didn't quantify the damages it is seeking from Barclays .

Barclays said in a statement that it would seek the suit's "dismissal at the earliest opportunity," and that it considers the claims "disconnected from the facts." Bank officials declined to elaborate on why the talks broke down, but one person familiar with the matter said that continuing the talks under "a fresh pair of eyes" in a new presidential administration might be more beneficial.

A prolonged legal battle would be handled by Justice Department officials appointed by President-elect Donald Trump . Neither Mr. Trump nor his aides have indicated how they might handle legacy financial-crisis fallout inherited from Mr. Obama. But Mr. Trump in 2013 criticized J.P. Morgan CEO James Dimon that year as "the worst banker in the United States" for reaching a $13 billion settlement in a similar case. "What happened to the days when you actually go to trial?" Mr. Trump said at the time.

The Barclays suit comes as the Justice Department is deep in negotiations over mortgage securities with two other big European banks  Deutsche Bank AG and Credit Suisse Group AG  it aims to wrap up by next month. While those talks are progressing, they have yet to reach a point that suggests a settlement is certain, according to people familiar with the discussions.

The Justice negotiations this year have been a big cloud hanging over an already-fragile European banking industry, weighing on several major lenders' stock prices. Investors have worried about whether those institutions' capital is sufficient to cover any possible U.S. fines. As of this summer, Barclays had set aside about $3.1 billion to cover fines and litigation, and has been selling assets in Europe and Africa to bolster its balance sheet.

The 198-page lawsuit by the U.S. government says the bank "engaged in a fraudulent scheme to sell tens of billions of dollars of residential mortgage-backed securities (RMBS), in which it repeatedly deceived investors about the characteristics of the loans backing those trusts."

Barclays sold the securities to a wide range of investors, including financial institutions, Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac , federal home-loan banks, credit unions, pension plans, charitable and religious organizations, and university endowments.

"Many of these investors suffered devastating losses,'' according to the suit, which was filed in a U.S. District Court in New York. The suit singles out two former New York-based Barclays executives, Paul Menefee and John Carroll, who had been hired in 2003 to help run the bank's asset-securitization team, according to an announcement at the time. Both were previously at Morgan Stanley and left Barclays in 2008.

The complaint is filled with expletive-laced conversations involving those men and others familiar with the loans, taken from transcripts of recorded phone calls and emails. In one, the complaint says that Mr. Menefee, who was charge of due diligence on the subprime deals at issue, allegedly said that one loan pool "scares the sh*t out of me.'' At another point, that same executive said about a Wells Fargo & Co . pool, according to the complaint: "We have to eat their sh*t loans."

Mr. Menefee's lawyer called the complaint "a misguided attempt'' to blame his client and others for losses incurred by sophisticated institutional investors after an industrywide housing market collapse.

Mr. Menefee, the lawyer said, plans to refute the accusations in court "where it will be shown why the government's own witness described Mr. Menefee as  honest',  careful', and  the fairest person he ever dealt with.'''

Mr. Carroll's lawyer didn't immediately respond to requests for comment.

The suit also alleges that companies hired to conduct due-diligence checks on the mortgages warned the bank, and those warnings were essentially ignored. "These vendors described some of these securitized loans as  craptacular,' others as  scariest collateral,' and others as having the  distinct aroma of default,'" the suit says.

The allegations relate to activities and employees at Barclays , before the bank's 2008 buy of the collapsed investment bank Lehman Brothers .

The breakdown in the talks between the government and the bank came as Barclays executives made clear they were wary of being pressured to make a big payout.

Barclays Chief Executive Jes Staley had told people in recent months that the bank wouldn't give U.S. authorities a blank check, and wouldn't settle if he felt the price was too high, according to people familiar with the discussions.

Government officials had discussed with Barclays lawyers numerous times the possibility of suing, but the bank only learned Thursday that a lawsuit was arriving the same day, according to a person familiar with the matter.

The impasse between the two sides was still big: They remained multiple billions of dollars apart, with Barclays unwilling to pay $5 billion an amount the government had said was in the range of possibilities, according to a person familiar with the matter.

Lawyers for the bank could respond to the lawsuit as early as next month, people close the matter said.

One of the key arguments Barclays made to Justice Department lawyers during the negotiations was that Barclays was the most-exposed investor in the securities in question, and had already taken a big write-down during the financial crisis as a result of losses.

Barclays also insisted that losses from the securities were related to broad market events, not its own practices, and argued that its due diligence on the deals was deemed at or above industry standards, including by government-backed entities that approved of Barclays 's methodology at the time.

In recent days, government lawyers were scheduling calls with Barclays lawyers, pushing for progress in the talks, a person familiar with the matter said.

On Thursday, the government gave the bank a brief heads-up before filing the complaint about 3 p.m. ET.

Italy Sets Up Fund to Help Troubled Banks

By Giovanni Legorano

Italy's government has set up a backstop fund to shore up troubled banks, setting the stage for the rescue of troubled Italian lender Banca Monte dei Paschi di Siena SpA .

At a cabinet meeting in the early hours of Friday, the government of Prime Minister Paolo Gentiloni approved the creation of a  ¬20 billion ($20.9 billion) fund to help troubled banks. Shortly afterward, Monte dei Paschi said it will apply to tap this fund for fresh equity to shore up its balance sheet.

Following the move, the Italian market regulator suspended trading in Monte dei Paschi's ordinary shares, related derivatives and 10 types of bonds for the entirety of Friday's session.

The government's decision came just hours after the Tuscan bank declared that a last-ditch effort to raise capital from private investors had failed. The bank, Italy's No. 3 lender, attempted to raise  ¬5 billion to stay afloat and avert a government bailout.

The government said that if a bank taps the newly created fund, its outstanding junior bonds will be forcibly converted into shares. European rules on bank rescues require that investors suffer at least some losses.

However, under the terms attached to the Italian government's new fund, the losses suffered by holders of Monte dei Paschi's junior bonds appear to be fairly limited.

The government said that for the Tuscan bank, some junior bonds held mainly by institutional investors will take a 25% haircut on their face value when converted into shares when the bank taps the fund.

In the case of those held by retail investors, however, their bond holdings will also be forcibly converted into shares, but at their full value. MPS will swap the shares obtained by retail investors from the forced conversion of their junior bonds with senior bonds and then the government will buy those shares from the bank.

A Treasury official said the government believes the mechanism to rescue Monte dei Paschi which other troubled banks can also tap abides by European rules, even though the government hasn't received a specific green light by the European Commission .

The Volcker Rule and Other Regulations Carl Icahn Likes

By Ryan Tracy

Carl Icahn , President-elect Donald Trump 's new regulatory adviser, might not favor financial deregulation the way many on Wall Street expect.

The billionaire investor, tapped for a new post called "special adviser to the president on regulatory reform," has said positive things about the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial-overhaul law and one of its signature initiatives, the Volcker rule bank-trading ban. On the other hand, he has raised concerns about risks in the asset-management industry and accountability on corporate boards.

"I'm not against regulations at all," Mr. Icahn said Thursday on CNBC. "I sort of believe that you need a rule of law."

In 2015, he praised the Volcker rule for getting banks out of the proprietary trading business, even though other financial-market participants say that banks' decreased trading has hurt market functioning.

A representative of Mr. Icahn didn't respond to a request for comment.

Mr. Icahn has already been involved in personnel decisions, such as helping choose the next head of the Securities and Exchange Commission , according to people familiar with the matter.

To be sure, Mr. Icahn is striking a deregulatory tone, suggesting in particular that bank regulations and environmental regulations need to be rolled back. He called the regulatory policies of the Obama administration "literally absurd" in the CNBC interview.

"There is too much regulation on the banks," he said without offering specifics. He even suggested that he would give bankers a role in rewriting regulations, adding that "the great experts" on bank rules include the chief executives of Bank of America Corp . and J.P. Morgan Chase & Co ., two of the largest U.S. banks.

But past comments suggest he won't be advocating the kind of wholesale regulatory rollback that many Republicans and business executives or even Mr. Trump himself would want to see.

"In fact, I think you need Dodd-Frank, to some extent, more than even Donald does," Mr. Icahn said in an interview with Fox News last month.

Mr. Trump's nominee for Treasury secretary, the investor and former Goldman Sachs Group Inc. banker Steven Mnuchin , has criticized the Volcker rule, a Dodd-Frank provision that bans banks from trading with their own funds.

Mr. Icahn, at a conference in 2015 where he publicly debated BlackRock Inc. Chief Executive Laurence Fink , said he thought the Volcker rule "is very important and it is good."

"Banks should not be a last resort to sell" securities, and should not have their proprietary trading desks buying them, Mr. Icahn said at the time.

He went on to say that he thought the rule had diminished banks' role in securities markets, which could mean there are fewer buyers and sellers in the market during a crisis. But rather than pointing the finger at regulations for this perceived market fragility, he blamed large asset managers like BlackRock for playing an outsize role in markets and creating risky investment funds that would have trouble meeting investor redemptions in a crisis.

Mr. Icahn's perspective is colored by his many years as an activist investor. He has supported a recent SEC "universal ballot" proposal that would make it easier for shareholders to vote on board candidates nominated by investors a rule that many Republicans and big businesses oppose.

In the CNBC appearance Thursday, he said regulators could finds ways to deter companies from borrowing to buy back their own stock money he said would be better spent investing in the company, including brick-and-mortar infrastructure.

"There would be ways, and I'm not going to go into it, where you could incentivize a company to invest in their own businesses rather than buy back stock," he said.

Indeed, Mr. Icahn may have benefited from some provisions of Dodd-Frank. He invests out of a family office, which the law exempts from rules that apply to hedge funds and private-equity funds.

 Andrew Ackerman and David Benoit contributed to this article.

SEC Watchdog Details Rare Probe Into Worker's  Attendance Fraud'

By Dave Michaels

The internal watchdog for the Securities and Exchange Commission has taken the rare step of accusing an employee of committing "attendance fraud," saying the public official was paid $125,000 for more than 1,200 hours of work "that he did not work or account for."

The claim is included in separate reports published this year by the inspector general for the securities regulator, most recently in the agency's semiannual report issued Nov. 14.

The report doesn't name the employee facing the accusation, but several people familiar with the matter said it is Erozan Kurtas, who was a star quant at the agency tasked with helping examiners parse hedge funds' complex trading strategies.

Mr. Kurtas left the SEC in 2015 to join the Financial Industry Regulatory Authority , an industry-funded monitor of U.S. brokerages, as the inspector general's probe was still under way. Finra named him head of the office of advanced data analytics, reporting directly to its chief executive.

Judith Germano, an attorney for Mr. Kurtas, didn't respond to repeated requests to address whether Mr. Kurtas was the employee named in the SEC report. But in an email statement in response to questions from the Journal, she said any claim that Mr. Kurtas "abused his position at the SEC is false and without merit."

"Erozan Kurtas was a top performer at the SEC who received outstanding written and verbal performance reviews from his supervisors and helped develop advanced software tools that saved taxpayers millions of dollars and helped move the SEC's exam program into the technological age," Ms. Germano wrote.

Federal inspectors general are independent watchdogs tasked with finding waste, fraud or abuse in the government. They routinely publish their findings to inform Congress about their work and to prod agencies to fix problems that they find.

Inspectors general can't file criminal or civil charges against an investigative target, but they sometimes refer their findings to federal prosecutors if they believe a law has been broken. The inspector general referred this investigation to a prosecutor, who "declined prosecution based on insufficient dollar loss and potential venue challenges," the report states.

It is unusual for the SEC's inspector general to probe attendance fraud because routine complaints about employees are usually handled by their managers. Since taking office in 2013, SEC Inspector General Carl Hoecker reported opening only two other similar cases. Investigators dismissed one claim outright, saying there was no evidence to support it. In another, they found an employee who logged over 200 hours of overtime didn't seek proper approval for additional time at the office.

Mr. Kurtas arrived at the SEC in 2010 after working at data-storage company Seagate Technology and credit-rating firm S&P Global Inc ., part of a recent wave of hires from outside the bureaucratic ranks to infuse the agency with more Wall Street expertise. His supervisors in Washington quickly promoted him as their in-house expert on the complex mathematical and statistical models that hedge funds and algorithmic trading firms use, the people said.

Mr. Kurtas preached that old-fashioned compliance experts "do not have the background to understand, monitor or test the models," according to a presentation to investment-firm compliance officers that he delivered in 2012.

Leaders at the SEC credited Mr. Kurtas with developing the National Exam Analytics Tool, known as NEAT, which allows the agency to absorb huge data sets from money managers and mutual funds and uses algorithms to look for signs of insider trading, cherry-picking or mispriced securities. The program is loaded on the desktop and laptop of every SEC inspector, according to other people familiar with the exam program's operations.

"With NEAT, our examiners are able to access and systematically analyze massive amounts of trading data from firms in a fraction of the time it has taken in years past," SEC Chairman Mary Jo White said in a 2014 speech.

The inspector general's office began investigating Mr. Kurtas after some employees reported that he wasn't seen as much as expected in the office, the people said.

The investigators checked the attendance of the investigated individual by measuring how often the person used a badge to enter the New York regional office, used a computer workstation at an SEC building, or remotely logged onto the SEC's computer networks, one of the people said. The U.S. Patent and Trademark Office used similar methods to examine potential payroll fraud by federal patent examiners as part of an investigation made public in August.

A person familiar with the accusation said one possible explanation that might exonerate Mr. Kurtas was that he often accompanied SEC inspectors on exams. That could have taken him outside of the office for long periods, during which he might not have logged onto an SEC computer network.

An earlier summary of the investigation, published in April, also didn't name Mr. Kurtas but said the subject of the probe "routinely arrived late for work and was frequently absent, in both cases failing to request or obtain authorized leave."

Justice Officials Support Rehearing of Case on CFPB's Constitutionality

By Yuka Hayashi

WASHINGTON Justice Department attorneys said Thursday a federal appeals court should convene a larger panel of judges to reconsider an earlier ruling that found the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau 's structure unconstitutional.

The U.S. Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit in the coming weeks is expected to decide whether to have the case heard by the entire court. An October ruling by a panel of its three judges said the agency's single-director structure violated the Constitution.

The Justice Department, in a friend-of-the-court brief, said a full panel of judges should hear the matter "in view of the importance of the case, the significance of the constitutional question."

The ruling, which could make it possible for the president to fire the CFPB director at will, has deepened the predicament for the CFPB. Republicans have been critical of the bureau, and in January will assume the White House and continue to control Congress. Citing the appeals court, some in the GOP have called on President-elect Donald Trump to fire Richard Cordray , the current CFPB director, and replace him with someone more friendly to the financial industry.

The CFPB was created by the Obama administration as part of the 2010 Dodd-Frank financial overhaul.

The appeals court took an unusual step in requesting a brief on rehearing the case from the U.S. Solicitor General's office, which represents the government on many appellate matters but wasn't previously involved in the CFPB case.

PHH on Thursday said in its brief the court should reject the request.

The appeals judges who ruled against the CFPB were Republican appointees, though they didn't all agree on the same reasoning in their decision. A majority of the judges on the appeals court are Democratic appointees and could be more sympathetic to the bureau.

In the case, PHH challenged Mr. Cordray's decision to impose a steep fine for allegedly accepting kickbacks from mortgage insurers.

Senate Democrats Press Wells Fargo Board for Answers on Sales Practices

By Emily Glazer

Democrats on the Senate Banking Committee demanded Wells Fargo & Co .'s board respond to questions the bank has yet to answer related to its sales practices scandal, according to a letter reviewed by The Wall Street Journal.

The senators asked the board for more information about its continuing independent investigation of the alleged fraud that caused the bank to pay a $185 million settlement in September over opening as many as 2.1 million accounts using fictitious or unauthorized customer information.

The senators in this latest letter also asked the board for a timeline of when its members learned of the questionable sales tactics, actions the board took to address the problem and reasons why it didn't investigate sooner, according to the letter. It asked for answers to its 10 questions by Jan. 7.

"Continued failure to answer questions especially basic questions about the causes and consequences of the fraud that Wells Fargo permitted for many years does nothing to restore the trust of Wells Fargo 's customers and shareholders, many of whom are our constituents," according to the letter.

Wells Fargo 's board in late September announced its independent investigation alongside moves to rescind a collective $61 million in pay from now-former Chief Executive John Stumpf and former retail banking head Carrie Tolstedt .

The board's independent directors "will take such other actions as they collectively deem appropriate," according to a statement at the time. The bank neither admitted nor denied the allegations in the settlement Sept. 8.

The 10 Democratic senators, led by ranking member Sen. Sherrod Brown (D., Ohio), submitted 58 questions to Wells Fargo management on Sept. 29 that they said weren't answered by Mr. Stumpf in two congressional hearings. The bank responded on Nov. 15, but cited the board's continuing independent investigation as a reason it couldn't fully answer some questions.

A bank spokeswoman at the time said Wells Fargo appreciates Congress's role and will continue to be responsive to lawmakers as the bank's board investigation progresses.

A person familiar with the board said Thursday that it previously responded to earlier letters from some of the Democratic senators that included some of the same questions.

A spokesman for the board reiterated Thursday that the board would make the findings of the investigation public upon its conclusion.

In addition to Sen. Brown, the latest letter was jointly submitted by Sens. Jack Reed (D., R.I.), Bob Menendez (D., N.J.), Jon Tester (D., Mont.), Mark Warner (D., Va.), Jeff Merkley (D., Ore.), Elizabeth Warren (D., Mass.), Heidi Heitkamp (D., N.D.) and Joe Donnelly (D., Ind.).

They asked the board to identify members of the special committee who are leading the investigation and the criteria to select them, why the board didn't launch an independent investigation earlier, and the probe's scope.

The senators also sought details on how the board will provide "sufficient transparency into and details of" the investigation to stakeholders and the public, among other questions relating to the sales practices issues.

WSJ Pro: Democrats Add Fresh Faces to Senate Banking Committee

By Gabriel T. Rubin

WASHINGTON Senate Democrats are reshuffling the makeup of the Banking Committee, with two veteran senators leaving the panel and three freshmen including two first elected in November joining.

Sens. Chuck Schumer of New York and Jeff Merkley of Oregon will be leaving the committee. Both are joining the Senate Democratic leadership, Mr. Schumer as the new minority leader and Mr. Merkley as chief deputy whip.

New to the banking committee will be Sen. Brian Schatz, a first-termer from Hawaii, and Sens.-elect Catherine Cortez Masto of Nevada and Chris Van Hollen of Maryland.

While Democrats are increasing their numbers on the committee to 11 from 10, Republicans will still have a majority on the panel once their assignments are announced.

Assignments will be finalized when the Senate reconvenes for the 115th Congress early next year. Sen. Mike Crapo (R., Idaho) is likely to succeed Sen. Richard Shelby (R., Ala.) as committee chairman. Sen. Sherrod Brown of Ohio will remain the committee's top Democrat.

The arrival of three strongly liberal Democrats will mark a shift leftward for the panel's Democratic contingent, despite the departure of Mr. Merkley, the only senator to endorse Sen. Bernie Sanders (I., Vt.) in his campaign for the Democratic presidential nomination.

Sen. Elizabeth Warren (D., Mass.), arguably the Democrats' strongest voice on financial regulation, will keep her seat on the committee. Without Mr. Schumer, who some liberals view warily due to his relationships in the financial industry, New York will lack its traditional presence on the Banking Committee.

Kurt Walters, the campaign director for the liberal group Demand Progress, hailed the changes on the committee and gave Mr. Schumer, often a target for criticism, credit for listening to the left flank of the party.

"[Mr. Schumer's] absence as a vote on the committee makes a small difference, but what's far more important is that he is showing that he has a willingness to listen to progressives and respond to the energy in the party's base," Mr. Walters said in an interview. "With Democrats trying to regain their footing as the party of economic populism, it's important to have strong voices in favor of Wall Street reform," he added.

Representatives for Mr. Schumer didn't respond to requests for comment.

Fed Finds Continued Growth in Noncash Payments

By Robin Sidel

Americans are pulling out their credit cards, but also hanging onto their checkbooks.

Those are some of the findings from a Federal Reserve study released Thursday that analyzes payment trends over the past three years and tracks the unrelenting march of electronic transactions.

In its sixth rendition of the study, the Fed said the number of U.S. noncash payments continued to gain ground between 2012 and 2015, rising 5.3% annually to 144 billion. The noncash payments, which include debit cards, credit cards and checks, had a value of nearly $178 trillion in 2015, up about 13% since 2012.

Debit cards helped lead the way as the number of those payments rose to 69.5 billion in 2015, up 23% from 2012.

The value of the debit-card payments rose by a smaller amount, however, reflecting the fact that debit transactions typically are of lesser value than those made on credit cards.

The number of credit-card payments reached 33.8 billion in 2015 with a value of $3.16 trillion, representing increases of roughly 25% from 2012. The growth was led by general-purpose cards that can be used everywhere compared with private-label cards that can only be used in specific stores.

The average value of general-purpose credit-card payments dropped to $90 from $93 between 2012 and 2015, the Fed said.

The surge in online shopping led to a growth in remote card payments, also called card-not-present transactions, the Fed said. Those transactions represented 19% of all card payments in 2015, up from 16% in 2012.

Check usage continued to fall among Americans, but the decline appears to be leveling off.

The number of check payments fell to 17.3 billion with a value of $26.83 trillion between 2012 and 2015, down 17% and 1.4%, respectively. Check payments fell at an annual rate of 4.4%, representing a slowdown in the rate of decline for the first time since the descent began in the mid-1990s, the Fed said.

The study, which is conducted every three years, is considered to be among the most comprehensive reports that track payments usage in the U.S. Starting in 2017, the Fed said it would collect some of the survey data annually, rather than every three years.

Platinum Partners Fraud Case Has Investigators Casting Wide Net

By Rob Copeland, Erica Orden and Nicole Hong

Federal investigators are asking questions about a billionaire New York hedge-fund manager and a Bermuda reinsurer as they examine the alleged fraud by hedge fund Platinum Partners .

On Monday, six Platinum executives, including chief investment officer Mark Nordlicht, were arrested and charged with faking the firm's investment performance. The allegations against Platinum, which had more than $1 billion under management, outline what would be one of the largest investment frauds since Bernard L. Madoff's Ponzi scheme.

As part of the Platinum probe, prosecutors are casting a wide net.

Investigators have interviewed potential witnesses about the relationship between Platinum co-founder Murray Huberfeld and billionaire hedge-fund manager Israel Englander , people familiar with the matter said. Mr. Englander, head of $34 billion Millennium Management LLC and one of the most emulated traders in the hedge-fund industry, hasn't been accused of wrongdoing.

A spokesman for Mr. Englander said he was unaware of any investigation into him personally and hadn't received any inquiries from prosecutors.

Mr. Huberfeld and Mr. Englander, who have been friends for decades, are members of the Orthodox Jewish community from Brooklyn, people close to them said. Mr. Huberfeld sought Mr. Englander's advice before helping start Platinum in 2003, one of the people said.

Mr. Huberfeld himself was arrested in June when he was charged with playing a role in the alleged bribery of a New York union official in exchange for investments in Platinum. He pleaded not guilty. He wasn't named in Monday's complaint but is the unnamed "Co-Conspirator 1" in the criminal indictment, said a person familiar with matter.

Federal investigators are probing the actions of Bermuda reinsurer Beechwood Re, people familiar with the matter said. Beechwood's investment adviser arm was two-thirds owned by Platinum's executives and close associates, according to regulators. Beechwood repeatedly directed its clients to invest in Platinum and related deals over the past three years, according to filings and people familiar with the matter.

Beechwood didn't disclose its Platinum ties to clients before telling them to invest with the firm, The Wall Street Journal earlier reported. A former Beechwood executive who was Mr. Huberfeld's nephew and later returned to Platinum was among those arrested Monday.

A Beechwood spokesman said: "Beechwood did not engage in wrongdoing and intends to cooperate fully with any investigation." Beechwood's chief executive has previously said he didn't tell clients about his firm's ties to Platinum because the ownership stakes were passive.

Representatives for the two U.S. Attorney's offices handling the Platinum investigations declined to comment.

Platinum's flagship fund, which has filed for bankruptcy protection, specialized in esoteric assets like loans tied to life insurance policies, which the firm was largely permitted to value itself, investor documents show. Launched in 2003, the firm reported some of the best performance in the hedge-fund world, with no down years and few down months for any of its funds.

Prosecutors said the men arrested on Monday made up the performance figures to collect their cut of any unrealized investment gains. All of the executives pleaded not guilty and declined further comment.

Platinum's investors, whom authorities say may total more than 600, include the families of New York real-estate executives Ralph Herzka and Rubin Schron and philanthropist Gordon Diamond, people familiar with the investments said.

Mr. Schron said in an interview that his investments were selected by a money manager. "I'm not really familiar with the investments we invest in," he said. "Maybe I've heard the name."

The money manager, Jack Bojman, said Platinum was in the portfolio before he was hired. "We just hoped it performed," he said.

Over the past year, Platinum restricted redemptions and then suspended them entirely. At the same time, its executives told investors they would eventually be paid back in full, perhaps even with further investment gains, investors said.

Shlomo Rechnitz, a billionaire California nursing-home magnate, had tens of millions of dollars tied up in Platinum and related deals, a person familiar with the matter said. He told friends he has cut back his charitable giving following Platinum's woes, in one instance slashing his annual donation to Jerusalem's Mir Yeshiva to $1 million from $5 million in years past.

Torah Umesorah, an Orthodox Jewish education charity which provides teacher training for more than 800 schools, had millions invested in Platinum. That is because when developer Richard Stadtmauer made a 2008 donation to the organization he recommended that it be invested solely in Platinum, according to three people familiar with the matter. Mr. Stadtmauer had an apartment in the same Manhattan building as Mr. Huberfeld and Mr. Nordlicht, according to a person familiar with the situation.

Torah Umesorah asked for the money back last year and was rebuffed, people familiar with the matter said. Mr. Stadtmauer then threatened to sue Platinum if it didn't give the money back, the people said.

Instead, Platinum agreed to treat the redemption as a loan, bearing interest. It hasn't yet been repaid, the people said.

Arrangements like that were flagged in Monday's indictment, which prosecutors termed "preferential redemptions, often for arbitrary reasons."

WSJ Pro: FDIC Seeks Input on Handbook for Creating New Banks

By Ryan Tracy

WASHINGTON The Federal Deposit Insurance Corp . sought feedback on a new handbook for investors looking to start a bank, a response to criticism that the agency hasn't approved enough new charters since the financial crisis.

The handbook, the first of its kind in recent history, describes the new charter application process and outlines hurdles that applicants for new bank charters have to clear. The FDIC also asked several questions about the handbook Thursday, seeking advice on how the agency could provide more information.

New bank charters have slowed significantly since 2008, when 90 so-called de novo banks were formed, according to the FDIC. Since 2011, the FDIC says it has approved five new charter applications.

The agency also says it received five applications in 2016.

The FDIC said it welcomes new bank applications and has attributed the dearth of new banks to market conditions, such as low interest rates, which are making it less attractive to start a bank. The banking industry and its allies in Congress have said a larger factor is increased regulation on banks and, in particular, increased scrutiny of new bank charters by the FDIC.

"This handbook is the latest in a series of steps we have taken to support the establishment of de novo institutions," FDIC Chairman Martin J. Gruenberg said in a statement Thursday. "Our goal is to increase transparency about the application approval process and resources available to assist potential organizers."

Lawmakers Ask SEC to Block Chinese Group's Plan to Buy Chicago Stock Exchange

By Alexander Osipovich

A bipartisan group of U.S. lawmakers has urged regulators to block the proposed acquisition of the Chicago Stock Exchange by a Chinese-led investment group, warning the deal could threaten national security.

The lawmakers' appeal, sent in a letter on Thursday to departing Securities and Exchange Commission Chair Mary Jo White and her two fellow commissioners, was a last-ditch effort to block the sale after it was approved earlier this month by the Committee on Foreign Investment in the U.S . The CFIUS review was regarded as the bigger hurdle to the sale, since the committee has probed some deals so deeply that Chinese investors have walked away.

"Allowing a Chinese owned and possibly government-affiliated firm direct access to manipulate our $22 trillion U.S. equity marketplace is unprecedented and poses an unnecessary and unacceptable risk to American companies and to the financial security of this country," said the letter, a copy of which was reviewed by The Wall Street Journal.

Five members of the House of Representatives, including three Republicans and two Democrats, signed the letter, which also urged the SEC to provide more time for public feedback on the proposed deal. The SEC's deadline for responses is Jan. 3.

"Holding the public comment period for a brief duration during the holiday season clearly indicates an effort to avoid negative response," the letter said. The signatories are Rep. Robert Pittenger (R., N.C.), Rep. Earl L. "Buddy" Carter (R., Ga.), Rep. Peter DeFazio (D., Ore.), Collin Peterson (D., Minn.) and David Joyce (R., Ohio).

An SEC spokesman said the regulator was aware of the letter but declined to comment further.

Mr. Pittenger, one the signers of the letter, said he expected the incoming team of President-elect Donald J. Trump to be warier of the deal than the Obama administration.

"I think he understands the great concerns we have with China," Mr. Pittenger said in a telephone interview with The Wall Street Journal.

The Chicago Stock Exchange has less than 1% of U.S. equities volume, and its parent company had revenue of just $19.5 million last year. But its license remains valuable because new exchanges face a gantlet of regulatory barriers before they can launch.

If the proposed acquisition goes through, Chinese investors would own 49.5% of the exchange's parent company, according to a filing released by the Chicago bourse in late November.

According to the filing, the new owners would include a Chinese company, Chongqing Casin Enterprise Group Co., which would own 20% of the exchange's parent company. The filing said other proposed owners include Chongqing Jintian Industrial Co. Ltd., Chongqing Longshang Decoration Co. Ltd. and Raptor Group, the family office of former hedge-fund manager Jim Pallotta .

The filing said none of the firms that would acquire an ownership interest in the Chicago exchange through the deal was directly or indirectly controlled by a governmental entity.

Advocates of the deal say the possibility of seeing the exchange turned into a spy outpost is remote and the risks have been overstated by politicians who don't understand trading.

A spokesman for the Chicago Stock Exchange declined to comment. A U.S.-based lawyer for Chongqing Casin Enterprise Group, Peter Rooney of law firm Orrick, Herrington & Sutcliffe LLP , didn't immediately respond to a request for comment.

Fitch Cuts Portugal Banking Sector to Negative

Fitch changes outlook for Portuguese banking sector to negative from stable to reflect "the intensified pressure on capital from the weak profitability and asset quality, amid a highly indebted economy with low growth prospects." Adds that with a phase-in total capital ratio of 13.1% at end-June 2016, Portugal's banking system is expected to try to increase their buffers in 2017, including by tapping the subordinated debt market. (patricia.kowsmann@wsj.com; @kowsmann)

Majority of Trump Voters Want to Keep or Expand CFPB

Republican lawmakers are pushing the incoming administration to significantly limit the power of the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau , a watchdog agency crated by the Obama administration after the financial crisis. According to a poll, that goes against the wishes of a majority of Trump voters. The survey released by Morning Consult showed 41% of Trump voters said they wanted the new administration to leave the CFPB alone, while 14% said they wanted its power expanded. Only 7% want to get rid of the agency, while 21% want to see its power curbed. Trump has called for repealing the post-crisis financial regulations broadly but hasn't said anything specific about the CFPB. The survey was conducted by Morning Consult and Glover Park Group , a Washington communications firm founded by Michael Feldman , a former Democratic staffer. (yuka.hayashi@wsj.com; @TokyoWoods)

European Syndicated Loan Volume Falls, Dealogic Data Show

European syndicated loan volume totaled $765.9 billion so far in 2016, down 35% from the same period in 2015, data from Dealogic show. This is the lowest volume since 2012 when syndicated loans stood at $713.6 billion. Refinancing and amendments with $401.1 billion accounted for 53% of year to date- deals, an increase by 3% from the same time period in 2015. Acquisition-related loan volume dropped 18% to $300.1 billion, or 364 deals, in 2016 from $367.5 billion, or 439 deals, in 2015. (nina.Trentmann@wsj.com , Twitter: @Nina_Trentmann)

What Trump's Cabinet Picks Mean for Markets

"http://www.wsj.com/articles/what-trumps-cabinet-picks-mean-for-markets-1482422691", the people that President-elect Donald Trump is slotting for his administration have emphasized that he aims to be extremely shareholder friendly. The question facing investors is what sort of value to place on that friendship.

Never before has a U.S. president stocked his cabinet with as many businessmen as Mr. Trump is aiming to. While putting up a Goldman Sachs Group alum like Steven Mnuchin for Treasury secretary counts as almost status quo, tapping Exxon Mobil Chief Executive Rex Tillerson for secretary of state, and fast-food chain executive Andy Puzder as labor secretary shows how highly Mr. Trump values business experience and how focused he is on reshaping the regulatory environment in businesses' favor.

The Impact of Prudential Policies on Credit Supply

How do prudential policies affect the supply of credit in the  U.S.? "https://www.federalreserve.gov/econresdata/ifdp/2016/files/ifdp1186.pdf"by Richard Correa and Seung Jung Lee of the Federal Reserve, the Comprehensive Capital Analysis and Review had a negative effect on jumbo mortgage originations and approval rates at stress-tested banks when it was first applied in 2011. They also found Supervisory Guidance on Leveraged Lending led to a notable decrease in "speculative-grade term-loan originations" at regulated banks in 2014. Their analysis shows that banks with worse capital positions were  affected more negatively by prudential policies.

How Trump and Republicans Can Repeal CFTC Regulations

The transition to the presidency of Donald Trump provides the right environment for Congress to use its powers under the Congressional Review Act to strip back recent Obama administration regulations, "http://www.cadwalader.com/resources/clients-friends-memos/the-trump-administration-potential-levers-of-regulatory-change-affecting-the-commodities-markets", a law firm. The review act, which allows Congress to effectively overturn "major" new rules before they become effective, could apply to the Commodity Futures Trading Commission 's "new position aggregation rule and re-proposed rules addressing the imposition of capital requirements for non-prudentially regulated swap dealers and the regulation of automated trading in the commodities markets," the firm says.
