Economy
Quick Hits: Central Banks of Thailand and Taiwan Expected to Hold Rates; Turkey's central bank is seen raising rates substantially next year, after standing pat Tuesday

By WSJ Staff 
815 words
20 December 2016
16:47
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The central banks of Thailand and Taiwan are expected to leave their policy interest rates unchanged at meetings this week, and the Bank of Japan's Haruhiko Kuroda said the BOJ won't reduce its ETF purchases even as stock prices in Tokyo have seen strong gains. Here are quick hits on central banking from around the world.

Thailand's Central Bank Expected to Keep Policy Rate Stable

Thailand's central bank is expected to maintain its policy interest rate at 1.5% for the 13th straight meeting, according to 15 economists polled by The Wall Street Journal. "Concerns about private debt levels will prevent further easing," Moody's Analytics says, "unless serious downside risks materialize." Says Amonthep Chawla, head of research at CIMB Thai Bank: "Thailand's economy is recovering at a slow pace, which is seen to require low interest rate and high liquidity to support growth." The Bank of Thailand's latest decision on its benchmark interest rate is due at 0705 GMT Wednesday.

Nopparat Chaichalearmmongkol

Taiwan's Central Bank Likely to Keep Rates on Hold

Taiwan's central bank is expected to hold fire at its policy meeting Thursday. Economists say recent economic data ranging from export figures to industrial production point to positive growth momentum in the fourth quarter, relieving pressure on the central bank to take further action to support the economy at this stage, though they disagree on what the bank's next step might be. Citi says the central bank has done enough to support the economy and it is now down to the government to pull its weight. Ma Tieying, an economist at DBS Group Research, sees the benchmark discount rate remaining at 1.375% over the next four quarters, though she expects the next move to be upward. Capital Economics takes a dimmer view. "We think the recovery is likely to tail off early next year," the research firm says. If the economy slows as Capital expects, the bank's easing cycle will resume, it says.

Paul Jackson

Bank of Japan's ETF Purchases Encounter X-Factor: Trump

The Bank of Japan's 6-trillion-a-year ($51 billion) purchases of stock funds known as ETFs is coming under scrutiny, thanks to Donald Trump. At a post-policy meeting press conference Tuesday, BOJ Gov. Haruhiko Kuroda was asked several times about whether he will proceed with the aggressive purchases even after recent big gains in Tokyo shares, driven by expectations for Mr. Trump's promised high-spending policies. Mr. Kuroda denied the possibility of any reduction and reiterated that the measure wasn't stock-price manipulation but was aimed at generating inflation. But the news conference suggests such grilling could only intensify should stocks rise more.

Takashi Nakamichi

Kuroda Briefly Slips in Explaining BOJ Policy

Investors aren't the only ones who sometimes get confused about the Bank of Japan's policies: Even Gov. Haruhiko Kuroda slips up. At a news conference Tuesday, Mr. Kuroda started answering a question about interest rates by saying the central bank sought to keep the yield of the 10-year government bond around 0.1%. If true, that would be a big policy shift, but he quickly corrected himself and affirmed, in line with current policy, that the yield target is zero. At a previous news conference, Mr. Kuroda said his five-year term would expire in March 2018, only to correct himself a few minutes later and confirm that his term ends in April 2018.

Peter Landers

EUR/SEK Moving Lower Ahead of Riksbank Meeting

EUR/SEK is moving lower as market participants don't believe the Riksbank will cut rates on Wednesday, says Danske Bank. Danske expects the Riksbank to prolong the current quantitative-easing program by six months and to cut the repo rate by an additional 10 basis points to minus 0.60%. "We stress that the Riksbank will, at least, be very dovish, which should put a floor under EUR/SEK into the monetary policy decision."

Dominic Chopping

Substantial Rate Rises by Turkey Likely in 2017

Turkey's central bank interest rate decision is only likely to be a pause in the tightening cycle, says Capital Economics, adding that it expects substantial interest-rate increases next year. The central bank Tuesday held all interest rates steady, surprising economists who had expected a rate increase. "The accompanying statement didn't give much away, although it did suggest that economic weakness GDP contracted by 1.8% y/y in Q3 meant another rate hike wasn't warranted today," says Capital Economics.

Yeliz Candemir

Eliminating IOER Payments Could Boost Inflation Risk, Research Argues

149 words
20 December 2016
15:17
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The risk of inflation would dramatically rise if the Fed were "http://www.fedfin.com/images/stories/client_reports/FedFin, which enables policy makers to put a floor under short-term interest rates, according to a new research paper commissioned by the American Bankers Association. Karen Shaw Petrou of Federal Financial Analytics writes that without paying interest on reserves or using overnight reverse repurchase agreements, the Fed wouldn't be able to put a floor under rates, because other policy tools no longer function as they used to. Eliminating those tools would create a shadow monetary-policy transmission, forcing the Fed to rely on nonbanks and government-sponsored enterprises for its short-term floor, Ms. Petrou argues.

Who Might Trump Choose as CFPB Chief?

Donald Trump's victory has thrown the future of the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau intso huge uncertainty, raising questions about its structure, budget and rules in the pipeline. But industry experts are increasingly focusing on a potential change at the agency's helm as the most logical first step.

Director Richard Cordray intends to stay in the role, said Jen Howard, the CFPB's communications chief. Mr. Cordray "was confirmed by a bipartisan group of 66 senators to serve a term until July 2018 and has no plans to step down," she said in a statement.

Still, experts say Mr. Trump could decide to remove him (an option "http://www.wsj.com/articles/trump-versus-cordray-can-new-president-fire-cfpb-chief-on-day-one-1480719515" to the president-elect) or Mr. Cordray might choose to pursue a new opportunity, such as seeking public office in his home state of Ohio.

Mr. Trump hasn't commented on the CFPB's future and representatives of his transition team have been mum about a personnel change at the agency. But a few names have been frequently mentioned among industry experts as potential picks for the top job.

Rep. Randy Neugebauer (R., Texas)

Mr. Neugebauer is retiring this month from the House, where he led his party's attack on the CFPB as chairman of the Financial Services Committee's subcommittee on financial institutions. He was closely involved in drafting of the "http://www.wsj.com/pro/financialregulation/graphics/understanding-the-financial-choice-act/", committee Chairman Jeb Hensarling's broad financial deregulation package. The proposal includes a raft of steps to transform the CFPB, including turning it into a five-person commission and putting its budget under congressional oversight.

In an interview last week, Mr. Neugebauer said that change at the CFPB will initially come not from the legislative process, but from Mr. Trump's "putting in people that will bring change to the process." He added that Mr. Trump would look at Mr. Cordray's performance to determine whether he could be relieved of his position for cause, such as malfeasance, neglect and inefficiency.

Is Mr. Neugebauer himself interested in leading the CFPB? "I really don't want to speculate on something I might or I might not do when the said opportunity really hasn't availed itself so far," he said.

Speculation over Mr. Neugebauer's interest in the job stems in part from comment he made during a house hearing in June 2015, remarking that if the next administration were to be run by the Republicans, the CFPB would be headed by a commission and that its director could be "Randy Neugebauer." He said in the interview he was using his name as an example rather than lobbying for the job.

Todd Zywicki

Mr. Zywicki is a law professor at "http://www.wsj.com/articles/creightons-take-free-market-thinkers-eye-trump-era-as-their-renaissance-1479212303" and a former policy-planning director at the Federal Trade Commission. A well-known critic of consumer financial regulation with strong ties to Republican lawmakers, he has made frequent appearances at congressional hearings. At one hearing earlier this year, he called the CFPB a "super-bureaucracy wielding an unprecedented combination of vast, vaguely defined substantive powers with no democratic accountability."

Mr. Zywicki didn't respond to repeated requests for comment.

Besides his deep knowledge of consumer financial laws, Mr. Zywicki would bring a unique experience as a change agent at the CFPB: his longtime association with Sen. Elizabeth Warren (D., Mass.), a leading proponent of the agency. In the early 2000s, the two law professors sparred at congressional hearings and in the media over revamping bankruptcy laws, with Ms. Warren advocating for consumers and Mr. Zywicki siding with industry.

Other names mentioned as possible candidates include "http://www.wsj.com/articles/dodd-frank-should-be-repealed-1475096111" , director of financial regulation studies at the libertarian Cato Institute; "http://www.wsj.com/articles/trump-names-fed-critic-others-to-treasury-landing-team-1479823288" , distinguished senior fellow at R Street Institute, another libertarian think tank; and J. Howard Beales, a George Washington University professor and former senior official at the Federal Trade Commission.

Mr. Pollock declined to comment and Mr. Beales couldn't be reached for comment. Mr. Calabria said he hadn't talked to anyone on the transition team about the job, but "given the importance of having quality individuals willing to serve, I'd certainly have to give the position some consideration if asked to serve."

Promoting Productive Lending Through Regulation

Finance & Markets: EU to Scrutinize Data Use

By Julia-Ambra Verlaine 
490 words
20 December 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B7
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

BRUSSELS -- European authorities are considering regulating the use of consumer data tied to insurance and other financial products, as social media and technology ranging from the Apple Watch to fitness-tracking devices increase the amount of personal information that is available online.

In a bid to avoid unfair customer treatment and exploitation by insurers and lenders, the European Union's top financial watchdogs launched a three-month period to consult stakeholders and the public.

"Internet and mobile devices have become a core part of our lifestyle, and the ways in which data is generated, collected, stored, processed and used have evolved at unprecedented rates," said Gabriel Bernardino, chairman of the European Insurance and Occupational Pensions Authority.

As a result, the continent's supervisors are trying to grasp what the "big-data phenomenon" means for consumers and the financial industry as a whole.

A sharp drop in the cost of storing data has helped foster so-called big data, initially fueled by the use of cellphones tracking everything from location and purchasing habits to self-expression via photos shared on Instagram.

Wearable devices tracking health and fitness provide insurers with details on how often an individual exercises, while telematic boxes in cars monitor the habits of drivers. Coupled with sophisticated algorithms, insurers and lenders can detect patterns in a process known as predictive analytics.

EU rules covering consumer and data protection haven't been revisited to incorporate such technological advances. While steps are being taken to address cybersecurity and protect sensitive data from hackers, rules tailored to information stemming from social media are lacking, officials say.

Statistics show that 81% of Europeans feel they don't have control over their personal data online, according to a survey conducted by Eurobarometer in 2015, and users often don't realize what social-media information is publicly available.

Regulators worry that new data-collection processes could encourage insurers and banks to change their pricing practices to the detriment of consumers. For instance, data showing that a client was loyal to a brand or brands could prompt companies to increase their prices.

Rates for household insurance, which is a legal requirement for renters or property owners in several EU countries, could rise as satellite imagery pinpoints which properties are more prone to flooding, earthquakes or elevated crime.

On the flip side, big data could help decrease regulatory and compliance costs for global investment banks required under new postcrisis rules to extensively report internal risk.

EU authorities are also looking into how investment funds are using nonfinancial information such as social-media news and satellite images to develop trading strategies.

The Joint Committee of the European Supervisory Authorities -- which includes the European Banking Authority, the European Securities and Markets Authority, and EIOPA -- said officials would decide next year whether to propose action to regulate data collection.

Technology
Breach Adds to Hunger for Insurance

By Nicole Friedman 
548 words
16 December 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Cyberinsurance is the fastest-growing insurance product in America, fueled by a slate of recent corporate and government hackings.

Yahoo Inc. on Wednesday disclosed that an unauthorized third party stole data associated with more than 1 billion user accounts in 2013, the company's second disclosure of a data theft this year. In October,a distributed denial-of-service attack left hundreds of websites unreachable, including Twitter Inc. and Netflix Inc.

PricewaterhouseCoopers forecasts that the now roughly $3 billion corporate cyberinsurance market will grow to $7.5 billion in premiums by 2020, driven partly by rising prices. The growth would be in contrast to flat or falling prices in most othertypes of insurance.

"Nothing else is growing as quickly as cyber," said Bob Parisi, cyberproduct leader for brokerage Marsh, a unit of Marsh & McLennan Cos. "Some of the leading carriers are telling us they are doubling their book every year."

Rising demand will allow insurance firms to raise prices between 5% and 10% in 2017, with larger increases for retailers and health-care companies, said insurance broker Willis Towers Watson. For most other types of commercial insurance, the broker predicted price declines.

But such rapid growth for a young product could leave insurance companies exposed to risks that they don't fully understand and unexpected costs.

Unlike with natural disasters or car accidents, insurers don't have years of actuarial data to rely upon when pricing cyberinsurance plans.And while insurers can spread out their property risk by insuring cars or buildings in multiple states or countries, it is hard to do the same with cyberinsurance. A single cyberattack could affect many companies around the world.

"Risk used to have some sort of geographic boundary," but cyberrisk "knows no boundary," said Inga Beale, chief executive officer at Lloyd's of London, an insurance and reinsurance market.

For example, October's attack was aimed at web-technology provider Dynamic Network Services Inc., known as Dyn, which helps direct traffic across the internet. Before the attack, some insurance companies might not have known which of their customers used Dyn, making it difficult to predict how exposed they might be to a Dyn outage, said Scott Stransky, assistant vice president at AIR Worldwide, a risk-modeling firm.

"Because of how competitive the cyberinsurance world is, there are fewer and fewer questions being asked" before policies are priced and sold, he said.

The need for experts has meant competition to hire experienced cyberinsurance underwriters is fierce. Marsh joined this year with analytics firm Cyence to help its clients assess their cyberrisk, and broker Aon PLC agreed in October to acquire cybersecurity specialist Stroz Friedberg Inc.

Stroz Friedberg is working with Yahoo on its two reported data breaches, people familiar with the matter said.

Insurers including American International Group Inc. have invested in or joined with firms that focus on cyberrisk in recent years.

Most companies with cyberinsurance policies have payout limits of $10 million to $25 million, according to a survey by Aon.

The average payout from an insurance company for a data breach is $665,000, according to an October study by cyberrisk assessment company NetDiligence.

Markets
Yahoo and Other Breaches Drive Surge in Corporate Hacking Insurance; Cyberinsurance is the fastest-growing insurance product in America

By Nicole Friedman 
798 words
15 December 2016
14:36
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Cyberinsurance is the fastest-growing insurance product in America, fueled by a slate of recent corporate and government hackings.

Yahoo Inc. on Wednesday "http://www.wsj.com/articles/yahoo-discloses-new-breach-of-1-billion-user-accounts-1481753131" associated with more than one billion user accounts in 2013, the company's second disclosure of a data theft this year. In October, a distributed denial-of-service attack left hundreds of websites unreachable, including Twitter Inc. and Netflix Inc.

PricewaterhouseCoopers forecasts that the now roughly $3 billion corporate cyberinsurance market will grow to $7.5 billion in premiums by 2020, driven partly by rising prices. The growth would be in contrast to flat or falling prices in most other types of insurance due to a lack of major catastrophes in recent years.

"Nothing else is growing as quickly as cyber," said Bob Parisi, cyber product leader for brokerage Marsh, a unit of Marsh & McLennan Cos. "Some of the leading carriers are telling us they are doubling their book every year."

Cyberinsurance plans are typically narrow and only apply to data breaches, including the cost of notifying customers and complying with state regulations.

Rising demand will allow insurance firms to raise prices between 5% and 10% in 2017, with larger increases for retailers and health-care companies, said insurance broker Willis Towers Watson. For most other types of commercial insurance, the broker predicted price declines.

But such rapid growth for a young product could leave insurance companies exposed to risks that they don't fully understand and unexpected costs.

Unlike with natural disasters or car accidents, insurers don't have years of actuarial data to rely upon when pricing cyberinsurance plans. And while insurers can spread out their property risk by insuring cars or buildings in multiple states or countries, it is hard to do the same with cyberinsurance. A single cyberattack could affect many companies around the world.

"Risk used to have some sort of geographic boundary," but cyberrisk "knows no boundary," said Inga Beale, chief executive officer at Lloyd's of London, an insurance and reinsurance market.

For example, October's attack was aimed at web-technology provider Dynamic Network Services Inc., known as Dyn, which helps direct traffic across the internet.

Before the attack, some insurance companies might not have known which of their customers used Dyn, making it difficult to predict how exposed they might be to a Dyn outage, said Scott Stransky, assistant vice president at AIR Worldwide, a risk-modeling firm.

"Because of how competitive the cyberinsurance world is, there are fewer and fewer questions being asked" before policies are priced and sold, he said.

The need for experts has meant competition to hire experienced cyberinsurance underwriters is fierce. Marsh joined this year with analytics firm Cyence to help its clients assess their cyberrisk, and broker Aon PLC agreed in October to acquire cybersecurity specialist Stroz Friedberg Inc.

Stroz Friedberg is working with Yahoo on its two reported data breaches, people familiar with the matter said.

Insurers including American International Group Inc. have invested in or joined with firms that focus on cyberrisk in recent years.

Most companies with cyberinsurance policies have payout limits of $10 million to $25 million, according to a survey by Aon. Large companies can buy policies with limits above $100 million, to as high as about $500 million. While most big U.S. companies have purchased cyberinsurance, coverage remains scant among small businesses and outside the U.S.

Companies are increasingly worried about hacks that cause website outages or damage physical infrastructure, and some insurers offer coverage for lost revenue due to a cyberattack. The growth of internet-connected household devices, from thermostats to watches to videogame consoles, also raises concerns that hackers could use those devices to store malicious software or conduct attacks.

The average payout from an insurance company for a data breach is $665,000, according to an October study by cyberrisk assessment company NetDiligence. Business-interruption losses could be much larger, experts say.

Insurance executives say the October attack probably didn't cause insurance losses because cyberinsurance for business interruption usually only kicks in after a website has been down for several hours. But it raised alarms among insurers and their clients.

"That was one of the first events where I was like,  Holy hell, we're going to have an aggregation problem in the industry,'" said Emy Donavan, North American head of cyber for Allianz Global Corporate & Specialty.

Leslie Scism contributed to this article.

Write to Nicole Friedman at "mailto:nicole.friedman@wsj.com"

Flight-Tracking App Hopper Raises $62 Million in Expansion Bid; A year after offering ticket bookings, the Canadian startup seeks wider international profile

By Cat Zakrzewski

WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Hopper Inc. has raised 82 million Canadian dollars (US $61.7 million) as it moves to expand its flight price-tracking mobile app to more countries and gain more airline partners abroad.

Caisse de dpt et placement du Qubec, a large Canadian pension fund, was the lead investor and invested C$40 million. The remainder of the Series C financing came from existing investors Brightspeak Ventures, Accomplice, OMERS, Investissement Quebec and BDC Capital IT Venture Fund.

The app has raised C$104 million to date. Hopper Chief Executive and co-founder Frederic Lalonde said the company wouldn't disclose valuation, but added, "We're growing substantially."

In addition to tracking flight prices, Hopper began booking tickets through its app last year and is selling about $1 million a day in tickets, according to the company. Its app, available in iTunes and the Google Play store, has been downloaded more than 10 million times.

Hopper is a mobile-only price tracker, and Mr. Lalonde said people's purchasing habits are very different on their phones compared with what they do online. He said about 90% of Hopper's sales come as a result of people receiving push notifications.

He said Hopper, whose logo is a running rabbit, has the ability to compete because of its massive data set.

"Underneath the cute running bunny there's a massive big-data play," said Mr. Lalonde.

He said Hopper is able to predict flight prices because it tracks data around travel bookings throughout the Web, not just its own data.

The company has also seen some success in suggesting flight purchases to customers. If a customer is tracking flights from New York to Rome, Hopper may suggest a flight deal for the same dates from New York to Milan, even if the customer wasn't tracking those locations.

"People are buying a trip that a machine told them to look at rather than what they asked for," he said.

Mr. Lalonde said he believes the future of e-commerce is in predicting consumer behavior.

As Hopper seeks to expand internationally, the company will use the funding to shore up its infrastructure and develop payment systems and partnerships abroad, Mr. Lalonde said. The company plans to ramp up its staff in the next year, from 40 employees to 120.

Databricks Inc. wants to help companies that don't have the same engineering might as a Google or a Facebook to leverage big data, in industries ranging from health care to media.

"The rest of the Fortune 500 are struggling," Databricks Chief Executive Ali Ghodsi said.

The company announced on Thursday it raised $60 million in new capital from existing investors New Enterprise Associates and Andreessen Horowitz. Valuation wasn't disclosed for the round, and the company has raised $107.5 million to date.

Databricks launched in 2013 to commercialize Apache Spark, an open-source data-processing engine. Databricks has about 400 customers, including Capital One, Riot Games, Under Armor's MyFitnessPal, Viacom, NBC Universal and Chegg.

Mr. Ghodsi said the company will use the funding to expand both in the U.S. market, where it already has a strong foothold, and in Asia and Europe.

Additionally the company has made inroads among financial institutions as they use more cloud services. It can be used to detect fraudulent transactions.

The company is also used widely in media companies, the industrial internet and among federal agencies.

In addition to its data platform, Databricks also provides Spark training. In 2015, the company trained more than 22,000 people.

Letters to the Editor
Major Surgery Needed for Health-Care Reform

711 words
15 December 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The proposal of James C. Capretta and Scott Gottlieb does little more than tweak the current market for individual coverage ("The Four Legs of a New Health-Care System," op-ed, Dec. 1). No one has been bold enough to address the fundamental failure -- the exchanges aren't markets in any meaningful sense. They function more like regulated utilities with severe limits on price and product differentiation. If insurance is priced based on the expected average cost of all potential applicants (i.e., community rated), only those who are less healthy than average will be willing to purchase insurance. Behavior termed "adverse selection" lies at the heart of the rapidly deteriorating Affordable Care Act insurance exchanges.

Perhaps there is a big-data approach to address the incomplete information problem. Unlike the rigid community rating in the exchanges, each policy could be priced to reflect its expected costs. This would largely eliminate the incentives underlying adverse selection and could increase voluntary participation by healthier individuals.

Insurance companies could decline to insure individuals who they deem uninsurable. A "residual market," an industry-supported insurer of last resort, an alternative to a medical high-risk pool, would be available at an affordable premium to anyone who is unable to obtain coverage in the competitive "voluntary" market.

This describes the existing state-based markets for workers' compensation insurance. Republicans say they want the states to play the lead role in individual health-insurance markets. The states already know how to do this. The National Association of Insurance Commissioners has a model act in its files. Enough with tweaks, it's time to be bold.

Harry Shuford

Delray Beach, Fla.

---

Republicans should take control of the dialogue and make health care the primary issue, dismissing ObamaCare as a failed partisan attempt addressing the wrong problem, i.e., the cost of insurance rather than the cost of health care. The primary focus should be on reducing the cost of health care and making it available. Both would be served by strengthening the authors' suggestions to deregulate the market for medical services by going further to open up the medical delivery system to various paramedical professionals like physicians assistants, nurse practitioners, midwives, pharmacists, etc. Doing so would free up the upper-tier skills of M.D.s for services where they're really required.

Tim Mahar

Rockledge, Fla.

---

What is missing is a recommendation to increase price transparency and thereby promote competition in the provision of medical services based on price. After all, should the retail cost of an MRI, what a person with a high-deductible plan is asked to pay, really exceed what the Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services pays by more than a factor of 10? What if medical-service providers had to compete for customers? Do you think there would be that large a spread for any medical service?

James Patek

Fripp Island, S.C.

---

The four-legged health-care plan collapses because it doesn't address the essential issue of the individual mandate. It's unfair that some people opt to go uninsured and then show up in the emergency room where care must be delivered, while others pay their premiums, whether they use medical services or not, and are essentially subsidizing the uninsured. The individual mandate must be continued to maintain fairness for all and for the four-legged system to remain standing.

Michael Mayer, M.D.

Westfield, N.J.

---

A fifth principle should be included: a change in the medical tort system. Defensive medicine contributes huge costs to the current system. Indeed, no true estimate can be made as the pervasive climate of defensive medicine works in ways that even the practicing physician cannot accurately quantify. This is because much of the most modern medicine still includes enormous expert opinion differences, often unique to the individual case. Therefore physicians trained in this litigious environment order far too many expensive tests in an effort to protect themselves from lawsuits in the event of a poor outcome. My educated guess is that 25%-30% of medical costs are generated for this sole reason.

Peter C. Welch, M.D., Ph.D.

Carmel, N.Y.

Technology
Apple , SoftBank Held Talks on Tech Fund

By Liz Hoffman and Tripp Mickle 
537 words
14 December 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Asia Edition)
AWSJ
B3
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Apple Inc . has held talks about investing in a $100 billion fund being raised by Japanese internet and telecommunications company SoftBank Group Corp ., a move that would provide Silicon Valley cash -- and cachet -- to what would be the world's largest technology fund.

Apple and SoftBank have discussed an investment of as much as $1 billion, according to people familiar with the matter, who cautioned that an agreement hasn't been completed. The status of the talks couldn't be learned.

An Apple investment would connect the world's most valuable company -- Apple 's market value is nearly $600 billion -- with one of the world's largest pools of investment capital.

The SoftBank Vision Fund, which aims for a launch next year, is targeting $100 billion and is seeded by SoftBank, one of Japan's largest mobile-phone providers and owner of Sprint Corp . It marks an ambitious effort by SoftBank Chief Executive Masayoshi Son to position his company as a leader in emerging technologies such as artificial intelligence and the Internet of Things, in which everyday objects such as thermostats, watches and cars are connected online.

By investing, Apple also would hope to gain insight on emerging technologies, the people familiar with the matter said. Such a move also would reflect a shift in Apple 's investment strategy, which until recently focused on small stakes in young technology companies.

Earlier this year, Apple invested $1 billion in Didi Chuxing Technology Co ., China's homegrown ride-sharing competitor to Uber Technologies Inc . SoftBank is also an investor in Didi. "We're constantly looking on the outside for great talent and great intellectual property," Apple Chief Executive Tim Cook said on a conference call in July.

SoftBank itself is investing at least $25 billion in the fund and is completing an investment of as much as $45 billion from Saudi Arabia's government fund. The sovereign-wealth fund of Abu Dhabi also is considering a large investment, according to people familiar with the matter.

Mr. Son said last week after a meeting with President-elect Donald Trump that he plans to invest as much as $50 billion of the fund in the U.S.

Apple and SoftBank have business ties stretching back nearly a decade. In 2008, the companies struck a deal to make SoftBank the exclusive iPhone seller in Japan. The partnership drove record sales, helping to make SoftBank Japan's third-largest cellular carrier by 2011.

Mr. Son has praised late Apple CEO Steve Jobs , whom he once compared to Leonardo da Vinci, and remains close with Mr. Cook.

SoftBank distributes iPhones and iPads to its roughly 20,000 employees, and Mr. Son has said he rarely touches a personal computer.

Mr. Son also is close to Terry Gou , the head of Foxconn Technology Group , the Taiwan-based iPhone assembler formally known as Hon Hai Precision Industry Co . After his meeting with Mr. Trump last Tuesday, Mr. Son was photographed carrying a piece of paper with Foxconn's logo next to SoftBank's. Earlier this year, SoftBank bought U.K.-based microchip manufacturer ARM Holdings PLC , a supplier of microprocessors for the iPhone.

Rand Merchant, a South African investment group, has snapped up a 30 per cent stake in UK motor insurer Hastings.

Business
Marketing Tech Company Neustar to Be Taken Private by Golden Gate Capital; Neustar values the transaction at $2.9 billion including debt

By Suzanne Vranica and Anne Steele 
457 words
14 December 2016
16:55
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Neustar Inc., an advertising-technology company that provides data and analytics to marketers, on Wednesday said it agreed to be bought by a private investment group led by Golden Gate Capital for about $1.8 billion.

Stockholders will receive $33.50 per share in cash, a 21% premium to Tuesday's closing price and a 45% premium to Neustar's closing stock price on Nov. 11 the day before private-equity firm Golden Gate Capital's disclosure of an equity position in the company.

The deal is valued at $2.9 billion including debt and is expected to close by the end of the third quarter of 2017.

"We believe this transaction will enable us to continue to execute against our strategy and strengthen our market position as a leader in marketing, risk, security and communication solutions," said Lisa Hook, Neustar's president and chief executive, in a statement.

Over the past few years, Neustar has aggressively moved into the marketing technology space with the help of several acquisitions, as it seeks to become a major player in the information services market and compete with the likes of Oracle, Adobe and Salesforce.

Last year, it bought MarketShare, a marketing analytics company that helps advertisers plan and analyze their ad spending and figure out which channels will drive the most sales, "http://www.wsj.com/articles/ad-tech-company-neustar-agrees-to-buy-marketshare-for-450-million-1446757618".

Neustar has also hired several high-profile advertising executives including Steven Wolfe Pereira, who was named chief marketing officer of the firm earlier this year.

Earlier this year, Neustar had announced it was seeking to split its company to separate its number portability business, which helps carriers and businesses switch customer phone numbers between companies, from its information management and marketing businesses. That split is now off the table, according to a person familiar with matter.

Neustar said it has "built a robust market position around unique, hard-to-replicate data sets and the data science that provides authoritative identities, updated in real time."

The transaction is another sign of the consolidation taking place in the marketing and ad tech space. Last month, for example, Adobe acquired video ad buying software company TubeMogul for about $450 million.

Neustar's shares jumped 20% to $33.08 in morning trading. Before the offer was made public, the shares were down 43% from three years ago but were up 12% in the past year.

The terms of the deal to be taken private include a 30-day period for Neustar to solicit alternative proposals.

Pro Private Markets
Conversica Raises $34 Million Series B for Business-Focused AI Chat; Software firm has been developing an artificially intelligent sales assistant and will soon make assistants to tackle other business problems

By Cat Zakrzewski 
435 words
14 December 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

In 2016, chatbots and virtual assistants attracted interest from investors chasing a future where most interactions with businesses will happen through an artificially intelligent machine.

But Conversica has been working toward that vision for six years. The Foster City, Calif.-based company has been selling an artificially intelligent sales assistant, and with new funding in hand, is planning to tackle other business problems.

"Most other companies are really very early on," said Conversica Chief Executive Alex Terry. "It's helpful to see the rest of world coming around to the view Conversica has had for several years."

Mr. Terry said the company has $18 million in annually recurring revenue, while many comparable companies have yet to generate income.

Conversica was expected to announce Wednesday that it has raised an additional $34 million in Series B funding. Providence Strategic Growth Capital Partners LLC led the round, with participation from Toba Capital, Wellington Financial LP and Recruit Holdings Co. Ltd. Existing investor Kennet Partners and founder Ben Brigham also invested.

PSG Managing Director Gopi Vaddi and Toba Capital Partner Wilder Ramsey has joined Conversica's board.

Conversica has used conversational artificial intelligence to develop an assistant that it says can make sales teams more efficient. The technology works with email and SMS texting, and it can help minimize the time salespeople spend sending emails and following up with potential clients.

Mr. Terry said that although there are fears about the impact artificial intelligence will have on jobs, most customers using Conversica have actually hired more salespeople because they are seeing higher closing rates.

He compared the shift to what happened at banks when they launched ATMs. He said that despite fears there would be fewer bank tellers, the technology helped lead to creating more bank locations. Although there weren't as many bank tellers at each location, there was still an overall increase in bank tellers.

"Like any technological evolution, there are short-term changes and long-term changes," he said.

Conversica says customers include IBM and Epson and other technology companies. The company has also worked with auto dealers like Fiat Chrysler.

Mr. Terry said other Conversica assistants are still under development, but the company has already began to try to solve other business problems. For example, Conversica has helped auto companies schedule appointments for tuneups at the dealership.

Business News: Apple , SoftBank Held Fund Talks

By Liz Hoffman and Tripp Mickle 
524 words
13 December 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
B4
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Apple Inc . has held talks about investing in a $100 billion fund being raised by Japanese internet and telecommunications company SoftBank Group Corp ., a move that would put Silicon Valley cash -- and cachet -- into what would be the world's largest technology fund.

Apple and SoftBank have discussed an investment of as much as $1 billion, according to people familiar with the matter, who cautioned that an agreement hasn't been completed. The status of the talks couldn't be learned.

An Apple investment would connect the world's most valuable company -- Apple 's market value is nearly $600 billion -- with one of the world's largest pools of investment capital.

The SoftBank Vision Fund, which aims for a launch next year, is targeting $100 billion and is seeded by SoftBank, one of Japan's largest mobile-phone providers and owner of Sprint Corp . It marks an ambitious effort by SoftBank Chief Executive Masayoshi Son to position his company as a leader in emerging technologies like artificial intelligence and the Internet of Things, in which everyday objects such as thermostats, watches and cars are connected online.

By investing, Apple also would hope to gain insight on emerging technologies, the people familiar with the matter said.

Such a move also would reflect a shift in Apple 's investment strategy, which until recently focused on small stakes in young technology companies. Earlier this year, Apple invested $1 billion in Didi Chuxing Technology Co ., China's homegrown ride-sharing competitor to Uber Technologies Inc . SoftBank is also an investor in Didi.

"We're constantly looking on the outside for great talent and great intellectual property," Apple Chief Executive Tim Cook said on a conference call in July.

SoftBank itself is investing at least $25 billion in the fund and is completing an investment of as much as $45 billion from Saudi Arabia's government fund. The sovereign-wealth fund of Abu Dhabi also is weighing a large investment, according to people familiar with the matter.

Mr. Son said last week after a meeting with President-elect Donald Trump that he plans to invest as much as $50 billion of the fund in the U.S.

Apple and SoftBank have business ties stretching back nearly a decade. In 2008, the companies struck a deal to make SoftBank the exclusive iPhone seller in Japan. The partnership drove record sales, helping to make SoftBank Japan's third-largest cellular carrier by 2011.

Mr. Son has praised late Apple CEO Steve Jobs , whom he once compared to Leonardo da Vinci, and remains close with Mr. Cook.

SoftBank distributes iPhones and iPads to its roughly 20,000 employees.

Mr. Son also is close to Terry Gou , the head of Foxconn Technology Group , the Taiwan-based iPhone assembler formally known as Hon Hai Precision Industry Co . After his meeting with Mr. Trump last Tuesday, Mr. Son was photographed carrying a piece of paper with Foxconn's logo next to SoftBank's. Earlier this year, SoftBank bought U.K.-based microchip manufacturer ARM Holdings PLC , a supplier of microprocessors for the iPhone.

MARKETS & INVESTING
Funds eye alternative data to gain alpha edge; Analysis: Asset management

By Robin Wigglesworth 
892 words
13 December 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
20
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Managers' push to uncover 'tradable signals' using digital fingerprints raises privacy fears

A money manager might once have dispatched a junior grunt to the local Sears department store to gauge how sales were doing. This year a hedge fund could be peering into your emails for hints on how Amazon, the world's largest online retailer, will do in the critical Christmas quarter.

One of the latest technological weapons deployed by the most sophisticated asset managers desperate for an investment edge involves doing just that, albeit in a roundabout way.

There are technology firms that offer companies the ability to scan incoming emails. Software "cookies" can also look for emails sent from the likes of Amazon, Walmart or Best Buy, check for sales receipts and then log that data in a database. That data then lands in the lap of hedge funds via a third-party vendor.

Scouring emails for such nuggets of information is just the tip of the " alternative data " iceberg. Almost everything we do leaves a digital fingerprint, which can be scraped, aggregated and sold to investment firms looking for tradable signals, or to use the jargon - market-beating "alpha".

"The buyside is struggling more and more to find alpha, but data is alpha, and more and more of it is becoming available every day," says Tammer Kamel, co-founder and chief executive of Quandl, a US alternative data provider. "The demand is there, the supply is now there as well, and we want to sit in the middle of this."

Supply is certainly swelling. The world's annual data generation will more than double each year for the foreseeable future, and reach 44 zettabytes (trillions of gigabytes) by 2020, according to IDC .

Luckily, computing power has kept pace. Today's high-end Nvidia video cards are so powerful they would have been classified as the world's most powerful supercomputer before 2002, Goldman Sachs analysts noted.

Coupled with huge strides in artificial intelligence - particularly in areas such as "deep learning" - this is allowing investment groups to mine enormous data sets to unearth insights they hope will keep their returns ahead of rivals.

Take scanned email receipts. Earlier this autumn, GoPro, the action camera maker loved by daredevils, reported a terrible quarter of sinking sales , sending its shares tumbling. Yet by using a database of electronic receipts extracted from more than 3m email inboxes, Quandl says it could see ahead of time that revenues would disappoint.

The potential of alternative data has long been known to hedge funds, but traditional asset managers, looking for ways to improve performance, are now employing it too. For example, UK asset manager Schroders has set up a "Data Insights" team to "enhance and complement the existing skills of our fund managers and analysts".

"Global trends in digitalisation, social media, open data and technology are all creating vast streams of alternative data that are often highly unstructured and extremely obscure. However, they contain valuable and often unique insights," the investment group's latest annual report said.

The investment industry's push into alternative data raises some awkward questions. First, some money managers are concerned the personal data are not always scrubbed completely from the data sets they are sold. The majority of vendors do a good job, but standards are far from uniform, they say.

"We talk about it all the time," said Wesley Chan, director of stock selection research at Acadian Asset Management. "On one hand, we're excited about the potential; on the other, we're racing to delete apps on our phone. The data vendors claim to anonymise the data through aggregation, but it doesn't always work perfectly."

Mr Kamel stresses Quandl , one of the companies with a better reputation, is obsessed about scrubbing off personal information and says there is a "healthy paranoia" among the company's clients about the issue.

"No one wants to be on the wrong side of this," he says. "We make it our mission to mitigate 100 per cent of those risks. One fiasco in this space and the consequences for a fund would be dire."

Scraping, selling and buying data are perfectly legal in most countries, as long as safeguards are taken. Even so, Mr Chan warns mishaps are probable. "Six months ago I would have said we just have to live with it, nothing is private any more anyway. But at some point there will have to be some sort of bill of rights to control it," he says. "This will continue until there's a scandal, which is likely to happen."

If fund managers begin to lean more on alternative data to help make investment decisions, some believe it ultimately has the potential to dislodge quarterly corporate earnings reports and monthly economic data from the centre of investors' radars.

So far big data can only offer clues on what these market-moving releases will show. But in time, it is conceivable that our digital fingerprints will provide an accurate, instantaneous gauge that will render traditional investment information obsolete.

"As this perfect storm of technological sophistication comes together, we have never been closer to charting out a real-time map of consumer behaviour in both the real and digital world," Tabb Group, a research firm, notes.

The Political Fallout From Wells Fargo's Latest Penalty; Democrats seize on latest slap at Wells Fargo to defend postcrisis rules

By Donna Borak and Emily Glazer 
519 words
13 December 2016
23:42
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Democrats seized on "http://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-regulators-sanction-wells-fargo-declaring-living-will-deficiencies-1481664744" at Wells Fargo & Co. to defend postcrisis rules against an anticipated attack from President-elect Donald Trump.

The news "is a reminder that Wall Street reform is working to rein in the megabanks that crashed our economy and got bailed out by taxpayers," Sen. Sherrod Brown of Ohio, the top Democrat on the Senate Banking Committee, said in a statement. "Even one too big to fail bank is too many, and we need watchdogs that will continue to impose tough rules and strong penalties to make banks simpler and safer, not the opposite."

Wells Fargo said in a statement that while it is disappointed by the regulators' latest decision it will work closely with the agencies to better understand their concerns and took early regulator feedback "very seriously and took several steps to address it," including creating a special office assigned to deal with the living wills process.

Some policy analysts said they expect Tuesday's announcement by the Federal Reserve and Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. to stoke further pressure from some politicians to use this process to break up the biggest banks repeating arguments made in recent months that Wells Fargo is "too complex to manage."

"I fully expect Democrats to begin pounding the table once again on big bank break up bills and even Glass-Steagall, if only to call President-elect Donald Trump out on his campaign proposal" to reinstate the Depression-era law that separates commercial banking from investment activities, said Issac Boltansky, an analyst for Compass Point Research & Trading LLC, an investment firm.

Even though Tuesday's decision was unrelated to the earlier scandal, "this appears to be a clear case where the legal and reputational risk of one politically potent problem creates franchise-value risk for a large bank," said Karen Shaw Petrou, managing partner at policy-analysis firm Federal Financial Analytics Inc.

Wells Fargo's failure of the living wills regulatory test is "the latest in a series of embarrassments for Wells, which was once viewed as the Main Street good guy in contrast with its slicker Wall Street competitors," banking analyst Ian Katz wrote in a Tuesday evening note. Mr. Katz of Capital Alpha Partners wrote that while the "pain" from the sales tactics scandal this fall had largely slowed down in headlines, recent reported actions by other agencies indicate "regulators and lawmakers haven't let up."

Still, the penalties "could have been worse," wrote Jaret Seiberg, an analyst with Cowen and Co. He said that "this could have been far more consequential if Hillary Clinton had won the White House," noting that she had "said she could demand that any bank that failed its living will submission be broken up." Mr. Trump, he added "did not make a similar pledge, which suggests the risk of a structural change is minimal."

Pro Private Markets
Startups Cautiously Prep for Health-Care Law Change; Some customers are expected to hold off on new investments until there is more clarity to Trump's promised revamp of Obamacare

By Yuliya Chernova and Brian Gormley 
978 words
13 December 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Count Paul Magelli among the entrepreneurs already preparing for a repeal of the federal health-care law.

President-elect Donald Trump has promised to scrap the Affordable Care Act, which affects companies throughout health care. That has startups readying for a period of uncertainty about the law's replacement and the possibility that some customers will hold off on new investments until there is more clarity.

Mr. Magelli, chief executive of Apervita Inc., a Chicago-based supplier of data analytics to health-care providers, has been making more customer calls since the election to see if clients' budgets and timelines are changing.

"We are literally doing a bottom-up review and re-planning around likely time scales for those projects," Mr. Magelli said.

He added that he expects potential customers like the Department of Veterans Affairs, which has been developing a digital-health initiative, will be among those whose plans change.

The health-care law has encouraged the use of medical products and services by widening health-insurance coverage and other provisions. Mr. Trump's choice of Affordable Care Act critic Tom Price (R., Ga.) to serve as secretary of the Health and Human Services Department makes the likelihood of major changes even higher.

Expecting a shift, health systems, self-insured employers and other groups that buy medical technologies will ask if specific purchases will help them today and in a post-repeal era, said Mohamad Makhzoumi, general partner of New Enterprise Associates. They probably won't freeze new purchases but startups should expect longer sales cycles, he said.

"It's fair to say we are taking a wait-and-see approach and have not made any decisions to change our 2017 plans at this time," said Bryce Williams, vice president at Blue Shield of California, a nonprofit health plan.

Businesses that have passed the startup phase and are looking to land large sales could face delays because of health systems evaluating major capital investments more carefully, said Sarah Krevans, CEO of Sutter Health, a health system in Northern California. But technologies that improve outcomes and delight patients are sure to attract buyers.

"As a not-for-profit system, we're going to be interested in that in any environment," Ms. Krevans said.

In case purchasing behaviors do change, startups like New York-based Wellth Inc. are diversifying. Wellth, whose app helps chronically ill patients comply with their treatment plan, began by piloting with university hospitals. But it is now making a push to sell its services to life insurance and pharmaceutical companies as well.

Wellth Chief Executive Matt Loper recently met with drugmaker Novo Nordisk A/S to discuss how his technology could increase adherence to medication regimens.

"That's a completely new segment for us," Mr. Loper said. "We came up with the strategy right after the election."

A repeal of the health-care law would have varied effects on venture-backed medical insurers such as Oscar Insurance Corp., which is already diversifying its business. Oscar formed after the law's passage to sell insurance to consumers through exchanges, but it has struggled to meet revenue goals, The Wall Street Journal has reported.

In August, $2.7 billion-valued Oscar said it would withdraw from individual insurance markets in Dallas-Fort Worth and New Jersey. Should the Affordable Care Act, also known as Obamacare, be repealed, there would be fewer compelling reasons for healthier people to buy insurance on health exchanges, which has potential to hurt a company like Oscar even more.

But the startup could benefit if the law were revamped to eliminate caps on insurance profits, which could allow the company to make more money down the line. Should the law be changed to lift requirements on what health plans must offer, that could also enable Oscar to offer more types of plans and tailor them to specific groups.

New York-based Oscar, co-founded by Joshua Kushner, whose brother is Trump son-in-law Jared Kushner, is also expanding by setting up a business to sell health plans to corporations.

Many venture capitalists and entrepreneurs say their technologies will find large markets regardless of regulatory changes under consideration.

"Costs have to be lowered. Outcomes have to be improved and clearly measurable," said Bill Geary, general partner of Flare Capital Partners. "The shift from fee-for-service to value-based care is unstoppable it's a train that has left the station."

Phoenix-based startup Solera Health Inc., which matches consumers to diabetes-prevention programs, likely wouldn't be threatened by a repeal of the law, founder and CEO Brenda Schmidt said.

"Preventing diabetes is a more cost-effective way of addressing health-care costs than waiting until someone is diabetic," Ms. Schmidt said.

Insurers recognize this and will likely support effective prevention programs whether the federal government requires them to or not, she said.

For many in the industry, periods of change pose targets for entrepreneurs who spot ways to profit from new rules and regulations.

Repealing Obamacare would "reset the entire industry," said George Kalogeropoulos, CEO of San Francisco-based HealthSherpa, which helps consumers find medical insurance. "We get the opportunity to go big."

When Obamacare was initially being implemented, various states and insurers spent billions of dollars building software that was compliant with the law, he said. HealthSherpa, founded in 2013, hadn't launched at the time. Now it has relationships with many insurers and sees a potential overhaul as an opening.

All insurers, said Mr. Kalogeropoulos, "will be looking for  Trumpcare' software."

COMPANIES
Crowdsourced DIY algo funds swell coffers; Financials

By Robin Wigglesworth, US markets editor 
392 words
12 December 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
17
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Two "crowdsourced" hedge funds that harness the work of amateur algorithmic traders have raised more money from investors, underlining the swelling interest in the latest development in quantitative finance.

Silicon Valley-based Numerai and Quantiacs collect submissions from freelance data scientists, computer engineers, physicists and mathematicians by offering regular prizes for the best stock market predictions or automated trading strategies. Although many hedge fund veterans scoff at the chances of DIY algo traders succeeding over time, some investors are taken with the potential of this model.

Numerai has raised $6m at a $30m valuation from a batch of investors led by Union Square Ventures, a venture capital firm that also invested in Twitter, Zynga and Tumblr. Howard Morgan , one of the founders of Renaissance Technologies, reinvested after joining the initial fundraising earlier this year, which was done at a $10m valuation.

The future of hedge funds is going to be quantitative and based on artificial intelligence rather than traditional fundamental analysis, argues Richard Craib , Numerai's founder.

Numerai's members download encrypted data and use AI techniques like "deep learning" to detect patterns, and then submit predictions to the company, which then uses them for its hedge fund. There are now 7,500 participants registered on its platform and submitting predictions, up from 4,500 three months ago, and the best ones win prize money. The money raised will be ploughed mainly into new data for computer scientists to experiment with.

"We were intrigued by the fact that Numerai is at its core about a community of scientists, that just happens to have a hedge fund bolted on to it," said Andy Weissman, a partner at USV.

Quantiacs' model focuses more on being a platform for computer scientists to submit fully-formed trading algorithms, which outside investors invest in individually. Quantiacs has raised "single-digit" millions of dollars since opening up to external investors this autumn, and is in talks with a handful more investors at the moment, according to its founder, Martin Froehler .

"We want to democratise quantitative finance, opening up to everyone what has only been reserved to the elites," he said.

Last month Quantopian, one of the more high-profile DIY quant platforms, raised $25m from a clutch of venture capitalists led by Andreessen Horowitz.

COMPANIES
 Big data  sellers fail to hide delicate details; Technology

By Robin Wigglesworth in New York 
483 words
12 December 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
20
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Hedge funds claim vendors are not adequately erasing personal information

The " alternative data " industry, which sells information such as app downloads and credit card purchases to investment groups, is failing to adequately erase personal details before sharing the material, according to several hedge funds.

The rapidly growing world of big data is seen as an increasingly attractive source of information for asset managers seeking a vital investment edge, with data providers selling everything from social media chatter and emailed receipts to federal lobbying information and even satellite images from space.

But several hedge funds say some vendors are selling information that still contains sensitive personal details that could be used to identify individuals.

"The vendors claim to strip out all the personal information, but we occasionally find phone numbers, zip codes and so on," said Matthew Granade, chief market intelligence officer at Steven Cohen's Point72.

"It's a big enough deal that we have a couple of full-time tech people wash the data ourselves."

The head of another big hedge fund said that even when personal information had been scrubbed from a data set, it was far too easy to restore.

"We were shocked at how easy it was to de-anonymise the data," he said.

"It took one of my analysts 30 minutes to discover someone who was probably having an affair."

Sophisticated algorithms such as " machine learning " allow money managers to sift through enormous data sets for profitable patterns.

Tabb Group, a consultancy, estimates that in the US alone, spending on big data will double in the next five years to $400m, while CB Insights, a data provider, has counted at least 30 start-ups in the field.

But the sensitivity of some of the information being offered has raised concerns.

Robert Schoshinski, assistant director in the Federal Trade Commission's division of privacy and identity protection, said the issue was "on the FTC's radar" but he refused to say if there were any open investigations into misuse of data.

Tammer Kamel, chief executive of Quandl , a well reputed alternative data vendor, said that his company was "super zealous" about scrubbing any personal information out of its aggregated data.

"No one wants to be on the wrong side of this," he said.

Another hedge fund manager pointed out that if there were legal issues, the litigation axe would be more likely to fall on them than the data vendors.

"We are incredibly careful about licensing and privacy issues because when things go wrong legally, the plaintiffs go after the people with the money," he said.

But there is no overarching US privacy law to protect consumers, with standards set individually by different states, industries and even companies, according to Albert Gidari, director of privacy at the Stanford Center for Internet and Society .

'Alternative data' sellers fail to remove personal information, say hedge funds

FT REPORT - FUND MANAGEMENT: INDEX INVESTING
Bond market sell-off fails to upset fixed income ETFs

By Paul Amery 
899 words
12 December 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
Surveys FID1
05
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Credit Regulators are trying to reduce conflicts of interest among indexers, reports Paul Amery

There may have been a mass sell-off of US bonds since Donald Trump's election as president, but the exchange traded funds tracking global debt markets are on course for a record year.

Between July and November yields on 10-year US Treasury notes - which move inversely to prices - rose by a full percentage point as investors sold government bonds. Yet the use of fixed income in the fast-growing ETF market has accelerated rapidly in 2016. Assets in ETFs tracking bond indices reached $594bn worldwide by November, a tenfold increase since 2007, according to BlackRock. Equity ETF sales total $186bn so far this year, while bond ETFs have attracted a record $109bn of inflows, despite representing only a fifth of the global ETF market by assets.

"Fixed income ETFs are increasingly being used as a gateway to the overall bond markets, especially in corporate bonds and especially in the US," says Sophia Dancygier, managing director at index provider IHS Markit.

But the indices that underlie bond ETFs differ from those tracked by equity funds. Compared to equity indices, fixed income indices are more complex, requiring more individual data on prices and features such as bond call dates and seniority.

"In fixed income, most securities don't trade daily and you are forced to use an evaluation process to determine prices," says Matt Tucker, head of fixed income strategy at BlackRock's ETF unit iShares. "A single security can have three different prices attributed to it by three different index firms, all of which may be plausible. All this makes indexing a much greater challenge."

Recent regulatory changes have shaken up the business of providing benchmarks. The Libor and foreign exchange rate-rigging scandals have led to a wholesale review of the framework for index production. Iosco, the international securities regulator, issued a new set of principles for benchmarks in 2013. Meanwhile, new EU benchmark regulations will come into force in January 2018.

Both initiatives are designed to reduce conflicts of interest at index providers and, in practical terms, to separate index management and administration from trading.

In response to the heavier compliance burden, several banks have exited the fixed income index business in the past two years, most notably Barclays, which sold its benchmarking unit to Bloomberg for 520m last December. Analysts expect more banks to sell their index units.

Yet other banks, such as Citi, Bank of America Merrill Lynch, JPMorgan and Nomura, continue to produce and disseminate bond indices.

Another important regulatory development is Europe's updated Markets in Financial Instruments Directive (Mifid II), due to take effect in 2018.

One of Mifid II's aims is to increase transparency in fixed income trading by requiring greater price disclosure before and after trades. In parallel, it also aims to push more bond trades on to central trading venues. The data collected at these venues are expected to prove valuable for index designers.

Banks that stay in the fixed income index business will be under pressure to end the practice of offering data at low cost or even for free to clients to win business due to the potential for conflicts of interest.

"We have to charge independently for the indices and the services we provide, independently from what the trading desk might or might not receive in terms of execution flow," says Arom Pathammavong, head of Citi's fixed income index business.

Srikant Dash, formerly head of Bloomberg's index business and now an independent adviser and board member, notes that being offered index data is only one of the services bond investors require from information providers.

"Compared to equity indexing, which is a brand-based business, the fixed income index business is a three-legged stool," he says. "One part is the index itself, the second is pricing and the third is analytics. They all have to work together."

Financial technology provider Intercontinental Exchange (ICE) is making a push into bond indexing. This year it launched indices on US government and corporate bonds.

Meanwhile, index providers that have traditionally seemed focused on equity benchmarks are investing heavily in their fixed income operations. In November London Stock Exchange Group bought data and analytics provider Mergent, increasing its capabilities in the corporate bond market.

"Customers are clamouring for more fixed income index providers, especially independent firms," says Waqas Samad, head of LSE's fixed income indices.

In October, provider S&P Dow Jones Indices teamed up with fixed income trading platform MarketAxess to launch US corporate bond indices. "We're using the data from the platform to help identify the most liquid corporate bonds for the index selection process," says James Rieger, global head of fixed income at S&P Dow Jones Indices.

Despite increased competition among benchmark providers, some bond index users say they now face sharply rising costs as a result of the indexing boom.

"The fixed income index firms have gained substantial pricing power," says an executive at the Information Provider User Group, a non-profit body representing the purchasers of market data. "The cost of one fixed income index product data licence we've looked at has risen fourfold since 2008, from $50,000 a year to $200,000."

Pro Private Markets
Dutch Firm Tops Private-Equity Performance Rankings

By Jessica Davies 
728 words
12 December 2016
16:53
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

If you're looking for more bang for your buck, the midmarket has traditionally been the place to find it.

It is harder to double your money on 20 billion ($25.32 billion) than 100 million, said Oliver Gottschalg, the founder and head of private-equity analytics firm Peracs, who for the past seven years has compiled an annual performance ranking of the top private-equity funds in Europe and the U.S. based on how much money they have returned to investors over a decade.

That balance is slowly shifting, however, as the magnifying glass focuses on performance through the global financial crisis of 2008. Four out of 10 of the top performers in this year's ranking were in the large-cap space, up from two last year, following a year in which strong historical performance enabled the asset class to deliver record distributions to investors of $443 billion, up from $45 billion in 2003, according to data provider Preqin Ltd.

Resilience

The highest-ranking firms in 2016 were those that demonstrated resilience through turbulent times, as the influence of more recent vintages (that matured past the performance lag known as the J-curve) came to the fore. Big buyout shops Apollo Global Management LLC, Advent International Corp., Clayton Dubilier & Rice and Hellman & Friedman all featured in the top 10 best-performing firms, showing they weathered the crisis as safe places to put investor money.

For the first time in five years, a European firm rose to the top of the leaderboard, hosting a smorgasbord of boom-era vintages mixed with superior unripened funds. Dutch midmarket firm Waterland Private Equity Investments knocked U.S. middleweight Vista Equity Partners off the top spot, which the San Francisco firm had held for last two years.

Like a fine Gouda,  ¬4 billion ($4.24 billion) firm Waterland Private Equity unboxed the value of a 2008 fund that has ripened since the global financial crisis and emerged full of flavor despite the rotten performance of many funds during the period. Its younger fund of 2011 also had the nose on runner-up Vista's latest fund from the period, pushing the Dutch firm to top place, up from third last year.

If you were an investor in one of Waterland's funds, you would have made nearly twice the amount of money as you would have done if you had invested in the average private-equity firm over the past decade.

However, overall performance is shrinking, as last year's top performer Vista Equity performed better against the industry average than the top ranking firms did this year. The difference between this year's ranking and the 2015 list is that some of the older funds from pre-2003 wouldn't have been considered and newer funds from 2011 would have been included.

Waterland took the top spot because its 2008 fund, which raised  ¬800 million within the three months following March 2008, was able to "navigate the crisis" much better than other funds, said Mr. Gottschalg. Another factor was the strong performance of its 2011 vehicle, which he said fared better than Vista's fund of the same vintage. According to data provider Preqin's 2016 list of consistent performers in alternative assets, four of Waterland's funds had a top-quartile ranking.

Realizations

The Gottschalg ranking looked at Waterland's Funds 2, 3, 4 and 5. The Dutch firm has made two significant exits over the past couple of years, including the sale of a majority stake in Swiss nursing home operator Seniocare AG to strategic investor Tertianum in August 2015; and the sale of a majority stake in Belgian air conditioning company P. Lemmens Co. in mid-2014.

"They all have their magic, but all are stellar at what they're doing," Mr. Gottschalg said.

Another firm that ranked highly was New York firm Clayton Dubilier & Rice, which has been consistently rising in the ranking, scoring third place in the 2016 list, according to Mr. Gottschalg. "They've been able to sell at high rates over the past year or two," he said.

Swedish firm Altor Equity Partners, which Preqin said in the autumn was one of the most consistently top-performing fund manager by implementation skill, meanwhile came fourth on the Gottschalg ranking.

Pro Private Markets
Solvvy's Machine Learning  Helps Answer Customer Support Questions; Software ingests prior interactions with customers from support forums, private chats

By Tomio Geron 
529 words
12 December 2016
12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

For companies managing customer service and other support, it's often a repetitive process of answering many of the same questions over and over again.

Solvvy, a new startup, is using machine learning to answer many of these questions automatically.

Now the Palo Alto, Calif.-based company has $4.5 million in seed funding to back its ambitions, led by True Ventures. Pear Ventures, Carnegie Mellon University and Signatures Capital participated, along with Investment Group of Santa Barbara, Dan Rose and Hamid Barkhordar.

The company's technology is based on research conducted by two of the co-founders, Mehdi Samadi and Justin Betteridge, who were Ph.D. students in artificial intelligence at Carnegie Mellon University.

Customers include Upwork, DoubleDutch, TaskRabbit and Amazon's Alexa.com.

When clients sign up with Solvvy, the software ingests prior interactions with customers from support forums, private chats with support or other forms. Solvvy says it differs from other services such as chat bots because it uses artificial intelligence and natural language processing to answer questions even if they're long and complicated or phrased in different ways.

Then the software can answer questions immediately from customers using natural language processing--to answer questions even if they're misspelled or phrased in different ways. This eliminates the wait time for customers. If a question deals with a specific customer issue that needs a human, it's routed to a person.

Solvvy integrates with a client's existing customer support tool, from companies such as such as Zendesk, Salesforce, Oracle or Atlassian--and looks virtually the same once installed.

For customer service representatives, Solvvy enables them to focus on more important customer issues, said Solvvy Chief Executive Mahesh Ram.

The software learns from interactions with customers to determine if data is missing and questions aren't being answered. "It's learning from both sides," said Mr. Ram. "It helps our algorithm get smarter, learning which can't get answered today, but ought to be. So we'd tell the client,  You don't have these answers in your knowledge base. Perhaps you ought to create this new knowledge.' "

Eventually, Solvvy could potentially hook into other software, such as customer-relationship management tools, to automatically take actions for a customer. "We're able to build technology in the near future to not just provide information but that can actually map a question to an action," Mr. Samadi said. "That's really the future we see."

Solvvy reduces the number of tickets that its customer service agents get by about 10% to 15% by automatically answering them, Mr. Ram said.

"We see a lot of companies that say they're  AI for X,' but they're really just veneers," said Puneet Agarwal, partner at True Ventures and Solvvy board member. "But when we met Justin, Mehdi and Mahesh we saw they had real depth around their technology. It wasn't a veneer it's really core to what they're doing."

Mr. Flint joins James Currier, Gigi Levy Weiss and Stan Chudnovsky as a managing partner in the accelerator and venture fund. The former Trulia chief executive, like the others on the team, has spent his career working on software-related startups.

The firm is set up so each partner can jump in to work with any of the companies at any time, and they take no board seats with investments. The intention is to bring the wisdom of a tight-knit team of software entrepreneurs to each company.

"We're four successful software CEOs, and we're all now doing a startup together," said Mr. Currier of the firm's approach.

NFX Guild plans to raise a second fund of about $100 million, according to people familiar with the matter.

The Guild accelerator is an invitation-only program for classes of 20 to 25 startups. Entrepreneurs learn a core of fundamentals to build their businesses. The program includes business strategies for tapping into network effects, sales and growth, and it helps with equity programs and to find capital connections.

The firm is also developing its own software to help Guild members to fundraise, manage mentors, recruit employees, share private and public data, and connect with each other on building their businesses.

"We're going to apply software to everything we do," Mr. Currier said of the firm's tool set under development.

Amid a crush of capital-chasing startup founders, NFX Guild's growth-minded accelerator and member-focused software differentiate the firm, Mr. Currier said.

If that sounds familiar, it is.
