Whether NFX Guild can capture a subset of startups for its smaller network with an exclusive aura remains to be seen. Its pitch is that it is a smaller, more intimate affair offering personalized focus on companies.

NFX Guild, which launched in April 2015, runs a three-month accelerator program intended to jump-start growth in seed to post-Series A companies. NFX Guild has been investing out of a $15 million fund, and it hasn't previously disclosed its capital.

Investments from NFX Guild range $40,000 to $120,000 for equity in startups, depending on the stage of the company's development.

The firm looks for investments in companies that work with augmented or virtual reality, artificial intelligence, Internet of Things, 3-D printing, blockchain 2.0, robotics and synthetic biology.

"NFX Guild is unique and is applying software and network effects to the ecosystem," said Mr. Flint, who recently began working at the firm.

The partners declined to disclose the compensation economics of the firm for the four-person team.

Limited partners who invested in NFX Guild's first fund include Greylock Partners, Shasta Ventures and Mayfield Fund.

Mr. Levy Weiss is based in Herzliya, Israel, where he is offering advice to area entrepreneurs. He makes frequent trips to the U.S. and selects startup founders for the NFX Guild accelerator program in the San Francisco Bay Area.

Investing partner Mr. Chudnovsky is also the product chief for Messaging at Facebook and is a limited partner in the fund.

The NFX Guild has a conference and demo day at the Computer History Museum in Mountain View, Calif., on March 2 for its current class and investors to attend.

By Tomio Geron

12:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Salespeople today work from their mobile devices more than ever before, but most customer relationship management applications were built during the web era.

Startup Tact enables salespeople to do their work on a mobile device, using artificial intelligence to assist in various tasks.

Tact has raised $15 million in Series B financing led by Upfront Ventures. New investor Microsoft Ventures participated, along with existing investors Accel Partners and Redpoint Ventures. The company has raised about $30 million in total.

Tact connects to a salesperson's Salesforce and LinkedIn accounts, as well as email and calendar.

Using Tact, after a sales meeting or call, salespeople get an automated mobile notification to record a voice note on the app, with next steps or follow up, and Tact routes the notes to Salesforce or other apps. Using Tact quickly can raise the likelihood of salespeople putting data into apps like Salesforce, said Chuck Ganapathi, founder and chief executive of Tact.

"(Salespeople) need mobile applications designed for mobile first," said Mark Suster, managing partner at Upfront, who is joining Tact's board. "They need voice interaction in cars. They don't want to have to log into a heavy database."

Salespeople can also ask the app a question, such as "What's my forecast this quarter?" and Tact will pull up the data on the app. They can also communicate with Tact via text by asking, "Find Jerry Smith on LinkedIn," and Tact will ask if they want to connect on LinkedIn.

While there are several personal assistant apps for consumer such as X.ai, Tact focuses on business, which makes it easier to focus on helping with the tasks that workers need to get done, said Mr. Ganapathi, who previously worked at Salesforce and Siebel.

"I wouldn't be surprised if bots and AI have a lot more success in the enterprise for that reason," he said. "You know the objectives of the human very clearly."

U.S. News: Rising Mortgage Rates Could Threaten Housing Demand

By Laura Kusisto 
707 words
12 December 2016
The Wall Street Journal (Europe Edition)
WSJE
A8
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Rising interest rates pose a dilemma for people who love their mortgage more than they hate their house.

A sustained period of rising rates could freeze homeowners with rock-bottom mortgages who otherwise might want to trade up for bigger or better properties.

Such situations, which economists call "rate lock," could weigh on housing demand in 2017, economists said.

A seven-year run of historically low mortgage rates has encouraged home buying, helping increase prices sharply after the housing crash. The S&P CoreLogic Case-Shiller U.S. National Home Price Index reached a record in September.

But mortgage rates have jumped more than half a percentage point since the election, and economists are bracing for higher rates in 2017.

Rising rates make mortgage payments more expensive. That gives homeowners incentive to remain in place rather than trade up, said Nela Richardson, chief economist at real-estate brokerage firm Redfin.

"It doesn't take much to turn off the faucet in this market because inventory is so low and prices have gone up so quickly," she said.

The average rate for a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage rose to 4.13% last Thursday, according to mortgage company Freddie Mac, up from 3.54% before the election. That was the highest level since October 2014.

The latest bump boosts the monthly cost of owning the typical U.S. home by more than $70 a month, or about $26,000 over the life of a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage, according to Black Knight Financial Services, a mortgage and real estate technology and data provider.

"I suspect it's already having an impact at the margin. Another half a percent and the impact will be substantial," said Lou Barnes, a capital markets analyst at Premier Mortgage Group, based in Boulder, Colo. Homeowners in pricey coastal markets, where affordability is already strained, could feel the pinch sooner. In California, where the median-priced home is closer to $500,000, homeowners already are looking at paying about $170 more a month based on the recent rate rise.

According to Zillow, a typical household in Los Angeles already spends 38% of its income on mortgage payments -- greater than the recommended 30%.

In all, about 66% of U.S. homeowners with mortgages have rates of less than 4.5%, according to real estate analytics firm CoreLogic Inc. Economists said rates would likely need to rise above 5% for a large number of homeowners across the U.S. to face the rate lock dilemma.

As the economy strengthens, the U.S. Federal Reserve is expected to raise short-term interest rates when it meets this month. Mortgage rates are tied more closely to Treasury bond yields, which also tend to rise during periods of inflation.

Many economists expect mortgage rates to continue rising gradually from here, though such predictions have been wrong before.

Rates have risen because the economy is strengthening and investors are betting that tax cuts and increased government spending on infrastructure will spur more growth. Higher wage growth could offset the effect of higher mortgage rates.

But the fact that so many homeowners enjoy such low rates could also prove an economic brake, creating a disincentive for homeowners to move to a new city in pursuit of a new job if it means their mortgage might be more expensive, said David Berson, chief economist at Nationwide Insurance.

Recent history suggests the impact of rising rates can be swift and substantial. In 2013 mortgage rates surged to 4.5% from 3.6% as investors anticipated the Federal Reserve would pull back from its bond-buying program. The pace of sales of previously owned homes declined 8% from July to December of that year, according to the National Association of Realtors.

The rate of home-price rises around that time was cut essentially in half, to 5% from 9%, according to Black Knight.

"If 2013 is any guide, we could expect to see a slowdown in [price] appreciation," said Ben Graboske, vice president of Black Knight's data and analytics division. "We still think house prices will grow, just more meekly."

The average rate for a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage rose to 4.13% last Thursday, according to mortgage company Freddie Mac, up from 3.54% before the election. That was the highest level since October 2014.

The latest bump boosts the monthly cost of owning the typical U.S. home by more than $70 a month, or about $26,000 over the life of a 30-year fixed-rate mortgage, according to Black Knight Financial Services, a mortgage and real estate technology and data provider.

"I suspect it's already having an impact at the margin. Another half a percent and the impact will be substantial," said Lou Barnes, a capital markets analyst at Boulder-based Premier Mortgage Group.

Homeowners in pricey coastal markets, where affordability is already strained, could feel the pinch sooner. In California, where the median-priced home is closer to $500,000, homeowners already are looking at paying about $170 more a month based on the recent rate rise.

According to Zillow, a typical household in Los Angeles already spends 38% of its income on mortgage payments greater than the recommended 30%. In the Bay Area, about 5% fewer households will fall under the recommended 33% debt-to-income ratio to afford a $1 million mortgage if interest rates rise from 4% to 5%, according to Selma Hepp, chief economist at Pacific Union International, a real-estate firm.

In all, about 66% of U.S. homeowners with mortgages have rates of less than 4.5%, according to real estate analytics firm CoreLogic Inc. Economists said rates would likely need to rise above 5% for a large number of homeowners across the U.S. to face the rate lock dilemma.

As the economy strengthens, the U.S. Federal Reserve is expected to raise short-term interest rates when it meets this month. Mortgage rates are tied more closely to Treasury bond yields, which also tend to rise during periods of inflation.

Many economists expect mortgage rates to continue rising gradually from here, though such predictions have been wrong before.

Rates have risen because the economy is strengthening and investors are betting that tax cuts and increased government spending on infrastructure will spur more growth. Higher wage growth could offset the effect of higher mortgage rates.

But the fact that so many homeowners enjoy such low rates could also prove an economic brake, creating a disincentive for homeowners to move to a new city in pursuit of a new job if it means their mortgage might be more expensive, said David Berson, chief economist at Nationwide Insurance.

Recent history suggests the impact of rising rates can be swift and substantial. In 2013 mortgage rates surged to 4.5% from 3.6% as investors anticipated the Federal Reserve would pull back from its bond-buying program. The pace of sales of previously owned homes declined 8% from July to December of that year, according to the National Association of Realtors.

The rate of home-price increases around that time was cut essentially in half, to 5% from 9%, according to Black Knight.

"If 2013 is any guide, we could expect to see a slowdown in [price] appreciation," said Ben Graboske, vice president of Black Knight's data and analytics division. "We still think house prices will grow, just more meekly."

Jeanette Mateo, a 41-year-old health-care project coordinator in Sacramento, Calif., said if rates rise it could affect her decision to sell. She and her partner already agreed once to sell their house but pulled out of the deal "10 or 15 minutes later" because they were nervous about being able to find another one they could afford.

They pay about $900 a month on their mortgage, which they refinanced in 2009 when rates were around 5%. Now they are eyeing a home in a new development, which would give them a bigger kitchen and an extra garage.

Ms. Mateo said if rates rise significantly they might not be able to afford to trade up, and they worry that it would be more difficult to sell if some buyers are scared off.

Hedge funds begin to venture beyond costly Mayfair enclave

Lindsay Fortado and Judith Evans 
1,003 words
10 December 2016
05:03
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Hedge funds have become as synonymous with Mayfair as bespoke tailoring and luxury shopping in recent decades. But times are changing.

Some of the biggest names in the industry are leaving the confines of the narrow grid of streets sandwiched between Oxford and Regent Streets, Piccadilly and Hyde Park, put off by sky-high rents and searching for larger office space as the industry matures.

With returns dwindling at some hedge funds, and investors pushing back over fee structures, firms are also under pressure to cut costs elsewhere. Lavishly ostentatious offices that made sense in the boomtime are no longer a priority.

It once made sense for hedge funds to be based in Mayfair and St James s. Their investor base was initially high-net worth individuals, many of whom lived in the area and would meet in one of the Michelin-starred restaurants. But the money flowing into hedge funds has become more institutional, with pension funds, endowments, fund of funds and sovereign wealth funds dominating the investor list.

Larger hedge fund firms, including most recently Sir Michael Hintze s CQS, Marshall Wace and Systematica, have all found bases outside Mayfair, while some of the biggest groups, including Winton Capital and Man Group s AHL funds, have always been elsewhere. Winton, a $32bn quantitative investing firm, looked for office space in Mayfair before opting to build new headquarters in Hammersmith several years ago because of the cost.

Andrew Barnes, head of tenant representation for the West End at JLL, the property advisers, says:  What we have seen is a real desire not to pay top dollar any more. Pre the financial crisis, hedge funds were always the rent setters and they paid whatever it took to get the best space. 

 There was a kind of one-upmanship   who drives the nicest car, who s got the nicest office. That changed in 2008 in a big way. People are far more cost-conscious and concerned about the profile and the image they are portraying to their clients and investors. 

Mr Barnes says hedge funds were more comfortable looking for offices outside the traditional locations around Berkeley Square or St James s Square such as Knightsbridge, or areas north of Oxford Street such as Portman Square and Cavendish Square.

An overhaul of business rates from 2017 will see buildings in Mayfair face extra rates bills as high as 60 per square foot, he says, adding to the premium that tenants must pay for space in that area.

New fund managers are also finding that their money goes farther elsewhere   and that they no longer need to be in Mayfair to prove that they are serious players in the industry.

James Holloway, the founder of Piquant Technologies, a quantitative hedge fund that uses artificial intelligence, says being in Mayfair was more of a detriment than a benefit for him when looking for office space.

The three-year old fund, which now manages about $200m, started out in the West London neighbourhood Turnham Green,  in someone s attic, effectively,  he recalled. That was followed by a space in Waterloo,  with 40-watt bulbs in the staircase and paint peeling off the walls,  he says.  We realised after a while we couldn t really bring investors here. 

They settled in Marylebone, just north of Mayfair and already home to the hedge funds Brevan Howard, Arrowgrass, Cevian and Aspect Capital, for nearly half the price.

 Being a quant fund, we just need desk space,  he says.  So if you could be anywhere in the world, why would you want to be in Mayfair, which is one of the most expensive places in the world? We ve heard very uniformly from investors, thank god you re not in Mayfair. It s quite a bad sign for managers, especially new ones who should be frugal, to be spending so much on rent. 

Many hedge funds continue to sign office leases in the most prestigious locations at eye-watering rents   and Mayfair and St James remain a draw. For example, Silver Ridge Asset Management, a macro hedge fund set up by Anil Prasad, the former head of foreign exchange at Citigroup, took a floor of 3 St James s Square for 160 per square foot per year in the first quarter of this year   more than double the average office rent in the City of London.

 There s a lot of signalling in this business,  says one hedge fund seed investor.  Now there s getting to be less of that. But location is really critical when you re starting something so it s harder to not be there. 

Mr Barnes adds:  To have SW1 on your letterhead is a very prestigious thing to have. 

By contrast, the quant fund Quadrature Capital in January leased new offices in the Leadenhall Building skyscraper, known as the Cheesegrater, in the City of London, where the highest reported rents are about 100 per sq ft; CQS leased space on the Strand in Covent Garden last year for 85 per sq ft.

Jones Lang LaSalle says that further headline-making Mayfair deals will be  few and far between  and tend to occur only where the spaces involved are relatively small.

Two investment managers, Orbis and Oaktree Capital Management, have both in the past year hunted for offices in Mayfair and nearby areas, only to settle for better value elsewhere   Orbis on a sublease in Marylebone, Oaktree in Victoria.

The Brexit vote is also adding to caution among office tenants, including hedge funds. One fund manager, GAM, scrapped plans to move to new offices in the Victoria area after the vote.

Andy Tyler, head of West End office agency at Cushman & Wakefield, says that companies have generally become  more footloose and less wedded to the heartland area for their particular industry .

 The market is getting tougher,  Mr Tyler says.  Tenants are being more discerning and taking more time to make decisions on relocations.

UK COMPANIES
Injection of 230m boosts Oxford university start-up fund; Technology

By Madhumita Murgia, European Technology Correspondent 
447 words
10 December 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
18
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Oxford Sciences Innovation, which commercialises science and technology from Oxford university, has expanded its coffers to 580m in a new funding round, making it the largest private university fund in the UK.

The 230m injection of capital has come primarily from Asian investors, including large tech companies that wished to remain anonymous, and sovereign wealth funds, such as Singapore's Temasek and Oman Investment Fund.

The fund has backed approximately 20 start-ups spun out from the university's labs, including Oxford Nanoimaging, Vaccitech and Oxford Flow - double the planned number a year ago.

The UK has five of the world's top 10 medical research universities, according to Times Higher Education, and Oxford has been number one for the past five years.

The burst of funding highlights the increasing flow of private capital into the UK's academic sector to help spin out research breakthroughs.

The 50m UCL Technology Fund was set up in January to invest in intellectual property from University College London. In the same month, the 40m Apollo Therapeutics Fund was created in collaboration with Cambridge, Imperial College London and UCL, with backing from AstraZeneca, GlaxoSmithKline and Johnson & Johnson.

These have joined others such as Imperial Innovations, IP Group and Cambridge Innovation Capital which were already active on UK campuses.

Cambridge Innovation Capital recently added 75m to its fund ahead of a planned flotation, while Neil Woodford, Britain's best-known fund manager, spent 11.6m to take a 22 per cent stake in Imperial College's offshoot fund, Imperial Innovations.

"Raising this capital reflects our confidence in the breadth and quality of opportunity available to investors to help the University of Oxford develop a world-class commercial ecosystem around its unmatched intellectual capital and heritage," said Peter Davies, chair of OSI. "We are also very excited to be working with new shareholders from across the world, notably from Asia and continental Europe."

Deep technology start-ups have been attractive targets for Asian investors: telecommunications, media and technology accounted for almost a third of Chinese investment last year, according to consultants Grant Thornton.

Tencent, the Chinese internet group, for instance, invested in two British companies in 2015, mapping start-up Sensewhere and gaming company Miniclip. In October, Chinese state-backed fund CSC Group helped fund a multimillion pound artificial intelligence incubator in London.

"This funding is a further vote of confidence in Britain's world-class research sector and will provide a real boost to local businesses as they develop new technologies," said Jo Johnson, the minister of state for universities, science, research and innovation.

Viswanatha's Take: Democratic AGs See Role to Play in Trump Era

Trump's Labor Pick Spells Potential Trouble for Retirement Rule

Orrin Hatch May Prove a Crucial Ally for Trump

SEC Enforcement Chief to Step Down

Federal Bank Watchdogs Say They're Not Forcing Banks From Payday

Democratic AGs See Role to Play in Trump Era

As the direction of financial regulation under the Trump administration continues to take shape, one group is gearing up for an uptick in activity: state attorneys general who are Democrats.

Over the past eight years, states with a Republican top law-enforcement official took an aggressive approach to fighting Obama administration efforts on everything from immigration to environmental rules to overtime pay. Texas has filed "https://www.texastribune.org/2016/07/27/texas-federal-government-lawsuits/", according to the Texas Tribune.

Just last week, state banking regulators pushed back against the decision by the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency to "http://www.wsj.com/articles/regulator-will-start-issuing-bank-charters-for-fintech-firms-1480691712/", saying the move "represents an historic expansion of the role of the federal government, one that will permeate into the economies in all 50 states and distort the financial system with unwelcome consequences."

The next few years are unlikely to be an exact mirror image of the Obama administration, given that it's easier to litigate against new federal regulations imposed on states than against any potential rollback. But a strategy appears to be taking shape among Democratic state regulators, particularly when it comes to financial services.

Experts point to two potential paths. One is similar to the Republican playbook, which directly challenges federal government decisions. States could intervene, for example, in the continuing litigation over the structure of the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau if the Trump administration declines to pursue the appeal to defend the current setup, experts said.

A second effort involves more enforcement of both state consumer-protection laws, as well as the authority granted to states under the federal Dodd-Frank regulatory-overhaul law. Under the 2010 law, state attorneys general can, in some instances, enforce the federal ban on unfair, deceptive or abusive financial practices, as well as some mortgage provisions included in the legislation. Illinois and a few other states have brought a handful of such actions, but experts expect that activity to pick up, especially if enforcement activity at the federal level drops.

Twenty-two states have Democratic attorneys general. The ones most likely to take on an outsize role are those who have been active over the past few years, including the AGs of New York, Massachusetts and Illinois.

A spokeswoman for New York Attorney General Eric Schneiderman said he has "never shied away from rooting out fraud in financial markets...that work will continue and accelerate when the new administration takes office."

Mr. Schneiderman has already started taking on the role, issuing press releases criticizing President-elect Donald Trump's reported selections for the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/trump-expected-to-name-fast-food-executive-andy-puzder-as-labor-secretary-1481210445" and "http://www.wsj.com/articles/donald-trump-taps-oklahoma-attorney-general-scott-pruitt-to-lead-epa-1481143068". The EPA pick, Scott Pruitt, is himself a Republican AG who has fought Obama administration environmental regulations.

Massachusetts Attorney General Maura Healey said: "My office will continue to enforce important state laws to protect consumers and investors from financial fraud and abuses in Massachusetts. We know from our experience in the mortgage lending crisis that these critical regulatory safeguards need to remain in place." A spokeswoman for Illinois Attorney General Lisa Madigan declined to comment.

"If the SEC is weakened under the Trump administration...it will be all the more important that state attorneys general and other state regulators are able to step up their deterrence of fraud," said Chad Johnson, who ran the investor protection group at the New York attorney general's office until earlier this year.

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Trump's Labor Pick Spells Potential Trouble for Retirement Rule

By Yuka Hayashi

WASHINGTON The choice of Andy Puzder, a fast-food-chain executive with strong antiregulation views, for labor secretary has fueled expectations among financial-industry experts that the Labor Department's new retirement-savings rule faces delays or even a rollback.

While Republican lawmakers have renewed their pledge to block the rule in recent weeks, President-elect Donald Trump's stance is unknown. And like Mr. Trump, Mr. Puzder now chief executive of CKE Restaurants Holdings Inc., the parent company of Carl's Jr. and Hardee's hasn't publicly discussed his views on the so-called fiduciary rule, or on retirement savings in general. Mr. Puzder declined to comment for this article through a representative of CKE.

After a long battle with the financial industry and Republican lawmakers, the Obama administration finalized the Labor Department rule in April. The rule holds brokers and advisers who work with tax-advantaged retirement savings to a "fiduciary" standard, meaning they must work in the best interest of their clients and generally avoid conflicts, which can come about with commission-based compensation. Previously, they were required to offer only "suitable" guidance, a less-rigorous standard.

Mr. Puzder did at least once take the side of retirees challenging the financial industry. As a private-sector lawyer in St. Louis in the 1980s, he represented the city's police pension fund as it sued investment firms for what it called poor investment and bilking of the fund.

But analysts have said his handling of the fiduciary rule will be more likely driven by his well-chronicled staunch dislike of government regulations, which he believes stifle entrepreneurship and growth. As an adviser and contributor to Mr. Trump's campaign, he has criticized the Affordable Care Act and has fought against raising the federal minimum wage.

"More government is not the solution to every problem; it's the problem to every solution," Mr. Puzder he said in an interview with Franchise Times last month. "You can't order businesses to be profitable, or place restrictions on them and expect them to grow."

Mr. Puzder's remarks, along with his close dealings with financial companies during his career, are enough to make some experts believe the new administration will kill or significantly weaken the investment-advice rule, which is set to take effect in April 2017.

"The likelihood of seeing a delay or [it] being altered is certainly increasing," said Alois Pirker, research director at Aite Group. "I have my doubts they'll just call it off right away," Mr. Pirker said, adding that he instead expects a delay in implementing the rule, possibly until the end of next year. "That gives enough time to review or revise" the rule, he said.

Edward Mills, an analyst at FBR & Co., wrote in a research note Thursday that he predicts the new administration will first delay the implementation of the rule through an administrative action and then repeal or overhaul it. "His public comments show that he is largely opposed to Obama-era regulation in the labor and health-care arenas," Mr. Mills said of Mr. Puzder.

Besides the fiduciary rule, the Obama administration finalized a rule in August to encourage states to set up retirement-savings plans with automatic enrollment features for private-sector employees who don't have access to corporate-sponsored retirement savings plans, such as part-time workers.

Since Mr. Trump's victory, Republican lawmakers have given a high priority to blocking the fiduciary rule in their broader efforts to roll back Obama-era financial regulations. Among them is Rep. Jeb Hensarling (R., Texas), chairman of the House Financial Services Committee, who in June proposed blocking the fiduciary rule until after the Securities Exchange Commission has had a chance to study the issue closely.

Among Mr. Trump's advisers, Anthony Scaramucci, who runs hedge fund SkyBridge Capital, is the only one known to have commented on the rule. At an October conference, he said the rule would be repealed, calling it possibly "the dumbest decision to come out of the U.S. government in the last 50 to 60 years," according to Investment News. Mr. Scaramucci also criticized the rule in a Wall Street Journal opinion piece last month.

Proponents have said repealing the rule won't be easy. Since it has already been finalized, changes will require the new administration to go through an onerous process of public notice and comments as well as cost-benefit analysis. And legislative effort to scrap the rule would face strong opposition from Democrats in the Senate, including Sen. Elizabeth Warren (D., Mass.)

Sen. Cory Booker (D., N.J.), another strong backer of the fiduciary rule, said in an interview Thursday that if the Trump administration wants to fight the regulation, "I'm willing to take that fight to the American people."

The Congressional Review Act, a legislative weapon the Republicans are likely to use to kill some Obama-era rules, can't be used for the fiduciary rule as Mr. Obama has already vetoed a resolution approved by Congress in June.

"What we are anticipating is there is going to be numerous attempts to change and modify the rule and add more exemptions," said Cristina Martin Firvida, director of financial security at AARP, which has supported the rule. "But it's not going to be quick, and it's not going to be easy."

Still, the rule will be more vulnerable after January with the departure of two officials who have defended them forcefully. Mr. Puzder will succeed Thomas Perez, a progressive civil-rights lawyer. Phyllis Borzi, the chief architect of the rule who heads the department's Employee Benefits Security Administration, is expected to leave soon.

In a speech on Dec. 1, Ms. Borzi said the fiduciary rule has already had an effect. Investment companies such as BlackRock Inc. and Charles Schwab Corp have slashed fees on their exchange-traded funds. Betterment, an automated investment advice firm, bought a full-page newspaper ad this week, asking Mr. Trump to keep the rule intact.

"We are really not going back to the old ways," Ms. Borzi said.

 Lisa Beilfuss contributed to this article.

Orrin Hatch May Prove a Crucial Ally for Trump

By Brody Mullins and Rebecca Ballhaus

WASHINGTON In the weeks since Donald Trump was elected, a senior member of Congress has emerged as an important source of counsel: Sen. Orrin Hatch (R., Utah), the longest-serving Senate Republican and the chairman of a committee that will handle much of Mr. Trump's agenda.

Mr. Hatch was one of the first senators to endorse Mr. Trump for president and campaigned for him in Utah and other states where many residents share Mr. Hatch's Mormon faith.

Since the election, the two have spoken regularly about potential nominations for the Supreme Court and the Trump administration, as well as about Mr. Trump's legislative agenda, according to people familiar with the conversations.

As chairman of the Senate Finance Committee, Mr. Hatch heads the panel charged with confirming many of Mr. Trump's top nominees, including Treasury secretary. The committee will handle several other priorities for Mr. Trump, including replacing President Barack Obama's health-care law and overhauling the tax system.

Mr. Trump doesn't have close ties with many members of Congress, so Mr. Hatch has become an important middleman between Mr. Trump and the Senate. A few weeks ago, Mr. Trump tapped Mr. Hatch's chief of staff, Robert Porter, to assist with his transition process.

Mr. Hatch has also forged a close relationship beginning this summer with Reince Priebus, who has been chairman of the Republican National Committee and is Mr. Trump's incoming chief of staff. Mr. Hatch donated a total of $75,000 from his political-action committee to the RNC and Mr. Trump's presidential campaign, one of the few senators to do so.

Mr. Hatch first endorsed Jeb Bush, the former Florida governor, for president. He switched to Florida Sen. Marco Rubio after Mr. Bush dropped out. Later, he appeared open to the idea of 2012 nominee Mitt Romney a vocal critic of Mr. Trump running again.

Mr. Hatch endorsed Mr. Trump in May, when the nomination was all but sewn up. Still, he was among the first senators to do so. Mr. Hatch, a Mormon, campaigned for Mr. Trump in Utah and Nevada and held steady as prominent Utah and Mormon figures turned against Mr. Trump.

When Mr. Trump suggested a federal judge's Mexican heritage should disqualify him from overseeing a case involving Trump University, the now defunct real-estate school, Mr. Hatch told the Los Angeles Times: "Be nice to him. He's a poor first-time candidate."

At the summer's Republican convention, Mr. Hatch sat in the Trump family's box on occasion, where he spoke frequently with Donald Trump Jr. In the fall, he traveled with Mr. Trump's son while they campaigned together for several days in Arizona, Utah and Nevada. Mr. Trump won Arizona and Utah.

In October, after the release of a tape of Mr. Trump making lewd comments about groping women, Mr. Hatch was steadfast in his support, though he called Mr. Trump's comments "offensive and disgusting." Several other Utah Republicans, by contrast, withdrew their endorsements and called for Mr. Trump to quit the race.

"Hatch was up against more political winds than anyone else, but he held firm like a rock, because he really believed in Trump," said Rick Hohlt, a Republican consultant and friend of Mr. Hatch.

A few days after the campaign, Messrs. Trump and Hatch had an initial conversation about Mr. Trump's vision for his presidency. They have since exchanged cellphone numbers and have spoken roughly a dozen times, people familiar with the conversations say.

Mr. Trump has called to get the senator's thoughts on possible candidates for the Supreme Court and other administration posts, these people say. Last month, the two had several conversations about potential nominees for secretary of state, including former Republican presidential nominee Mitt Romney and former New York City Mayor Rudy Giuliani. Mr. Trump has since broadened his search.

Mr. Hatch is a former chairman of the Senate Judiciary Committee, which must approve Mr. Trump's nomination to the Supreme Court. By reaching out to Mr. Hatch for his guidance on nominations, Mr. Trump is signaling that he is taking a practical approach by considering whether potential nominations could win approval in the committee and the Senate.

The two have also discussed plans to replace the Affordable Care Act, enact tax reform and reduce federal regulations.

Mr. Hatch's committee must approve Mr. Trump's nomination of Steven Mnuchin to be Treasury secretary and Rep. Tom Price (R., Ga.) as secretary of the Health and Human Services Department. Mr. Hatch has meetings scheduled for later this week with both Messrs. Mnuchin and Price.

Earlier this week, Mr. Hatch met with Sen. Jeff Sessions (R., Ala.), who has been tapped to serve as attorney general.

SEC Enforcement Chief to Step Down

By Aruna Viswanatha

WASHINGTON The enforcement director for the Securities and Exchange Commission will leave the agency in the coming weeks, the agency said Thursday, the latest in a series of departures as the SEC prepares for a new administration that could take a more lenient approach.

Andrew Ceresney, who came to the agency with Chairman Mary Jo White in early 2013, has overseen a unit that brought a record number of cases in the past three years using a strategy that targeted small infractions in addition to bigger frauds.

"We really have brought important cases across the whole securities realm, in every priority area," Mr. Ceresney said in an interview, citing areas including financial reporting and insider trading, and cases addressing conduct at dark pools, private-equity firms and in municipal offerings.

The SEC said Mr. Ceresney's deputy, Stephanie Avakian, will become the acting director of the unit.

While the contours of the SEC's objectives in the Donald Trump administration are still taking shape, Mr. Trump and his advisers have said repeatedly they would try to roll back landmark financial regulations imposed during President Barack Obama's administration. On Monday, Mr. Trump met with former U.S. Attorney Debra Wong Yang and is considering her for the SEC chairmanship, which might signal a less-radical shift from the current regime since she would bring a background more in common with Ms. White.

In the interview, Mr. Ceresney said he didn't expect much to change in the near term since many cases remain in the pipeline and investigations can take years. He said he expected more insider trading cases that involve hacked information, cases targeting manipulative trading activity, and those against auditors over allegedly improper audits.

"Enforcement will always be a high priority, it's a bipartisan issue," Mr. Ceresney said. "Any enforcement director should be encouraging their people to be creative and aggressive but also fair."

After Ms. White's said last month that she planned to leave the agency at the end of the Obama administration, the SEC has announced many departures including the corporate finance director, the agency's chief economist, its chief accountant, its trading and markets director, and chief litigation counsel. The positions aren't technically political appointees, but the next chairman is expected to install new people in top posts.

When Ms. White came to the agency, she brought Mr. Ceresney, who had worked with her for more than a decade at her former law firm and at the Manhattan U.S. attorney's office. Together they pursued handpicked priorities, including the policing of financial-reporting fraud and punishing even the smallest violations on the theory that it would deter a culture of misconduct.

Under Mr. Ceresney's watch, the enforcement division filed 143 cases under an initiative targeting inaccurate municipal bond disclosures and dozens of cases alleging short selling violations in advance of public offerings. It also brought several major insider trading cases, including against hedge-fund manager Leon Cooperman and sports-bettor William "Billy" Walters, and reached settlements of hundreds of millions of dollars with Och-Ziff Capital Management Group for foreign bribery and Bank of America Corp.'s Merrill Lynch unit for misusing customer funds.

After years of commuting between New York and Washington, Mr. Ceresney said he doesn't know yet what he will do next but plans to take some time off, reflect on his time in government, and see his family more.

He said his wife is happy about the change but said she mentioned she would lose full access to the television remote on weekday evenings: "Well, it's not all good. Now I gotta debate with you what to watch," he said, relating what his wife told him.

Stifel, Former Executive to Pay $25 Million for Selling  Unsuitably Risky' CDOs

By Imani Moise

Stifel Nicolaus & Co. and a former executive will pay about $25 million after admitting to wrongdoing in case where the Securities and Exchange Commission alleged it sold "unsuitably risky and complex" financial instruments to five school districts in Wisconsin.

The SEC alleged in 2011 that the financial-services firm and a former senior vice president, David Noack, knowingly sold the CDOs to the districts in 2006, causing them to suffer a complete loss of their cash investments.

Analysis: EU Sees Limits to  Bespoke' Post-Brexit Deal With U.K.

By Laurence Norman and Stephen Fidler

Prime Minister Theresa May insists the agreement setting out the U.K.'s future relationship with the European Union won't be an off-the-shelf affair. Britain will get a "bespoke" arrangement exclusively tailored to its own interests, she has said.

That may be true. It is unlikely a future agreement will look like the EU's close economic relations with Norway, which has membership in the bloc's single market and puts no caps on EU migration. Nor will it simply mirror the EU's new trade agreement with Canada, for example.

Officials in Brussels appear to agree. If the U.K. wants a tailor-made deal, they say, there is no reason why it can't have one.

But the apparent accord on this point masks a fundamental difference between the two sides. British politicians and officials argue the U.K. brings things to the EU that no other partner does. They cite security and foreign-policy assistance, counterterrorism and intelligence sharing as well as the size and proximity of its economy. They see those assets as justifying a special deal that reflects the U.K.'s singular pattern of EU cooperation, which no other country has.

Yet for now a time when with opening positions still being formed that argument isn't carrying weight in Brussels and other EU capitals.

For the EU, a bespoke agreement means Britain would end up in a unique place on the spectrum of rights and obligations Brussels has long used to map out ties with non-EU countries.

Such an agreement could be narrow or broad, addressing any or all of the following, and more: tariff levels and nontariff barriers, trade in services, capital- and labor-market issues, business regulation, agriculture, fisheries, and noneconomic issues such as database-sharing to help fight terrorism.

But EU officials insist the U.K.'s access to the EU market will depend on the obligations it is willing to undertake.

Until now, Mrs. May has only given hints about her vision of the future relationship. She is expected to set out more details before the end of March, when she triggers Article 50 of the Lisbon Treaty and starts a two-year time period for the two sides to agree on Britain's exit terms.

She has said she wants the U.K. to control immigration from EU countries and end the European Court of Justice's jurisdiction over U.K. affairs. She has also said she wants Britain to enjoy free trade in goods and services and for British companies to have maximum freedom to operate in the single market.

European governments say restricting free movement of labor and ignoring the ECJ are incompatible with membership in the EU's single market. For one thing, the ECJ polices the single market; if the British aren't subject to its rulings, how can they be part of that market?

British government ministers have openly disagreed about whether the country should keep making payments to the EU, as Norway, Switzerland and even Israel do. EU officials say Britain will have to pay into the budget if it wants access to EU programs on science and research, education and security. In other words, Britain can't have its cake and eat it, too.

Vicky Ford, a member of the European Parliament from Mrs. May's own Conservative Party, said in a speech this week that Mrs. May had outlined three priorities: immigration control, market access and control over regulation. She urged both sides to remain flexible and said the U.K. should steer clear of maximalist demands, especially on immigration.

"Like a three-legged stool, if one is prioritized more than the others, it can all become unbalanced," she said.

Yet, so far, no dice. On Tuesday, the EU's chief Brexit negotiator Michel Barnier gave the clearest indications yet that Britain will have to base its choices on a largely predetermined set of benefits and costs that already apply in other EU agreements.

"When you examine different options for future relations naturally, you don't need me to do it you can look at the different models that exist," he said, in his first public remarks since taking up his post. He cited as an example Norway, whose close access to the single market is secured by large payments to the EU.

Mujtaba Rahman, Europe head for the Eurasia Group consulting firm, said Mr. Barnier has made "implied and assumed" thinking "an explicit part of the EU's red lines."

Federal Bank Watchdogs Say They're Not Forcing Banks From Payday

By Annamaria Andriotis and Telis Demos

Federal regulators in court filings Thursday pushed back against claims that they are forcing banks to cut off payday lenders.

The Federal Deposit Insurance Corp., the Office of the Comptroller of the Currency and the Federal Reserve disputed the payday-loan industry's assertion that regulators are forcing banks to shut down lenders' accounts and cutting off access to banking services, saying that the theory is based "on erroneous and unsupported speculation," according to a court filing.

Advance America, one of the largest payday lenders, and the Community Financial Services Association of America, which represents payday lenders, filed an emergency motion last month that seeks a preliminary injunction against the regulators in an attempt to block what they allege is arm twisting of the banks to drop payday customers.

Advance America said in its legal filing that U.S. Bancorp and four other banks had notified the company they were ending their relationships with it in recent weeks. They said the banks pulled the plug under pressure from regulators the main basis for last month's court filing.

The three regulators disputed that allegation on Thursday. OCC official Serena Christenson, who oversees the team of examiners who work onsite at U.S. Bank's Minneapolis headquarters, said no one on her team "directed or otherwise pressured U.S. Bank's management or U.S. Bank's Board of Directors or any other bank employee to terminate the Bank's relationship with Advance America or any other customer," in a declaration filed with the U.S. District Court for the District of Columbia. A U.S. Bank spokesman declined to comment.

The payday industry is standing by its claims. "The government's protestations of innocence cannot be squared with the extensive factual record demonstrating its illegal conduct," said David Thompson, the CFSA's legal counsel.

Payday loans are high-cost, small-dollar loans that are mostly targeted at low-income borrowers as well as those with low credit scores. Interest rates on these loans are generally in triple-digit territory and borrowers often get stuck in a cycle of debt when they sign up for them.

Tensions between the payday industry and regulators came to a head back in 2014 when the payday-lending association and Advance America filed a suit after a first wave of banks canceled their relationships with payday lenders. Last month's action and Thursday's response by regulators is part of the litigation.

WSJ Pro Analysis: Fed Critics Favor Reversing Central Bank Mission Creep

By Adam Creighton

Nobel Prize-winning economist Jan Tinbergen once immortalized the idea that central bankers needed as many policy tools as they have policy targets.

They haven't let him down. Policy tool kits have grown since the financial crisis   to include acronyms WSJPro readers know well: ZIRP, NIRP, QE   to achieve price stability and the more nebulous goal of financial stability.

In the U.S., congressional Republicans aren't happy about this, advocating for a Federal Reserve that is "more predictable in its conduct of monetary policy and more transparent about its decision-making", taking particular aim at "vague and improvisational" forward guidance.

Former Fed governor Kevin Warsh, an adviser to the president-elect on financial matters, along with other Republican-leaning academics put some flesh on this potential "back to basics" approach to monetary policy at a policy conference in Washington, D.C. this week.

They called for a diminished Fed role in macroprudential regulation and a formal accord between the Treasury and the Fed.

They are going to have an uphill battle, though. The history of the Fed is one of continual mission creep. In 1977 the Federal Reserve Act was amended to give a then much less high-profile Fed a specific mandate to conduct policy "so as to promote effectively the goals of maximum employment, stable prices, and moderate long-term interest rates".

In 2012 the Board formalized that goal into a 2% inflation target over the medium term.

While a desire to promote financial stability underpinned the Fed's creation in 1913, the 2010 Dodd-Frank Act expanded the Fed's powers still further, via enhanced supervisory powers of bank and nonbank institutions through the Fed chair's membership of the Financial Stability Oversight Council.

"If you subjected macroprudential regulation to a Google search you'd find a paucity of literature before 2010," said Mr. Warsh, who served on the Fed board between 2006 and 2011, contrasting the emerging policy area--which involves using financial regulations to promote financial stability--with the voluminous analysis of monetary policy since World War II. Stanford University economist John Taylor, sometimes touted as a potential Fed chairman, said at the same conference that the central bank should gradually reduce its huge holdings of securities.

Former Philadelphia Fed President Charles Plosser and Hoover Institution fellow John Cochrane both advocated for an accord between the Treasury and the Federal Reserve to clarify responsibilities and powers.

Mr. Plosser argued that the Treasury should be able to instruct the Fed to purchase securities in a financial crisis, but plan to later shift those securities onto its own balance sheet so profits and losses become more transparent.

"Fed-funds rate changes have become like Papal elections," added Mr. Cochrane, who said the accord should give Treasury pre-eminent say over the maturity structure of public debt. "We've lived through this crazy world where the Treasury sells long and the Fed buys them all back up again," he said.

WSJ Pro: Market Talk: Daily Analysis Roundup

U.S. Daily Payment, Clearing Flows Valued at $7.1T

The Payments Risk Committee, a private sector group of senior managers from U.S. banks sponsored by the Federal Reserve Bank of New York, put out a new report Thursday calculating the value of aggregate daily U.S. dollar flows through all payment and clearing utilities or banks as $7.1T. The Committee said currently there was ample liquidity in the system to meet those obligations, but changes under way since the financial crisis meant that regulators should keep a close eye on the risk of any disruptions. "Notwithstanding the availability of sufficient liquidity, a number of changes that can affect liquidity are already under way and should be monitored to identify potential bottlenecks," the Fed's payments committee said. "It is critical that the industry look for continuous improvement and refinement of liquidity management processes." (Katy.Burne@wsj.com)

Analyst: BNY Mellon's Payment Failures Pose Systemic Risk

BNY Mellon's payment-clearing problem reported in WSJ Wednesday poses various policy problems and should "reignite discussions of how to regulate financial-market utilities," writes Karen Petrou of Federal Financial Analytics in a client note Thursday. The bank failed to process some payments on Monday because it lost access to the Swift funds-transfer network. BK's problems "will exacerbate industry and [prudential regulator] fears that critical markets could be disrupted with far-reaching systemic effect," she wrote. The Fed became concerned about BK's concentrated role in payments and at one point considered making the bank separate its Treasury and repo clearing arm into a utility. That idea never materialized. (katy.burne@wsj.com)

Moody's: Federal Charter Could Be Good for Fintech

Moody's says a bank regulator's proposal for fintech companies to obtain a federal charter would be a "credit positive" for bonds backed by loans from certain online lenders. The Office of the Comptroller of the Currency released details last Friday of a new regulatory structure in which fintech companies could receive a license that would allow them to, among other things, make loans above state usury limits. That could protect loans from online lenders that got a charter from legal rulings that have called into question whether their prior loans are enforceable in certain states. Moody's analysts also say it could be more expensive for fintech companies to operate under such a charter since it would come with minimum capital requirements, among other restrictions. (peter.rudegeair@wsj.com; @rudegeair)

Credit-Union Suit May Kick Up Tax-Exempt Status

A brewing legal fight between bankers and credit unions on the latter's membership rules has the potential to draw more attention to a debate about credit unions' tax-exempt status, says Cowen. The American Bankers Association today sued the National Credit Union Administration alleging the industry regulator illegally expanded credit-union-membership rules. But Cowen thinks "this lawsuit is designed to draw attention to the tax status of credit unions just as Congress appears ready to address corporate-tax reform." DC changing the tax status of politically popular credit unions is a long shot, but the issue could generate "headline risk." (ryan.tracy@wsj.com; @ryanjtracy)

Repo Data May Help Libor-Alternatives Search

The Office of Financial Research says it's uniquely positioned to help regulators come up with the best alternatives to Libor because it's collecting data other agencies haven't yet been able to. Director Richard Berner says the office has been working on gathering data for short-term loans called repos, struck directly between two banks and not routed to a third-party processor. The majority of repos collected already by regulators are cleared by an intermediary, but he notes the majority of trades making up Libor "are still based on judgment" instead of real-life transactions. As such, the one-to-one, or "bilateral," repos the OFR are collecting will help select an alternative reference rate. Says Berner: "The OFR is uniquely situated to collect such data that may lie beyond the reach of other regulators." (katy.burne@wsj.com)

Fed Schedules Final Vote on TLAC Bailout-Prevention Rule

The Federal Reserve's governing board will meet Thursday, December 15, at 11 am to consider a final rule mandating "total loss absorbing capacity" for big banks, the Fed says. The bailout prevention rule, first proposed last year, would mandate that banks issue debt that could be converted into equity in a crisis. The industry and regulators in other countries will be looking to see if the Fed made changes that address concerns raised since the proposal. (Ryan.Tracy@wsj.com; @ryanjtracy)

Mixing Lending and Collateralized Loan Obligations

Firms that both lend and manage collateralized loan obligations can "reap information advantages" from their "originate to distribute" activities. Does the commingling of the lending and collateral management roles by these firms compel them to trade differently? "http://libertystreeteconomics.newyorkfed.org/2016/12/are-all-clos-equal.html" explore that question in a new research brief. Risk-Sensitive Monetary Policy Works Best, Say BIS Researchers

A paper published by the "https://www.bis.org/publ/work594.pdf" makes the case for monetary policies that take into account the potential for financial risks, known as "leaning against the wind." The authors concluded that "the policy's main benefit arises from a dampening of the whole financial cycle   in terms of the frequency and severity of financial downturns." Their findings support a shift from a narrow focus on price stability "to a joint consideration of price and financial stability."

KKR offers to buy stake in GfK

Financial Times 
301 words
8 December 2016
11:45
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

KKR, the US-based buyout group, has offered to buy a large minority stake in Germany s GfK, a market research company that publishes data on consumer confidence and products.

KKR has offered to pay a  ¬43.50 per share for the Frankfurt listed business, a 44 per cent premium to Gfk s three-month average price, report Javier Espinoza and Arash Massoudi in London.

The offer has received the full support of GfK Verein, a foundation that controls 56.5 per cent of GfK s shares. The foundation will remain a partner following the KKR investment and will not be reducing its stake.

KKR said the tender offer is conditional to at least 18.5 per cent of GfK s other shareholders beyond the foundation taking up the bid.

GfK analyses big data to help major advertisers and consumer goods manufacturers enrich their consumer experience through advertisement.

In August GfK s stock dropped by more than 20 per cent following the sudden resignations of both its chief executive and chairman after falling out with GfK Verein.

The German company has also struggle to retain market share following delays in contracts and other initiatives.

It is also lacking innovation to properly compete with rivals in its local market.

KKR said it will help the Nuremberg-based group retain its market share as new entrants challenge the business as it looks to turn the business around.

The buyout group, which has already worked with similar companies, including Nielsen, the digital ad ratings service, said it sees potential to turn GfK into a global company as it looks to boost its digital ventures worldwide.

KKR beat off a number of suitors for the business, including TPG, another buyout group, people close to the matter said.

MARKETS - BACK SECTION
Buy signals grow stronger for passive products; Analysis: Fund management

By Chris Flood 
681 words
8 December 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
USA Ed1
22
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Billionaire investor Buffett is among those sceptical that active strategies can fight back

Legions of investors make a pilgrimage each year to Omaha, Nebraska, to listen to Warren Buffett at the annual shareholder meeting of Berkshire Hathaway.

Few, however, would have anticipated the advice the billionaire delivered in 2014 when he declared that 90 per cent of the cash that he would bequeath to his wife should go to a low-cost S&P 500 index tracker fund.

As well as highlighting the benefit of cheap tracker funds, Mr Buffett also attacked active investing, saying that non-professional investors should avoid trying to pick winning stocks.

A swath of academic evidence supports Mr Buffett, demonstrating that most active managers do not consistently outperform their benchmark once fees have been taken into account.

Among investors, a growing understanding of the importance of fees and charges on long-term returns is driving a huge shift into passively managed strategies, particularly among exchange traded funds that have attracted more than $1.5tn in net cash inflows since 2010.

Momentum in this direction looks set to intensify in the US. The government has proposed that a rule , due to come into force next April, will require financial professionals who provide retirement savings advice to uphold a fiduciary standard that puts clients' interests above their own.

"The new US rule will accelerate the demand for passive strategies and ETFs," says Ju-Hon Kwek of McKinsey. The consultancy believes the asset management industry stands on the brink of a "once-in-a-generation shift" as the result of weaker investment returns, tougher regulations and rising competitive pressures.

McKinsey predicts $8tn of traditional actively managed assets will be "up for grabs" over the next decade, creating a battleground for index fund providers and alternative managers including hedge funds , private equity , real estate and infrastructure.

But Mr Kwek warns it would be wrong to assume that passive funds will be the "sole beneficiary" of this shake-up. Innovative active managers will be able to use new tools, such as big data, to capitalise on their asset allocation expertise and to exploit illiquid investment opportunities.

Support for that view is provided by data from PwC, which show that holdings in passively managed funds and institutional mandates stood at about $7.3tn at the end of 2012, about 11.4 per cent of managed assets globally.

PwC forecasts that passively managed funds and mandates will more than triple to about $23.2bn by 2020, accounting for 21 per cent of the $112tn in globally managed assets.

This means the absolute increase in actively managed assets of $23.8bn by 2020 will outstrip the $15.9bn rise in passively managed assets.

Defenders of active management say that a long era of extraordinary interest rate policy by central banks which boosted the value of both equities and bonds since 2009 may have reached the end of the line. As financial markets respond to the prospect of greater fiscal stimulus led by the incoming administration of Donald Trump , the passive industry for its part is also ready for a return to the type of investment climate seen before the crisis.

Passive fund managers are developing increasingly sophisticated strategies that will present fresh challenges to traditional active managers. Smart beta - index-based strategies that can exploit the mispricing of assets - can be used as a low-cost substitute for some hedge fund strategies. Low-cost automated investment services, known as robo-advice , are being rolled out by fund managers and banks globally.

Anil Sharma, analyst with Morgan Stanley in London, concedes the post-crisis deterioration in the performance of hedge funds suggests that active managers could struggle to regain investors' confidence even as market conditions become more challenging.

Since Mr Buffett issued his advice in early 2014, Vanguard's S&P 500 ETF has returned 25.7 per cent while returns across the hedge fund industry have averaged just 4.6 per cent, according to HFR, the data provider.

ICV Partners said it invested in SG360, a provider of printing and multichannel direct marketing services.

Can the passive tailwind keep blowing for ETFs?

Chris Flood 
936 words
7 December 2016
04:05
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

This is part of a "http://www.ft.com/etfage" on the global rise of exchange traded funds

Legions of investors make a pilgrimage each year to Omaha, Nebraska, to listen to Warren Buffett at the annual shareholder meeting of Berkshire Hathaway.

Few, however, would have anticipated the advice that the billionaire delivered in 2014 when he declared that 90 per cent of the cash that he would bequeath to his wife should go to a low-cost S&P 500 index tracker fund.

As well as highlighting the benefit of cheap tracker funds, Mr Buffett also attacked active investing, arguing that non-professional investors should avoid trying to pick winning stocks.

A swath of academic evidence supports Mr Buffett, demonstrating that most active managers do not consistently outperform their benchmark once fees have been taken into account.

Among investors, a growing understanding of the importance of fees and charges on long-term returns is driving a massive shift into passively managed strategies, particularly among exchange traded funds that have attracted more than $1.5tn in net cash inflows since 2010.

Momentum in this direction looks set to intensify in the US. The government has proposed that a rule, due to come into force next April, will require financial professionals who provide retirement savings advice to uphold a fiduciary standard that puts clients  interests above their own.

 The new US rule will accelerate the demand for passive strategies and ETFs,  says Ju-Hon Kwek, a partner with McKinsey in New York.

The consultancy believes that the asset management industry stands on the brink of a  once-in-a-generation shift  as the result of weaker investment returns, tougher regulations and rising competitive pressures.

McKinsey predicts that $8tn of traditional actively managed assets would be  up for grabs  over the next decade, creating a battleground for index fund providers and alternative managers including "https://www.ft.com/topics/themes/Hedge_funds", "https://www.ft.com/topics/themes/Private_equity", "https://www.ft.com/companies/property" and infrastructure.

But Mr Kwek warns it would be wrong to assume that passive funds will be the  sole beneficiary  of this shake-up. Innovative active managers will be able to use new tools, such as big data, to capitalise on their asset allocation expertise and to exploit illiquid investment opportunities.

Anil Sharma, an analyst with Morgan Stanley in London, says more nimble active managers will be able to adapt by offering new products that answer growing demand for more environmentally and socially responsible investment strategies. More quantitatively-driven strategies that exploit big data will provide another avenue to resist the encroachment of passive ETFs, according to Mr Sharma, who says gloomy predictions of the death of active management are  exaggerated .

Support for that view is provided by data from PwC, the professional services company, which show that holdings in passively managed funds and institutional mandates stood at about $7.3tn at the end of 2012, about 11.4 per cent of managed assets globally.

PwC forecasts that passively managed funds and mandates will more than triple to about $23.2tn by 2020, accounting for 21 per cent of the $112tn in globally managed assets.

This means that the absolute increase in actively managed assets of $23.8tn by 2020 will outstrip the $15.9tn rise in passively managed assets.

Defenders of active management argue that a long era of extraordinary interest rate policy by central banks and which boosted the value of both equities and bonds since 2009 may have reached the end of the line.

Euan Munro, chief executive of Aviva Investors, says  this period is coming to an end , adding that investors will require active managers to help them navigate through the more volatile conditions that lie ahead.

 The journey towards a zero interest rate world provided a significant tailwind for all financial assets, making it relatively easy to secure healthy returns,  he says.

As financial markets respond to the prospect of greater fiscal stimulus led by the incoming administration of "https://www.ft.com/donald-trump", the passive industry for its part is also ready for a return to the type of investment climate seen before the crisis.

Passive fund managers are developing increasingly sophisticated strategies that will present fresh challenges to traditional active managers. Smart beta   index-based strategies that can exploit the mispricing of assets   can be used as a low-cost substitute for some hedge fund strategies.

Low-cost automated investment services, known as robo-advice, are being rolled out by fund managers and banks globally. Although some robo-advice services have started to incorporate actively managed funds, the vast majority of their assets are directed into passive ETFs to save costs. Citigroup, the US bank, has predicted robo-advice could develop into a $15tn market by the end of 2025.

Mr Sharma concedes that the post-crisis deterioration in the performance of hedge funds suggests that active managers could struggle to regain investors  confidence even as market conditions become more challenging.

Since Mr Buffett issued his advice in early 2014, Vanguard s S&P 500 ETF has returned 25.7 per cent while returns across the hedge fund industry   home to the most aggressive active managers   have averaged just 4.6 per cent, according to HFR, the data provider.

Active managers still hanging on to ETF coat-tails in Trump world

Rapid rise of the ETFs sparks growing pains

WORLD NEWS
Turks urged to trust in lira as strong dollar takes toll; Emerging markets

By Mehul Srivastava in Istanbul and Jonathan Wheatley in London 
679 words
7 December 2016
Financial Times
FTFT
London Ed1
08
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Currency's weakness has raised fears companies will struggle to repay debt

Emerging markets across the globe are already suffering because of expectations of a US interest rate rise - and few are feeling the pain more than Turkey .

The country's currency has been plunging in the wake of the election of Donald Trump , who favours fiscal stimulus rather than ultra-low interest rates, and ahead of a Federal Reserve meeting next week that could raise benchmark rates for only the second time since the financial crisis.

In response, President Recep Tayyip Erdogan has called on citizens' patriotism to alleviate the knock-on effect on the lira. He has exhorted them to break what he describes as the "tyranny of the dollar", asking people to stock up on the lira and gold instead and so defeat the "economic sabotage" perpetrated by the country's enemies.

Some businessmen have responded to the leader's call by offering free bus rides or haircuts to those who have sold dollars. Turkish officials add that capital controls have been discussed - but up to now rejected - as a means of arresting the slide of the lira, down more than 10 per cent against the dollar since Mr Trump's election on November 8.

"As long as our citizens don't abandon the lira, we will be OK," said one official, referring to the hope that Turkish nationals will respond to Mr Erdogan's calls by converting into lira some of the roughly $140bn held in the country's foreign currency accounts.

Other emerging markets leaders have been more restrained in their language and less imaginative in their solutions. But they face the same problem: a strengthening dollar and rising US interest rates threaten to hammer their currencies by sending overseas investors rushing for the exit.

Market movements highlight the interplay between low US interest rates and capital flows in and out of developing economies - and the high stakes for countries such as Turkey, whose companies took advantage of ultra-low monetary policy elsewhere to take out large amounts of foreign-denominated debt. According to the latest weekly data, foreign investors dumped $2.6bn-worth of Turkish bonds and equities in November. Turkish citizens ignored Mr Erdogan' s pleas and bought $1bn of foreign currency in the last week of the month, says Atilla Yesilada of Istanbul Analytics, a consultancy.

It is largely the same story across emerging markets. International investors ditched emerging market stocks and bonds in November on a scale not seen since their last great exodus in 2013, withdrawing more than $24bn from such assets, according to the Institute of International Finance.

Investors in emerging market assets fear that - after a false start a year ago - US monetary policy will now begin to tighten, partly because of Mr Trump's promises to reflate the economy through infrastructure spending.

While currencies such as the Mexican peso and the Indian rupee have also been battered, not all countries with weak currencies are burdened by high levels of foreign exchange debt.

But Turkey is particularly exposed. Some investors are concerned about political risk and Mr Erdogan's hostility to high interest rates. The principal worry, however, is the nearly $210bn in foreign currency liabilities owed by the nonbank corporate sector - debt that has expanded rapidly even as the Turkish state has cut back on its borrowing.

Continued weakness in the lira could make servicing those debts harder - particularly if the currency dips below TL3.70 to the dollar. It has already briefly hit TL3.59 but on Tuesday had strengthened to about TL3.50.

"Last week, all people could talk about was the lira," said one Europe-based analyst, who declined to be identified. "If everyone is heading out the door, nobody wants to be the last one."

Turkey has other problems, too - it relies on imported oil, which could further increase its current account deficit.

The Wall Street Journal Online

English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

KKR & Co. closed its first dedicated fund for taking stakes in fast-expanding technology, media and telecommunications companies at a time the sector has become a hot investment target for private equity.

The New York firm said its $711 million KKR Next Generation Technology Growth Fund LP exceeded its original target, which KKR declined to disclose.

The vehicle is expected to make equity investments of less than $100 million in sectors including software, information technology, security, digital media and the Internet of Things a phrase used to describe the addition of sensors, computing and communications to various consumer products.

The firm will make investments from the fund averaging $40 million to $50 million, said David Welsh, a KKR managing director who heads the firm's growth-equity program for technology, media and telecommunications.

Mr. Welsh formerly a partner at Adams Street Partners, where he focused on technology growth-equity investments joined KKR in October. He previously was an executive vice president of corporate development and strategy at antivirus company McAfee Inc.; and a general partner at Partech Ventures, which during his time at the venture firm invested in Five9 Inc., a provider of software for call centers.

KKR's new technology fund comes as the private-equity industry continues to raise ever larger piles of capital to put to work in the "http://www.wsj.com/articles/private-equity-has-a-crush-on-tech-1467308434" both a sign of the promise the space offers and a possible red flag about whether the sector is becoming overheated.

Private-equity funds focused on technology have amassed close to $50 billion so far this year as of Nov. 15, according to data provider Preqin Ltd., putting it close to the six-year peak of $52.2 billion raised in 2015.

KKR's growth-capital deals will be done within a group led by Herald Chen, Richard Sarnoff and Philipp Freise.

KKR contributed to security company Ping Identity Corp.'s growth-equity round this year. The firm also has provided growth capital to companies such as big data business Optimal Plus Ltd., sightseeing booking site GetYourGuide GmbH, data-integration technology provider Jitterbit Inc. and cybersecurity company Darktrace Ltd.

KKR joins a number of large private-equity firms that have formed growth-focused funds.

Technology investor Thoma Bravo said this year it raised $1.07 billion for its stand-alone, lower midmarket software focused fund, Thoma Bravo Discover Fund LP. That vehicle aims to make equity investments of up to $150 million and targets companies with annual revenue of $30 million to $150 million.

Enterprise software-focused Vista Equity Partners is in the midst of fundraising for its sixth flagship fund and recently "http://www.wsj.com/articles/vista-seeks-to-raise-upper-limit-for-tech-buyout-fund-to-10-5-billion-1479474000" by $500 million to $10.5 billion.

The firm also asked investors to raise its Vista Foundation Fund III LP's maximum size to $2.75 billion. That vehicle makes investments in software companies valued between $100 million and $500 million.

Infogroup provides data and software-as-a-service products to help companies increase sales and customer loyalty. The company's clients include large and small companies as well as government, academic and nonprofit entities.

The business includes five units: Infogroup Media Solutions; Infogroup Data Licensing; Infogroup Targeting Solutions; Infogroup Local Marketing Solutions; and Yes Lifecycle Marketing.

Infogroup's customers include large and small companies as well as government, academic and nonprofit entities.
