Tech
When Billionaires Meet: $50 Billion Pledge From SoftBank to Trump; After meeting Donald Trump, SoftBank's Masayoshi Son puts sheds new light on existing investment plans

By Ryan Knutson in New York and Alexander Martin in Tokyo 
865 words
7 December 2016
13:44
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Masayoshi Son, the brash billionaire who controls Sprint Corp., said Tuesday he would invest $50 billion in the U.S. and create 50,000 new jobs, following a 45-minute private meeting with President-elect Donald Trump.

The telecom mogul, who made his fortune in Japan with SoftBank GroupCorp., announced his investment plans in the lobby of Trump Tower, though he didn't provide details. Mr. Trump took credit for the investment, saying his November victory spurred SoftBank's decision.

Mr. Son told reporters he planned to "invest into the new startup companies in the United States." It would be difficult to create 50,000 jobs entirely by investing in startups, which generally employ few workers. Sprint employs about 30,000 people and has cut jobs to combat losses.

In an interview, Mr. Son said the money will be "http://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-group-launches-investment-fund-1476398189" that he began setting up earlier this year with Saudi Arabia's sovereign-wealth fund and other potential partners.

Mr. Son's plan to pour $50 billion is massive compared with the total amount of capital in venture circles. Venture-capital firms had $163 billion available to invest in new deals as of June 2016, according to research firm Preqin.

In addition to startups, Mr. Son also has his sights on acquisitions as large as $30 billion, a person familiar with his thinking said.

In addition to Kansas-based Sprint, which SoftBank acquired in 2013 for $22 billion, the company also led a $1 billion investment round last year in San Francisco-based online lender Social Finance Inc.

When he acquired Sprint, Mr. Son's initial plan was to merge the carrier with German-owned T-Mobile US Inc. to take on market leaders AT&T Inc. and Verizon Communications Inc., but he abandoned the effort after regulators signaled they would reject the plan. Some investors and analysts have said he could make another attempt after Mr. Trump's election and when a new chairman is appointed to the Federal Communications Commission.

Mr. Son planned to tell Mr. Trump about what happened with T-Mobile, and how he had wanted to invest in the U.S. but the regulatory climate was too harsh so he invested outside the U.S. instead, the person familiar with the matter said.

On Tuesday, Mr. Son declined to comment about his current interest in T-Mobile.

The 59-year-old is known as an ambitious investor who bets on tech and telecom ventures. His company has a large stake in China's Alibaba Group and most recently "http://www.wsj.com/articles/softbank-agrees-to-buy-arm-holdings-for-more-than-32-billion-1468808434" ARM Holdings PLC for $32 billion.

Mr. Son has a history of going straight to national leaders to talk business. In September he met South Korean President Park Geun-hye, and said he intends to invest about five trillion won (about $4.5 billion) in the country's technology sector. He also has met Indian Prime Minister Narendra Modi and pledged to spend billions on the nation's tech startups and renewable energy projects.

With the new $100 billion fund dubbed the SoftBank Vision Fund Mr. Son plans to spend heavily in fields including the so-called Internet of Things, artificial intelligence, deep learning and robotics. He has said he wants to become the Warren Buffett of the tech industry.

SoftBank plans to invest at least $25 billion during the next five years in the fund, while Saudi Arabia's Public Investment Fund may contribute an additional $45 billion over the same period as the fund's lead partner. Other investors are still being finalized. Investments are expected to be made over the next five years.

AT&T Inc. Chief Executive Randall Stephenson also spoke positively of the economic benefits of a Trump presidency Tuesday, largely because of lower taxes and less government oversight. He expressed hope that "a more moderate approach to some of these regulations is in the making under a Trump administration."

At a UBS AG conference in New York, he said, "If we achieve any kind of meaningful corporate tax reform I am quite convinced that it is going to change this trajectory in terms of capital investment." He added that the company's business plans for 2017 are incorporating scenarios for economics growth to be higher than expected, compared with recent years where the focus was on underperformance.

"I can't remember the last time I did an upside sensitivity in a business plan, but we are doing an upside sensitivity right now," he said.

Thomas Gryta contributed to this article.

Blackstone Is Taking Its Home Bet Public

By Ryan Dezember 
927 words
7 December 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Jonathan Gray of Blackstone Group LP went on the biggest home-buying spree in history after the U.S. foreclosure crisis, purchasing repossessed properties from courthouse steps and through online auctions.

Four years, $10 billion and roughly 50,000 homes later, he will find out whether his gambit will pay off. Invitation Homes LP, the Dallas-based company Blackstone formed to maintain and rent those homes, has filed confidentially for an initial public offering that could come as soon as January.

Though Blackstone is unlikely to sell much or even any of its stake in an IPO, the stock market debut will test investors' interest in the idea that the rental-home business can be institutionalized, just as apartments, shopping centers and office towers have been.

Blackstone and other investors saw the housing collapse as a rare opportunity to acquire homes for less than it cost to build them. Millions of foreclosures created a market large enough to justify investing in large systems to manage and maintain sprawling portfolios of rental homes.

Now, these new institutional landlords say the move toward rentals further supports their business model. They point to tight lending standards and a generation of renters who are outgrowing apartments but are too burdened by student debt to buy homes.

To generate the revenue growth that shareholders will demand, they must pace rent increases to avoid spooking tenants into becoming home buyers themselves. And now that foreclosure rates have returned to normal levels and prices have rebounded, they could find it difficult to add houses at attractive prices.

They also must convince investors that huge home-rental companies are viable long-term businesses, not just massive portfolios of properties that need to be sold off.

Estimated by investors at about $35 trillion, U.S. homes are one of the world's largest asset classes -- collectively worth more than the $20 trillion U.S. stock market. Since the housing meltdown, big investors have spent at least $32 billion buying and fixing up homes, and they own between 1% and 2% of all U.S. rental homes, analysts estimate.

Invitation Homes won't be the first Wall Street landlord to go public. American Homes 4 Rent, which was started in 2012 by self-storage magnate B. Wayne Hughes with cash from Alaska's state oil fund, made its stock-market debut in 2013. It owns about 48,000 rental houses in 22 states.

Barry Sternlicht's Starwood Waypoint Residential Trust began trading in early 2014. After merging earlier this year with the home portfolio of fellow real-estate mogul Thomas J. Barrack, it changed its name to Colony Starwood Homes and became the country's third-largest landlord.

After a slow start, American Homes and Colony shares took off this year, rising 27% and 33%, respectively, far exceeding the S&P 500's 7.9% gain.

"We're no longer trying to convince the investment world this is a legitimate business," Fred Tuomi, Colony Starwood's chief executive, said in an interview.

Though the chance has passed to acquire thousands of houses at steep discounts, Colony and its competitors are still buying individual homes here and there as well as groups of homes bundled by other investors during the crisis, Mr. Tuomi said. Colony, he said, could manage about 100,000 homes without having to invest much more in the systems it has built.

Economic factors have helped the stocks in the sector. Home prices have risen above their 2006 peaks in much of the country, boosting the value of these firms' holdings. That and rising interest rates have pushed homeownership out of reach for many. Lately, the homeownership rate has hovered around its lowest level in at least 50 years, according to U.S. Census data.

Meanwhile, rental vacancies, including apartments, are at their lowest level in at least a decade at 6.8% in the third quarter. Rents are up, particularly in single-family homes, where rents are growing faster than for apartments, according to Green Street Advisors LLC.

Some of the 14 metropolitan areas where Blackstone's Invitation Homes operates have had among the highest home-price appreciations in recent years. In Phoenix, where Blackstone bought its first house in 2012, prices have risen 57.5% since its home-buying binge began, according to Weiss Analytics.

Blackstone has said it focused on properties in top school districts that were suitable for families. Invitation Homes spent more money buying and fixing up its homes -- about $172,000, on average -- than any of its competitors, Amherst Capital Management estimated. Blackstone executives have said they spent about $25,000 repairing and updating each home.

The housing bet was one that Mr. Gray, who started Blackstone's real-estate business shortly after joining the firm in 1992, didn't have to make to ensure his status on Wall Street. But with a flood of cash from investors who flocked to Blackstone's funds after the financial crisis, the opportunity proved too tempting to pass up.

In interviews last year, Mr. Gray and Blackstone Chief Executive Stephen Schwarzman said tighter lending standards, buyers with wrecked credit and banks loaded with repossessed homes presented an unprecedented investment opportunity.

Blackstone, whose executives declined to comment for this article, went on to spend as much as $150 million a week on houses, aiming to acquire enough in each market to make maintenance efficient enough to be profitable.

Laura Kusisto contributed to this article.

Thriving UK tech start-ups fear US squeeze on talent

Madhumita Murgia, European Technology Correspondent 
989 words
6 December 2016
05:05
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

A few months after Pakistani-born Azmat Yusuf left Google s London office in 2010, he founded "https://citymapper.com/london", an urban transit app now used in 29 cities around the world.

His move from an industry heavyweight to a British technology start-up is one example of a common trend in the sector. The top five past employers of workers in UK tech start-ups today are IBM, HP, Microsoft, Google and Yahoo, according to a new report from Balderton Capital, which surveyed 1,000 venture-backed companies.

 Firms like Google, Facebook and Microsoft are effectively training our local workforce to become entrepreneurs,  says James Wise, partner at Balderton Capital, the European venture capital firm.  Some of the most successful founders, including within our own portfolio, from Citymapper to TransferWise, came out of these large tech companies. 

So, the recent announcements from two of the largest US tech companies that they planned to create hundreds of new jobs in the UK should have been seen as a significant boost to the broader British tech industry.

Last month, Google chief executive Sundar Pichai announced that the group s new UK headquarters would have space for at least 3,000 new employees in London. When Facebook followed with its own announcement the following week, promising to boost its UK headcount by half to 1,500, it increased the confidence displayed by US groups in Britain s technical expertise.

But while many entrepreneurs and investors believe these moves will galvanise the local workforce, others worry the industry   and particularly smaller tech companies and start-ups   could be curtailed by a talent squeeze. Increased competition for skilled engineers, along with harsher immigration controls post-Brexit, are fuelling fears of an industry staffing crisis in the UK.

 There is a global war for talent, and our [UK] companies are absolutely competing for talent. It is a significant concern for us,  says Fred Destin, a venture capitalist at Accel Partners, who sits on the board of companies including Deliveroo.  Anybody who tells you we are not competing for resources is not telling the truth. 

Neither Google nor Facebook explicitly broke down where the UK jobs would be created but both indicated that many of the roles would be high-skilled engineering jobs, drawn from the UK s pool of talented computer scientists.

-----------------

European tech capitals: London stands on top

-----------------

Britain has the highest proportion of science, technology and maths graduates under 30 in Europe. It is also a magnet for coding talent: London is home to 300,345 developers   the most of any European city, according to the Balderton study.

But last year 32 per cent of developers in the UK switched jobs, according to the report.  Salaries have gone up markedly, especially for niche skills like machine learning and data science, because there are high levels of attrition,  Mr Wise says.

The prospect of rising pay packets is expected to be a bigger obstacle for start-ups than large tech groups such as Google and Facebook.

UK start-ups employ 31 per cent of those who work in small tech businesses across Europe, making it by far the continent s largest early-stage tech employer.

 Our companies know they can never compete with world-leading companies on salaries alone,  Mr Wise says.  On that front, the ability of start-ups to continue to attract talent is going to be harder. 

In particular, British tech companies warn that UK start-ups may lose out on graduates, although they could make up the numbers through more experienced hires looking for fresh challenges.

 [Google and Facebook] may increase the competition for talent: they are more likely to have more developed recruitment functions with closer ties to universities, for example, but they also attract more talent to London,  says Jo White, director of communications at London fintech firm TransferWise.  But big companies have a different appeal to start-ups. For many people it is about the experience they are looking for at different stages in their career. 

Given that the UK s tech sector relies heavily on pan-European recruitment   with company founders estimating that between a third and half of UK start-up engineers are foreign   there are fears in some quarters that a shortage of skilled people could hit companies, both large and small.

-----------------

UK tech start-up workers  most common backgrounds

-----------------

Dominic Jacquesson, head of talent at venture firm Index Ventures, who helps to hire across companies including Funding Circle and TransferWise, says he can already see a talent squeeze.  Any form of visa sponsorship required [when the UK leaves the EU] would be a massive blow to UK tech in terms of recruitment. That deep pool would really start to dry up,  he says.

Despite these misgivings, there is a consensus of positivity around the new jobs announcements.

Many believe Google and Facebook will attract more diversity to the sector, from university graduates to experienced managers from professional services, government and academia, resulting in an ever more skilled workforce.

 I think [the job announcements are] insanely good news for the entire ecosystem because technical skills will evolve, as kids think,  these are the jobs I want when I graduate ,  says David Buttress, chief executive of JustEat, an online takeaway platform.  We re investing tens of millions of pounds building engineering talent in Bristol, and this will attract even more people to the industry. 

Others who have invested in innovative British tech for several years say the announcements are a sign of maturity in the UK market.  [Google and Facebook s] expansion is a huge vote of confidence and has nothing to do with Brexit,  says Saul Klein, venture capitalist at Localglobe and a co-founder of European start-up incubator Seedcamp.  The benefits will spill over into the UK tech ecosystem, which has been about 15 years in the making.

UK COMPANIES
Thriving start-ups fear a squeeze on talent; Technology: Recruitment

By Madhumita Murgia, European Technology Correspondent 
836 words
6 December 2016
Financial Times
FTFT

New UK jobs from Facebook and Google boost sector but intensify war for skilled staff

It is a significant concern for us," says Fred Destin, a venture capitalist at Accel Partners, who sits on the board of companies including Deliveroo. "Anybody who tells you we are not competing for resources is not telling the truth."

Google and Facebook did not detail what the new UK jobs were - but both indicated that many would be high-skilled engineering roles, drawn from the UK's pool of computer scientists.

Britain has the highest proportion of science, technology and maths graduates under 30 in Europe. It is also a magnet for coding talent: London is home to more than 300,000 developers - the most of any European city, according to the Balderton study. But last year, 32 per cent of developers in the UK switched jobs, according to the report. "Salaries have gone up markedly, especially for niche skills like machine learning and data science, because there are high levels of attrition," Mr Wise says.

The threat of rising pay packets is expected to be a bigger obstacle for start-ups than large tech groups such as Google and Facebook. UK start-ups employ 31 per cent of those who work in small tech businesses across Europe, making it by far the continent's largest early-stage tech employer. "Our companies know they can never compete with world-leading companies on salaries alone," Mr Wise says. "On that front, the ability of start-ups to continue to attract talent is going to be harder."

In particular, British tech companies warn that UK start-ups may lose out on graduates, although they could make up the numbers through more experienced hires looking for fresh challenges.

"[Google and Facebook] may increase the competition for talent: they are more likely to have more developed recruitment functions with closer ties to universities for example, but they also attract more talent to London," says Jo White, director of communications at the London fintech firm TransferWise. "But big companies have a different appeal to start-ups."

Given that the UK's tech sector relies heavily on pan-European recruitment - with company founders estimating that between a third and half of UK start-up engineers are foreign - there are fears that a shortage of skilled people could hit companies large and small.

Dominic Jacquesson, head of talent at venture firm Index Ventures, says he can already see a talent squeeze. "Any form of visa sponsorship required [when the UK leaves the EU] would be a massive blow to UK tech in terms of recruitment," he says.

Despite these misgivings, there is positivity around the new jobs announcements. Many believe Google and Facebook will attract more diversity, from university graduates to experienced managers from professional services, government and academia, resulting in an increasingly skilled workforce.

"I think [the job announcements are] insanely good news for the entire ecosystem because technical skills will evolve, as kids think, 'these are the jobs I want when I graduate'," says David Buttress , chief executive of JustEat, an online takeaway platform.

Wall Street as Landlord: Blackstone Going Public with a $10 Billion Bet on Foreclosed Homes; Invitation Homes IPO will test investors' interest in the idea that the rental-home business can be institutionalized as apartments were before

By Ryan Dezember 
1,120 words
6 December 2016
10:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Jonathan Gray of Blackstone Group LP went on the biggest homebuying spree in history after the U.S. foreclosure crisis, purchasing repossessed properties from the courthouse steps and through online auctions.

Four years, $10 billion and roughly 50,000 homes later, he will find out "http://www.wsj.com/articles/blackstones-latest-fund-raises-14-5-billion-in-institutional-capital-1427734491"off. Invitation Homes LP, the Dallas-based company Blackstone formed to maintain and rent those homes, has "http://www.wsj.com/articles/blackstone-groups-invitation-homes-files-confidentially-for-an-ipo-1480546946" that could come as soon as January.

Though Blackstone is unlikely to sell much or even any of its stake in an IPO, the stock market debut will test investors' interest in the idea that the rental-home business can be institutionalized as apartments, shopping centers and office towers were before.

Blackstone and others investors believed that the housing collapse presented a rare opportunity to acquire homes for less than it cost to build them. Millions of foreclosures created a market large enough to justify investing in large systems to manage and maintain sprawling portfolios of rental homes.

Now, these new institutional landlords say the move toward rentals further supports their business model. They point to tight lending standards and a generation of renters who are outgrowing apartments but are too burdened by student debt to buy homes.

Historically, mom-and-pop investors and regional players have owned nearly all the single-family homes for rent. Blackstone and other investors that emerged from the foreclosure crisis face unique challenges in joining them, beyond the chore of maintaining thousands of far-flung homes.

To generate the revenue growth that shareholders will demand, they must pace rent hikes to avoid spooking tenants into becoming home buyers themselves. And now that foreclosure rates have returned to normal levels and prices have rebounded, they could find it difficult to add new houses at attractive prices.

They also must convince investors that huge home-rental companies are viable long-term businesses, not just massive portfolios of properties that need to be sold off.

But if Blackstone and its rivals in the rental-home business succeed, they will have created a huge new field in which Wall Street can invest.

At about $35 trillion, U.S. homes are one of the world's largest asset classes collectively worth more than the $20 trillion U.S. stock market. Since the housing meltdown, big investors have spent at least $32 billion buying and fixing up homes, and they own between 1% and 2% of all U.S. rental homes, analysts estimate.

Invitation Homes won't be the first Wall Street landlord to go public.

American Homes 4 Rent, which was launched in 2012 by self-storage magnate B. Wayne Hughes with cash from Alaska's state oil fund, made its stock market debut in 2013. It owns about 48,000 rental houses in 22 states.

Barry Sternlicht's Starwood Waypoint Residential Trust began trading in early 2014. After merging earlier this year with the home portfolio of fellow real estate mogul Thomas J. Barrack, it changed its name to Colony Starwood Homes and became the country's third-largest landlord.

After a slow start, American Homes and Colony shares took off this year, rising 27% and 33%, respectively, far exceeding the S&P 500's 7.9% gain.

"We're no longer trying to convince the investment world this is a legitimate business," Fred Tuomi, Colony Starwood's chief executive, said in an interview. "We're not in this to simply fix and flip. We're in this for the long-term, steady and growing income stream."

Though the chance has passed to acquire thousands of houses at steep discounts, Colony and its competitors are still buying individual homes here and there as well as groups of homes bundled by other investors during the crisis, Mr. Tuomi said. Colony, he said, could manage about 100,000 homes without having to invest much more in the systems it built to manage them.

Economic factors have helped the stocks in the sector. Home prices have risen above their 2006 peaks in much of the country, boosting the value of these firms' holdings. That and rising interest rates have pushed homeownership out of reach for many. Lately, the homeownership rate has hovered around its lowest level in at least 50 years, according to U.S. Census data.

Meanwhile, rental vacancies, including apartments, are at their lowest level in at least a decade at 6.8% in the third quarter. Rents are up, particularly in single-family homes, where rents are growing faster than at apartments, according to Green Street Advisors LLC.

Some of the 14 metropolitan areas where Blackstone's Invitation Homes operates have had among the greatest home price appreciation in recent years. In Phoenix, where Blackstone bought its first house in 2012, prices have risen 57.5% since its homebuying binge began, according to Weiss Analytics. In Las Vegas, prices are up 60.3% since 2012.

Blackstone has said it focused on properties in top school districts that were suitable for families, a strategy shared by rivals who have told investors that tenants in such areas more readily accept rent hikes to avoid moving. Invitation Homes spent more money buying and fixing up its homes about $172,000, on average than any of its competitors, Amherst Capital Management estimated in a recent study. Blackstone executives have said they spent about $25,000 repairing and updating each home.

The housing bet was one Mr. Gray, who started Blackstone's real-estate business shortly after joining the firm in 1992, didn't have to make to ensure his status on Wall Street. But with a flood of cash from investors who flocked to Blackstone's funds after the financial crisis, the opportunity proved too tempting to pass.

In interviews with The Wall Street Journal last year, Mr. Gray and Blackstone Chief Executive Stephen Schwarzman said tighter lending standards, buyers with wrecked credit and banks loaded with repossessed homes presented an unprecedented investment opportunity.

Blackstone, whose executives declined to comment for this article, went on to spend as much as $150 million a week on houses, aiming to acquire enough in each market to make maintenance efficient enough to profit.

Laura Kusisto contributed to this article.

Pro Private Markets
Accompany Raises $20 Million, Led by Ignition Partners; Ex-Googlers want to build a platform that could make it possible for everyone to have a chief of staff

By Cat Zakrzewski 
351 words
6 December 2016
00:51
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Chief Executive Amy Chang said she built Accompany out of "complete desperation."

After being caught in a meeting without access to data that was readily accessible in the news, she began to look for a tool that could save her from future embarrassment. But the former Google Analytics chief couldn't find one.

So she started Accompany, a tool that collects data from the Web, email, calendars and social feeds to provide relevant information about professional contacts. The company's vision is to one day make it possible for every employee to have their own digital chief of staff.

She said chiefs of staff are tasked with making sure people have access to all the information they "need to be armed with" going into big meetings. Accompany syncs with a calendar and then provides helpful biographical details, recent emails or news about a company relevant to specific meetings.

The three-year-old company announced on Monday it has raised $20 million in fresh funding, bringing its total to $40.6 million. The company launched its service in beta to select users in August, and it plans to launch out of beta in the coming months.

On Monday, Ms. Chang announced in London onstage at TechCrunch Disrupt that the beta version would launch. She said Accompany will initially focus on English-speaking markets as different languages pose challenges for its speech recognition technology.

The company has 40 employees and offices in both Los Altos, Calif., and Portland, Oregon. The company will also use the funds to expand its team.

Ms. Chang said the funding from Ignition Partners resulted out of conversations she had with partners at the firm who sit on the board of Splunk with her. She said it was a preemptive round, where the investors approached the company.

"We know we're damn lucky for that," she said.

BlackRock Names BuzzFeed Executive Frank Cooper as Chief Marketing Officer; Company working to bolster business from individual investors

BlackRock Inc. has hired a new global chief marketing officer from digital media company BuzzFeed as the world's largest money manager works to bolster its business from individual investors.

Frank Cooper, who spent the last 18 months as chief marketing officer and chief creative officer at BuzzFeed, plans to join the New York asset manager in January, according to an internal memo seen by The Wall Street Journal.

He will report to Chief Executive Laurence Fink, who sent the internal memo and highlighted Mr. Cooper's experience using "new marketing technologies to help organizations from global consumer companies to technology startups to drive growth." Mr. Cooper is set to lead a marketing team that focuses on research and analytics, digital marketing and branding, the memo said.

At BuzzFeed Mr. Cooper "http://www.wsj.com/articles/buzzfeed-chief-marketing-officer-frank-cooper-to-exit-company-1480355112" and helped create sponsored content. He joined BuzzFeed after more than a decade at PepsiCo Inc. and worked previously at AOL, Warner Bros. and as an entertainment lawyer in Los Angeles.

BlackRock started as an institutional money-management business and has tried in recent years to diversify its business mix and find new ways to sell funds to retail investors. The firm is currently focused on gathering more assets from these retail, or individual investors, in part by using its proprietary Aladdin investment and risk-management technology.

Retail assets represented 12% of assets under management at BlackRock at the end of the third quarter, but 34% of its traditional investment fees. Its iShares exchange-traded fund unit accounted for 26% of assets and 37% of fees with its institutional business representing the balance.

The firm has decentralized some of its reporting lines in recent months with heads of the firm's regional retail businesses reporting to local executives in Europe, Asia and the Americas, rather than one global head.

Mr. Cooper will join BlackRock's Global Executive Committee. He partly succeeds the company's longtime global head of marketing and communications, Linda Robinson, who is moving to a vice chairman role, the firm told employees in April.

Global Head of Corporate Communications Jim Badenhausen, who has also served as interim chief marketing officer for the last two years and previously reported to Ms. Robinson, will now report to Mr. Fink.

Fullerton Health Singapore IPO scuppered after complaints

Jeevan Vasagar in Singapore 
573 words
4 December 2016
09:09
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2016 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

A Singapore-based provider of corporate health services called off its initial public offering following a campaign of anonymous letters complaining about the company s fee structure.

Fullerton Health scrapped itsplanned listing on SGX last monthafter a series of letters to regulators that criticised the fact that its fees are a percentage of medical bills rather than a flat price, and complained of unexpected reductions when doctors were reimbursed for their services.

TheIPO was priced at S$1.52 a share   the bottom end of its marketed range   and would have raised S$213m ($149m).

People familiar with the IPO process say the case highlights the challenge that new entrants to a market can face from established businesses   in this case the letters targeting the healthcare company appear to have been written by doctors.

Like  managed care  companies in the US, Fullerton Health aims to lower corporate healthcare costs through scale, which increases its bargaining power with panels of subcontracted doctors.

The Singapore business also uses data analytics to detect fraudulent healthcare claims. Its clients include BP, Singapore Airlines and Marina Bay Sands.

The claims about unexpected deductions appear to relate to adjudicated claims, in which doctors  bills are adjusted downwards before payment.

The campaign seemed orchestrated, with a fresh letter at the end of every week, one person familiar with the IPO process said, adding that the company and its advisers began referring to 6pm on Friday as  the witching hour .

The  poison pen  campaign has raised questions about SGX s policy of accepting anonymous complaints relating to IPO prospectuses.

Other exchanges such as Hong Kong say that while anonymous complaints will be noted, they may not be acted on if the exchange is unable to contact a correspondent to substantiate a claim.

A banker in Singapore said, however, that the public airing of complaints underlined the disruptive potential of the company. The banker said:  To my mind, causing so much angst suggests some validation of their business model. 

In its statement to the Singapore exchange, Fullerton Health said it had decided against the listing on SGX  in light of current market uncertainty .

It added:  Fullerton Health believes they have satisfactorily answered all questions from [the Monetary Authority of Singapore] and SGX. 

The Fullerton Health spokesman said: There is an ongoing debate regarding percentage fees in our industry, with a range of opinions on the merits of this simple structure.  The company is in consultation with doctors about alternative fee structures, it said.

It added:  We do not expect our business model, the strength of our business plan and our expectations for revenue growth to be affected by how we charge our fees. 

Singapore Medical Association, the doctors  professional body, has publicly criticised  managed care  companies for charging fees that are percentages of medical bills   and may be large sums in the case of inpatient admissions.

The doctors  body says these  excessive  fees for major operations are disproportionate to the administrative work required.

SGX, Southeast Asia s biggest stock market by volume, has been facing a dearth of IPOs.

Tan Boon Gin, chief regulatory officer at SGX, said the exchange worked closely with companies involved in listings to resolve any issues raised after a prospectus is lodged.

 What is important is materiality and proof 	. 	. 	. 	we will not entertain feedback that is clearly frivolous or vexatious,  Mr Tan said.

Verlaine's Take: What Italian Vote Means for Financial Regulation

Interactive: The Obama Administration's Last Regulatory Push

House Passes Bill to Change Criteria for Labeling Banks as SIFIs

China Escalates Efforts to Cool Down Financial System

Treasury Pick Steven Mnuchin Remained Tight With Goldman Circle

What Italian Vote Means for Financial Regulation

BRUSSELS As the U.S. gears up for the possibility of new approaches to financial regulation under the Trump administration, potential changes are also brewing across the Atlantic.

Italians are set to vote Sunday on a constitutional referendum that could alter the country's political course and roil markets, testing the stability of its No. 3 lender, Banca Monte dei Paschi di Siena SpA .

In the case of a "no" vote to overhauls that would limit the power of Italy's Senate, Prime Minister Matteo Renzi said he plans to step down, prompting the country to hold elections as soon as next year to form a new government. That would pave the way for anti-establishment forces such as the 5 Star Movement to gain influence.

The movement, founded in 2009 by Beppe Grillo, has a nearly 30% approval rating among Italian voters, according to a poll conducted in November by Demos & Pi. The group's take on financial regulation deviates from the current norm in Brussels and mainstream parties.

While an Italian "no" vote is unlikely to influence rules crafted in Brussels in the short term, it may well increase the influence of political movements seeking to increase regulation of financial institutions, including breaking them up.

"The 5 Star Movement supports an ambitious reform of the financial sector, based on a stringent regulation of shadow banking and complex financial systems as well as a sort of new European Glass-Steagall Act aimed at separating investment banking activities from the core commercial function," said 5 Star politician Marco Valli, a 31-year-old member of the European Parliament who sits on the economic and monetary affairs committee.

In a joint interview with The Wall Street Journal, Mr. Valli and Marco Zanni, also a 5 Star member of the European Parliament , said a "no" vote in Italy would boost their group's momentum.

In terms of financial regulation, separating retail banking activities from trading and investment banking is at the top of the list for the 5 Star Movement, an idea that finds support among other alternative parties such as the Green Party.

Two prominent Greens on the European Parliament 's economic and monetary affairs committee, Sven Giegold of Germany and Philippe Lamberts of Belgium, have both lamented the death of a bill put forward by the European Commission , the EU's executive arm, known as the "bank structural reform" proposal. The legislation was aimed at to breaking apart the bloc's largest banks.

"The atmosphere is, basically, the system is fixed," said Mr. Lamberts, who spearheaded a successful campaign to limit banker bonuses. "We see that the bank structural reform bill has been killed it has been kept artificially alive, but it is done."

The debate about whether or not postcrisis overhauls in the financial sector have safeguarded Europe from another too-big-to-fail scenario has been at the forefront of discussions as the EU continues to implement legislation written in the wake of 2008.

"The recent postcrisis regulation failed to address the main sources of instability in the financial sector," said the 30 year-old Mr. Zanni. "As a result, the system today appears more fragile than ever and exposed to problems related to systemic risks, high leverage, too-big-to-fail and interconnectedness."

Key Developments in Washington, on Wall Street, and Beyond

Italians to Stress Test EU Financial Regulators With Referendum

By Todd Buell

FRANKFURT Italy's constitutional referendum Sunday looms as a potential stress-test for Europe's new financial-regulation regime.

Following the U.S. market turmoil of 2008 and the ensuing euro crisis, European Union officials expanded oversight of the bloc's finance sector. The alphabet soup of agencies has spent the past several years staffing up, implementing regulations and grilling banks, insurance companies and other industry players on their liquidity and capital health.

The goal has been to prevent financial institutions from collapsing and ensuring taxpayers don't have to foot the bill to save private financiers. Now Italian voters could show if it all works as planned, in the event any post-vote market turmoil shakes the nation's vulnerable banks.

Italians will vote on Dec. 4 in a referendum on constitutional changes proposed by Prime Minister Matteo Renzi the next opportunity for voters in a major economy to give the political establishment another kicking. WSJ's Niki Blasina explains what's at stake. Photo: Getty Images.

Italy's vote on Sunday will decide on a constitutional change designed to facilitate reforms and limit the power of the Senate. Recent surveys favor the "no" camp. Italian Prime Minister Matteo Renzi has said he would quit if the referendum loses, which could lead to political instability. Mr. Renzi's resignation could lead to a caretaker government that would ferry the country to elections as soon as next year. Such a prospect is spooking investors, given the recent surge in Italy's large antiestablishment 5 Star Movement.

Analysts warn that a "no" vote could bring political and financial instability, as market uncertainty and risk-averse investors could make funding conditions tougher for the country's ailing lenders.

That could trigger a showdown between Rome and EU officials over how to apply new rules and result in losses for small Italian investors. Financial pain for ordinary Italians could turn them further against mainstream politicians.

Italian banks have tested the patience of markets and regulators all year with their struggle to recapitalize. Now, a "no" vote would be a real shock to the system, say some economists. It wasn't supposed to play out like this.

The European Central Bank in 2014 became supervisor of the 19-country eurozone's largest banks, with the aim of harmonizing standards. The next year the eurozone imposed a unified system for winding down failing banks, the Single Resolution Board . This year, Europe started implementing new rules on bank rescues, known as the Bank Resolution and Recovery Directive.

The new institutions follow the opening in 2011 of the European Banking Authority , which sets rules for banks across the whole 28-country EU. The EU also in 2011 established a market regulator, the European Securities and Markets Authority , and an insurance regulator, the European Insurance and Occupational Pensions Authority .

Europe's new system was designed to make banks less reliant on governments if they get in trouble and force market discipline into decision-making. The new regime is working so far, "but it has not been violated openly," said Jan Pieter Krahnen, a finance professor at Goethe University in Frankfurt. "I think of Italy as the first country facing this issue."

A "no" vote entails "significant risk that depositors start to pull their money out" of banks, said Jack Allen, an economist at Capital Economics.

No bank faces more risk than Banca Monte dei Paschi di Siena , the world's oldest bank and Italy's third-largest. The only bank to effectively fail European stress tests last summer, it needs to raise 5 billion euros (about $5.3 billion) in new capital by year-end. Political and market instability would significantly complicate the task.

New EU rules would let Rome help the bank only if investors also take a hit, for example by converting subordinate bonds issued by Monte dei Paschi for a lower value of its battered shares to fill the  ¬5 billion hole in its balance sheet. Italian mom-and-pop investors hold  ¬2 billion in such bonds.

Forcing losses on voters is "not going to be very popular," said Mr. Allen.

Italian efforts to protect those small investors by circumventing new EU rules risk sparking a clash between Rome and Brussels. EU officials could then face a choice between enforcing their get-tough standards and worsening an Italian political crisis.

Mr. Allen predicted EU officials would reach a compromise rather than aggravate the situation following a "no" vote.

Other observers said markets might not react extremely if the referendum fails.

"There's an exaggeration of the disruptive nature of the  no' vote," said Nicolas Vron, of Brussels think tank Bruegel .

Even a "yes" vote wouldn't solve the problems in Italy's crowded banking sector. The country has "more bank branches than restaurants," said investor Alberto Gallo, manager of the Algebris Macro Credit Fund. Intense competition among Italian banks has hurt profits that were already weakened by years of recession and weak economic growth.

Analysts said that whatever the outcome of Sunday's vote, Italy will continue to present challenges for EU financial regulators.

 Giovanni Legorano contributed to this article.

House Passes Bill to Change Criteria for Labeling Banks as SIFIs

By Rachel Witkowski

House lawmakers approved a bill Thursday that would change the way federal regulators designate banks as being systemically important and thus subject to tougher oversight.

The bill, sponsored by Rep. Blaine Luetkemeyer (R., Mo.), passed 254-161. It would scrap the current threshold institutions with $50 billion or more in assets and instead let regulators determine whether a firm should be designated as a systemically important financial institution, or SIFI, based on the firm's riskiness and other factors, including its size.

Republicans have argued, among other things, that some regional banks are being unfairly categorized as SIFIs and therefore, come under stricter regulatory standards just like the nation's largest banks.

The legislation irked Democrats, who argued on the House floor Thursday that the bill would unwind some of the restrictions in the Dodd-Frank Act that were meant to keep banks from triggering a repetition of the 2008 financial crisis and subsequent bailouts.

"We enacted Dodd-Frank...in response to the stunning greed and regulatory failures of our financial system, and yet, with this bill, the Republicans are displaying a staggering degree of historical amnesia," said Rep. Maxine Waters (D., Calif.), the ranking member of the House Financial Services Committee . "This bill is the epitome of that dangerous agenda...gutting our banking regulations' oversight."

The bill isn't expected to move forward in the Senate this year, and if it did, President Barack Obama has threatened to veto it. But the heated debate on the House floor foreshadows the fight that is likely to shape up into a battle next year as President-elect Donald Trump enters the White House and Republicans take control of the Senate, giving them leadership of both houses of Congress. Mr. Trump has said he would like to scale back, if not scrap, the Dodd-Frank law.

There is some bipartisan support in both the House and Senate to change the SIFI threshold, though many Democrats disagree with details in the House bill.

On Thursday, 20 Democrats joined 234 Republicans to pass the measure.

"It is clear that this is just the first act in a long, dangerous play that will continue well into next year" under Mr. Trump, Ms. Waters said on the House floor before the vote. "This is the first act in Trump's promise that he's going to deregulate, his promise that he's going to get rid of Dodd-Frank."

The House Financial Services Committee chairman, Rep. Jeb Hensarling (R., Texas), responded by saying, "If the ranking member believes this is the first act in getting rid of Dodd-Frank, she ain't seen nothing yet."

Although the debate was heated, some Democrats in both the House and Senate have said they support the idea of changing the SIFI threshold because they see the $50 billion in assets as an arbitrary number that doesn't fully account for risk. Most Democrats, however, staunchly oppose parts of the House bill, including a provision that would leave the designation of SIFIs solely up to the Financial Stability Oversight Council , a group of top regulators led by the Treasury secretary.

Mr. Trump's choice of Mr. Mnuchin for Treasury secretary gives many Democrats further pause since he serves on the board of CIT Group, a SIFI-designated bank, and would be chairman of the Financial Stability Oversight Council. If the House bill is enacted, the council would then have to re-examine the SIFI labels on dozens of banks.

"Mr. Mnuchin's bank is just one of 27 banks that stand to directly benefit from this legislation," Ms. Waters said. "Even if a potential Treasury Secretary Mnuchin decided to regulate his former employer, by the time he got around to it, the damage would have already been done."

China Escalates Efforts to Cool Down Financial System

By Shen Hong

SHANGHAI China's central bank has stepped up efforts to drain cash from the country's financial system in the past week, as it seeks to curb excessive borrowing and tame frothy markets.

The cost for banks to borrow from each other, measured by the so-called seven-day repo rate, rose to 3.49% this week a 19-month high from 2.92% on Monday. The surge followed the People's Bank of China 's withdrawal of a net 130 billion yuan ($18.85 billion) from China's money markets over the four trading days until Tuesday this week, which has been partially reversed.

At the same time, the PBOC has been guiding major state-owned banks to restrict their short-term lending to other financial institutions, according to market participants.

The scarcity of funds and sharp rise in borrowing costs has contributed to a selloff in China's domestic bond markets, which had thrived in recent months on an abundance of cheap funds available to investors. The yield on 10-year Chinese government bonds rose as high as 3.00% on Friday, its highest point since mid-June. Bond yields rise as their prices fall.

Analysts say the central bank could also be seeking to relieve the recent downward pressure on the yuan by raising domestic interest rates.

"It's all part of the government's broader deleveraging campaign that seeks to put China's high debt level under control. The message is clear: the days of easy money are over," said Suan Teck Kin, an economist at United Overseas Bank in Singapore.

Market participants say the PBOC's move to reduce the amount of cash in China's financial system was a surprise, in part, due to its timing.

"It was totally unexpected because we are talking about the month end, a period when banks need lots of cash to meet all kinds of capital requirements and so the PBOC would typically provide more funds to quench the thirst," a Shanghai-based head of money market trading at an Asian bank said.

The overnight repo, the most popular tool for speculators to access borrowed funds, has also surged to a 19-month-high of 2.60% from 2.36% on Monday.

Short-term loans, in the form of overnight and seven-day reverse repos, are typically used by financial institutions to meet their daily cash needs. However, in recent years, Chinese banks have channeled some of that money to investors, who have been able borrow it at cheap rates to plow into assets such as bonds.

The PBOC made a similar attempt in September to crack down on speculative investment behavior by artificially raising interbank interest rates via tighter supply of short-dated funds. However, the campaign lasted for just a few days, after signs of panic in markets forced the central bank to soothe investors with the resumption of large cash injections.

"They to want to manage expectations so that people will accept the new normal of more expensive funding costs but they don't want to let rates rise too fast either," said Mr. Suan.

PBOC officials couldn't immediately be reached for comment.

The current tight funding conditions in China could last until the Lunar New Year in late January, analysts say, if the central bank takes no further action to inject more cash into the financial system.

"If the PBOC just relies on its routine money market operations to inject more liquidity, it will only solve the difficulty of funding access but not the problem of high funding costs," said Liu Dongliang, a senior analyst at China Merchants Bank .

Treasury Pick Steven Mnuchin Remained Tight With Goldman Circle

By Anupreeta Das

Steven Mnuchin left Goldman Sachs Group Inc . 15 years ago, but Donald Trump's Treasury pick was born into the Goldman orbit and remains there long after he quit.

Mr. Mnuchin joined Goldman in 1985, following in his father's footsteps. The elder Mr. Mnuchin, Robert, had started in 1957 and spent his entire Wall Street career at Goldman, where he was revered as one of the people who pioneered block trading, the buying and selling of a large number of shares at once. Steven's brother Alan also worked at Goldman and now runs his own fund.

While at Goldman, Mr. Mnuchin worked in the fixed income department where he oversaw trading in mortgages. municipal bonds and other securities. Having made partner in 1994, Mr. Mnuchin later grew more interested in technology and was the bank's chief information officer before leaving in 2002.

By then, he had made a fortune from Goldman's 1999 IPO. Bigger riches awaited him, including from deals that came about through personal relationships and connections Mr. Mnuchin made at the firm. After leaving Goldman, Mr. Mnuchin spent a year working for the hedge fund of his Yale University roommate Edward Lampert, who too had worked at the bank once.

In 2003, Mr. Mnuchin was hired to run a new credit fund set up by the billionaire George Soros. Mark Schwartz, a Goldman veteran who was chief executive of Soros Fund Management at the time, oversaw Mr. Mnuchin's hire as well as the spin-off, a year later, of the credit fund.

In 2004, Mr. Mnuchin thus became a co-founder, with former Goldman colleagues Daniel Neidich and Chip Seelig, of Dune Capital Management LP . With backing from Mr. Soros, the fund initially focused on real estate lending and properties. Mr. Neidich had run Goldman's real-estate focused Whitehall Funds.

Mr. Mnuchin had met Mr. Trump by then and were social acquaintances. In the mid-2000s, the two did business together when Dune Capital joined private-equity firm Fortress Investment Group in making a $130 million mezzanine loan to fund the construction of Trump International Hotel & Tower in Chicago. Fortress co-founder Pete Briger happened to be a Goldman alum as well, having left the bank around the same time as Mr. Mnuchin.

In the mid-2000s, Messrs. Mnuchin and Seelig took Dune into the entertainment business, striking up a film-financing deal with Fox for over $500 million. Mr. Seelig struck out on his own in 2012, and Mr. Mnuchin has since found other partners for his film-financing activities.

Between 2008 and 2015, however, Mr. Mnuchin spent most of his time shepherding a turnaround at OneWest Bank . In the depths of the financial crisis, he had spotted a buying opportunity as lender IndyMac Bank failed and was taken over by regulators. There too, Mr. Mnuchin's old Goldman contacts came in handy.

IndyMac, based in Pasadena, Calif., collapsed in the summer of 2008 as customers grew concerned about its souring mortgages and withdrew deposits. Just months later, Mr. Mnuchin had corralled a group of seven investors, including several who had previously worked at Goldman, to buy IndyMac from the government for about $1.5 billion.

The group included funds run by some boldfaced Wall Street names including George Soros, Mr. Mnuchin's former employer; hedge-fund manager John Paulson; J. Christopher Flowers, a former Goldman mergers banker; and MSD Capital, the private investment firm of Michael Dell. In addition, Mr. Mnuchin brought in Silar Advisors LP, a fund run by former Goldman employee Robert Leeds and Stone Point Capital, another fund started by ex-Goldman executives.

In 2014, IndyMac, renamed OneWest, was sold to CIT Group Inc ., a larger bank that was run by John Thain, yet another former Goldman executive. Mr. Thain, the former CEO of Merrill Lynch & Co. was president of Goldman when Mr. Mnuchin was chief information officer. In fact, Goldman advised Mr. Mnuchin's side on the deal, which netted the sellers a more than $3 billion gain.

Soon after he became Mr. Trump's finance chair, Mr. Mnuchin negotiated a joint fundraising agreement with the Republican National Committee , easing the way for major Republican donors to give both to the party and to Mr. Trump. The committee's finance chairman, Lewis Eisenberg, is a Goldman veteran and contemporary of Mr. Mnuchin's father, who now runs an art gallery.

WSJ Pro: Sen. Brown Hopes Sen. Crapo Helps to Set Fannie and Freddie Free

By Rachel Witkowski

WASHINGTON Sen. Sherrod Brown, the top Democrat on the Senate Banking Committee, said Thursday he is hopeful that major changes in housing could move forward under Sen. Mike Crapo (R., Idaho), the committee's incoming chairman.

"It will be a better day with Sen. Crapo," Mr. Brown said at a conference here for affordable housing lenders.

Taking a jab at the current committee chairman, Sen. Richard Shelby (R., Ala.), he added, "I say it's a better day because he actually realizes the name of our committee isn't  banking committee.'" The full name is the Senate Committee and Banking, Housing and Urban Affairs.

Mr. Brown said he's hopeful that under Mr. Crapo, who is seen as more amenable to bipartisan deals than is Mr. Shelby, a path could be cleared for an agreement on housing overhaul legislation that would take Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac out of conservatorship. Fannie and Freddie have been in conservatorship since 2008, when the housing market nose-dived and the federal government stepped in to rescue the two mortgage giants, which buy mortgage loans and package them into securities for sale to investors.

"We've not done one hearing on housing in the two years that Sen. Shelby's been chairman," Mr. Brown said. "Sen. Crapo is an honorable guy, way more conservative than I am. That's fine. The point is that he will work with us. I am optimistic that we can do things together."

The comments on housing legislation come a day after President-elect Donald Trump's choice for Treasury secretary, Steven Mnuchin, said in an interview on Fox Business Network that the new administration would release Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac from government control "reasonably fast."

Mr. Brown said he is still not sure what steps Mr. Trump will take.

"Yesterday, the secretary-designee of the Treasury said some things about conservatorship, so I don't know if that's becoming a policy position or where they're going to go," Mr. Brown said. "I'm very open to figuring out how we do this."

WSJ Pro: Joe Biden to Make Last-Ditch Defense of Financial Regulations

By Gabriel T. Rubin

With the Obama administration's tougher financial regulations at risk of being rolled back by President-elect Donald Trump and congressional Republicans, Vice President Joe Biden plans to give a robust defense of those rules in a speech Monday.

Mr. Biden will appear with former Federal Reserve Chairman Paul Volcker at Georgetown University on Monday, where Mr. Biden will deliver remarks "on sound financial sector regulation," according to a university statement.

Mr. Biden's speech comes days after Mr. Trump announced his Treasury secretary nominee will be Steven Mnuchin , a former Goldman Sachs Group Inc . executive who has spoken critically of the Obama administration's signature financial regulation overhaul, the Dodd-Frank Act. The 2010 law was intended to strengthen the financial system in the wake of the 2008 financial crisis, but many Republicans and some Democrats have said it went too far.

The White House didn't immediately respond to requests for comment.

In an interview with CNBC after he was announced as Mr. Trump's Treasury nominee, Mr. Mnuchin said he wanted to "strip back" Dodd-Frank and criticized it for being "too complicated" and for having adverse effects on lending. Mr. Trump also sharply criticized the law on the campaign trail, though at times he also called for more financial regulation, like bringing back the Glass-Steagall Act, which separated commercial and investment banking.

He also criticized the Volcker rule, which restricts banks from making some kinds of speculative investments and was the brainchild of Mr. Volcker. "The No. 1 problem with the Volcker rule is it's way too complicated and people don't know how to interpret it," Mr. Mnuchin said. "So we're going to look at what do with it, as we are with all of Dodd-Frank."

Mr. Biden's speech comes as the Obama administration races to finish some of its outstanding financial regulations before the president's term expires next month. Republicans in Congress, however, have warned the administration against pushing through "midnight regulations," and many will likely be at risk once Mr. Trump has been inaugurated.

Top Bank Regulator Hints at Potential Fintech Charter

By Rachel Witkowski

WASHINGTON The comptroller of the currency gave some insight Thursday into his agency's consideration of whether to issue a groundbreaking federal charter for fintech firms.

The Office of the Comptroller of the Currency , which regulates national banks, is publishing a paper on the issue on Friday.

Comptroller Thomas Curry said if the regulator decides to propose a new chartering system for fintech firms in Friday's paper, it could be in the form of a "limited-purpose" charter under which the agency can set "enforceable" conditions based on each firm's business model and risk.

"Let's assume, for argument's sake, you can charter a fintech firm under the limited-purpose authority. I think it's important to be clear about what we expect," Mr. Curry said in an interview after an affordable-housing conference.

"That's really what we're trying to do with &the paper that's released tomorrow," he added. "And then how we apply the principles in that paper to individual applications by firms with what we expect to be very unique business models."

The OCC has the authority to apply special conditions to both a traditional bank charter and a limited-purpose charter. Those conditions can include requirements that the firm's management structure or a change in business plan be approved by the regulator.

A major debate over the fintech charter involves whether the OCC will require nonbank fintech firms to meet the same community-investment standards as banks.

Under the Community Reinvestment Act, banks are graded based on their investments back into developing the communities they lend to. Consumer groups have strongly advocated that the OCC apply the same standards to nonbank fintech firms seeking a federal charter.

But some fintech firms that handle payment transfers, for example, rather than online lending, say the CRA isn't applicable to their business.

Mr. Curry, who was speaking more broadly about the CRA at Thursday's event, said that was an issue the agency has been working on within the paper coming Friday.

"Our own mission statement talks about financial inclusion and fair access. I think that goes part and parcel with the national bank charter," Mr. Curry said during the conference.

Mr. Curry plans to release the paper during a speech at Georgetown University Law School in Washington.
